‚ÄúSomebody’s broken the lock and gone inside in a hurry. The drawer’s been shoved home anyhow and left projecting a bit. It caught my eye when I came in.‚Äù

He pulled the drawer open as he spoke, and Dr. Ringwood moved across and looked down into it over the Chief Constable’s shoulder. A number of jewel-boxes lay in one corner, and Sir Clinton turned his attention to these in the first place. He opened them, one after another, and found the contents of most of them in place. One or two rings, and a couple of small articles seemed to be missing.

‚ÄúQuite likely these are things she’s wearing to-night,‚Äù he explained, replacing the leather cases in the drawer as he spoke. ‚ÄúWe’ll try again.‚Äù

The next thing which came to his hand was a packet of photographs of various people. Among them was one of young Hassendean, but it seemed to have no special value for Mrs. Silverdale, since it had been carelessly thrust in among the rest of the packet.

‚ÄúNothing particularly helpful there, it seems,‚Äù was Sir Clinton’s opinion.

He turned next to several old dance-programmes which had been preserved with some care. Lifting them in turn and holding them so that the doctor could see them, the Chief Constable glanced at the scribbled names of the various partners.

‚ÄúOne gentleman seems to have been modest, anyhow,‚Äù he pointed out. ‚ÄúNo initials, even‚Äîjust an asterisk on the line.‚Äù

He flipped the programmes over rapidly.

‚ÄúMr. Asterisk seems to be a favourite, doctor. He occurs pretty often at each dance.‚Äù

‚ÄúHer dancing-partner, probably,‚Äù Dr. Ringwood surmised. ‚ÄúYoung Hassendean, most likely, I should think.‚Äù

Sir Clinton put down the programmes and searched again in the drawer. His hand fell on a battered notebook.

‚ÄúPart of a diary she seems to have kept while she was in a convent. . . . H’m! Just a school-girl’s production,‚Äù he turned over a few pages, reading as he went, ‚Äúand not altogether a nice school-girl,‚Äù he concluded, after he had paused at one entry. ‚ÄúThere’s nothing to be got out of that just now. I suppose it may be useful later on, in certain circumstances.‚Äù

He laid the little book down again and turned once more to the drawer.

‚ÄúThat seems to be the lot. One thing’s pretty clear. The person who broke that lock wasn’t a common burglar, for he’d have pouched the trinkets. The bother is that we ought to find out what this search was for; and since the thing has probably been removed, it leaves one with a fairly wide field for guessing. Let’s have another look round.‚Äù

Suddenly he bent forward and picked up a tiny object from the bottom of the drawer. As he lifted it, Dr. Ringwood could see that it was a scrap of paper; and when it was turned over he recognised it as a fragment torn from the corner of an envelope with part of the stamp still adhering to it.

‚ÄúH’m! Suggestive rather than conclusive,‚Äù was Sir Clinton’s verdict. ‚ÄúMy first guess would be that this has been torn off a roughly-opened letter. So there must have been letters in this drawer at one time or another. But whether our murderous friend was after a packet of letters or not, one can’t say definitely.‚Äù

He stood up and moved under the electric light in order to examine the fragment closely.

‚ÄúIt’s got the local post-mark on it. I can see the VEN. The date’s 1925, but the month part has been torn.‚Äù

He showed the scrap to Dr. Ringwood and then placed it carefully in his note-case.

‚ÄúI hate jumping to conclusions, doctor; but it certainly does look as if someone had broken in here to get hold of letters. And they must have been pretty important letters if it was worth while to go the length of casual murder to secure them.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood nodded.

‚ÄúHe must have been a pretty hard case to murder a defenceless woman.‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s face showed a faint trace of a smile.

‚ÄúThere are two sexes, doctor.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat do you mean? . . . Oh, of course. I said ‚Äòhe must,‚Äô and you think it might have been a woman?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don’t think so; but I hate to prejudge the case, you know. All that one can really say is that someone came here and killed that unfortunate woman. The rest’s simply conjecture and may be right or wrong. It’s easy enough to make up a story to fit the facts.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood walked across to the nearest chair and sat down.

‚ÄúMy brain’s too fagged to produce anything of the sort, I’m afraid,‚Äù he admitted, ‚Äúbut I’d like to hear anything that would explain the damned business.‚Äù

Sir Clinton closed the drawer gently and turned round to face the doctor.

‚ÄúOh, it’s easy enough,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúwhether it’s the true solution or not’s quite another question. You came here about twenty past ten, were let in by the maid, saw your patient, listened to what the maid had to tell you‚Äîlucky for us you took that precaution or we’d have missed all that evidence, since she can’t tell us now‚Äîand left this house at twenty-five to eleven. We came back again, just an hour later. The business was done in between those times, obviously.‚Äù

‚ÄúNot much theory there,‚Äù the doctor pointed out.

‚ÄúI’m simply trying it over in my mind,‚Äù Sir Clinton explained, ‚Äúand it’s just as well to have the time-limits clear to start with. Now we go on. Some time after you had got clear away from here, the murderer comes along. Let’s call that person X, just to avoid all prejudice about age or sex. Now X has thought out this murder beforehand, but not very long beforehand.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow do you make that out?‚Äù Dr. Ringwood demanded.

‚ÄúBecause the two bits of wood which form the handles of the tourniquet are simply pieces cut off a tree, and freshly cut, by the look of the ends. X must have had possession of these before coming into the house‚Äîhence premeditation. But if it had been a case of long premeditation, X would have had something better in the way of handles. I certainly wouldn’t have risked landing on a convenient branch at the last moment if I’d been doing the job myself; and X, I may say, strikes me as a remarkably cool, competent person, as you’ll see.‚Äù

‚ÄúGo on,‚Äù the doctor said, making no attempt to conceal his interest.

‚ÄúOur friend X probably had the cord in his or her pocket and had constructed the rough tourniquet while coming along the road. Our friend X was wearing gloves, I may say.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow do you know that?‚Äù Ringwood asked.

‚ÄúYou’ll see later. Now X went up to the front door and rang the bell. The maid came along, recognised X. . . .‚Äù

‚ÄúHow do you know that?‚Äù Ringwood repeated.

‚ÄúI don’t know it. I‚Äôm just giving you the hypothesis you asked for. I don’t say it’s correct. To continue: this person X inquired if Silverdale (or Mrs. Silverdale, perhaps) was at home. Naturally the maid said no. Most likely she told X that her companion had scarlatina. Then X decided to leave a note, and was invited into the house to write it. It was a long note, apparently; and the maid was told to go to the kitchen and wait till X had finished. So off she went.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell?‚Äù

‚ÄúX had no intention of putting pen to paper, of course. As soon as the maid was out of the way, X slipped upstairs and switched on the light in this room.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’d forgotten it was the light in this window that we saw from the outside,‚Äù Dr. Ringwood interrupted. ‚ÄúGo on.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen, very quietly, by shifting the table on the landing under the electric light, X removed the bulb that lighted the stair. One can reach it by standing on that table. Then X shifted the table back to its place. There were no finger-prints on the bulb‚Äîergo, X must have been wearing gloves, as I told you.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou seem to have got a lot of details,‚Äù the doctor admitted. ‚ÄúBut why all this man≈ìuvring?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’ll see immediately. I think I said already that whoever did the business was a very cool and competent person. When all was ready, X attracted the maid’s attention in some way. She came to the foot of the stairs, suspecting nothing, but probably wondering what X was doing, wandering about the house. It’s quite likely that X made the sick girl upstairs the pretext for calling and wandering out of bounds. Anyhow, the maid came to the foot of the stairs and moved the switch of the landing light. Nothing happened, of course, since the bulb had been removed. She tried the switch backwards and forwards once or twice most likely, and then she would conclude that the lamp was broken or the fuse gone. Probably she saw the reflection of the light from the room-door. In any case, she came quite unsuspiciously up the stair.‚Äù

Sir Clinton paused, as though to allow the doctor to raise objections; but none came, so he continued:

‚ÄúMeanwhile X had taken up a position opposite the door of the room, at the foot of the second flight of stairs. If you remember, a person crouching there in semi-darkness would be concealed from anyone mounting the first flight. The tourniquet was ready, of course.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood shuddered slightly. Apparently he found Sir Clinton’s picture a vivid one, in spite of the casual tone in which it had been drawn.

‚ÄúThe girl came up, quite unsuspicious,‚Äù Sir Clinton continued. ‚ÄúShe knew X; it wasn’t a question of a street-loafer or anything of that sort. An attack would be the last thing to cross her mind. And then, in an instant, the attack fell. Probably she turned to go into the lighted room, thinking that X was there; and then the noose would be round her neck, a knee would be in her back and . . .‚Äù

With a grim movement, Sir Clinton completed his narrative of the murder more effectively than words could have done.

‚ÄúThat left X a clear field. The girl upstairs was light-headed and couldn’t serve as a witness. X daren’t go near her for fear of catching scarlatina‚Äîand that would have been a fatal business, for naturally we shall keep our eye on all fresh scarlet cases for the next week or so. It’s on the cards that her scarlatina has saved her life.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood’s face showed his appreciation of this point.

‚ÄúAnd then?‚Äù he pressed Sir Clinton.

‚ÄúThe rest’s obvious. X came in here, hunting for something which we haven’t identified. Whatever it was, it was in this drawer and X knew where it was. Nothing else has been disturbed except slightly‚Äîpossibly in a hunt for the key of the drawer in case it had been left lying around loose. Not finding the key, X broke open the drawer and then we evidently arrived. That must have been a nasty moment up here. I don’t envy friend X’s sensations when we rang the front door bell. But a cool head pulls one through difficulties of that sort. While we were standing unsuspiciously on the front door steps, X slipped down stairs, out of the back door, and into the safety of the fog-screen.‚Äù

The Chief Constable rose to his feet as he concluded.

‚ÄúThen that’s what happened, you think?‚Äù Doctor Ringwood asked.

‚ÄúThat’s what may have happened,‚Äù Sir Clinton replied cautiously. ‚ÄúSome parts of it certainly are correct, since there’s sound evidence to support them. The rest’s no more than guess-work. Now I must go to the ‘phone.‚Äù

As the Chief Constable left the room, the sick girl upstairs whimpered faintly, and Dr. Ringwood got out of his chair with a yawn which he could not suppress. He paused on the threshold and looked out across the body to the spot at the turn of the stair. Sir Clinton’s word-picture of the murderer crouching there in ambush with his tourniquet had been a little too vivid for the doctor’s imagination.

Chapter V

The Bungalow Tragedy

In the course of his career, Sir Clinton Driffield had found it important to devote some attention to his outward appearance; but his object in doing so had been different from that of most men, for he aimed at making himself as inconspicuous as possible. To look well-dressed, but not too smart; to seem intelligent without betraying his special acuteness; to be able to meet people without arousing any speculations about himself in their minds; above all, to eliminate the slightest suggestion of officialism from his manner: these had been the objects of no little study on his part. In the days when he had held junior posts, this protective mimicry of the average man had served his purposes excellently, and he still cultivated it even though its main purpose had gone.

Seated at his office desk, with its wire baskets holding packets of neatly-docketed papers, he would have passed as a junior director in some big business firm. Only a certain tiredness about his eyes hinted at the sleepless night he had spent at Heatherfield and Ivy Lodge, and when he began to open his letters, even this symptom seemed to fade out.

As he picked up the envelopes before him, his eye was caught by the brown cover of a telegram, and he opened it first. He glanced over the wording and his eyebrows lifted slightly. Then, putting down the document, he picked up his desk-telephone and spoke to one of his subordinates.

‚ÄúHas Inspector Flamborough come in?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. He’s here just now.‚Äù

‚ÄúSend him along to me, please.‚Äù

Replacing the telephone on its bracket, Sir Clinton picked up the telegram once more and seemed to reconsider its wording. He looked up as someone knocked on the door and entered the room.

‚ÄúMorning, Inspector. You’re looking a bit tired. I suppose you’ve fixed up all last night’s business?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. Both bodies are in the mortuary; the doctor’s been warned about the P.M.‚Äôs; the coroner’s been informed about the inquests. And I’ve got young Hassendean’s papers all collected. I haven’t had time to do more than glance through them yet, sir.‚Äù

Sir Clinton gave a nod of approval and flipped the telegram across his desk.

‚ÄúSit down and have a look at that, Inspector. You can add it to your collection.‚Äù

Flamborough secured the slip of paper and glanced over it as he pulled a chair towards the desk.

‚Äú‚ÄòChief Constable, Westerhaven. Try hassendean bungalow lizardbridge road justice.‚Äô H’m! Handed in at the G.P.O. at 8.5 a.m. this morning. Seems to err a bit on the side of conciseness. He could have had three more words for his bob, and they wouldn’t have come amiss. Who sent it, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúA member of the Order of the Helpful Hand, perhaps. I found it on my desk when I came in a few minutes ago. Now you know as much about it as I do, Inspector.‚Äù

‚ÄúOne of these amateur sleuths, you think, sir?‚Äù asked the Inspector, and the sub-acid tinge in his tone betrayed his opinion of uninvited assistants. ‚ÄúI had about my fill of that lot when we were handling that Laxfield affair last year.‚Äù

He paused for a moment, and then continued:

‚ÄúHe’s been pretty sharp with his help. It’s handed in at 8.5 a.m. and the only thing published about the affair is a stop-press note shoved into the Herald. I bought a copy as I came along the road. Candidly, sir, it looks to me like a leg-pull.‚Äù

He glanced over the telegram disparagingly.

‚ÄúWhat does he mean by ‚ÄòLizardbridge road justice‚Äô? There’s no J.P. living on the Lizardbridge Road; and even if there were, the thing doesn’t make sense to me.‚Äù

‚ÄúI think ‚Äòjustice‚Äô is the signature, Inspector‚Äîwhat one might term his nom-de-kid, if one leaned towards slang, which of course you never do.‚Äù

The Inspector grinned. His unofficial language differed considerably from his official vocabulary, and Sir Clinton knew it.

‚ÄúJustice? I like that!‚Äù Flamborough ejaculated contemptuously, as he put the telegram down on the desk.

‚ÄúIt looks rather as though he wanted somebody’s blood,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered carelessly. ‚ÄúBut all the same, Inspector, we can’t afford to put it into the waste-paper basket. We’re very short of anything you could call a real clue in both these cases last night, remember. It won’t do to neglect this, even if it does turn out to be a mare’s nest.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough shrugged his shoulders almost imperceptibly, as though to indicate that the decision was none of his.

‚ÄúI’ll send a man down to the G.P.O. to make inquiries at once, sir, if you think it necessary. At that time in the morning there can’t have been many wires handed in and we ought to be able to get some description of the sender.‚Äù

‚ÄúPossibly,‚Äù was as far as Sir Clinton seemed inclined to go. ‚ÄúSend off your man, Inspector. And while he’s away, please find out something about this Hassendean Bungalow, as our friend calls it. It’s bound to be known to the Post Office people, and you’d better get on the local P.O. which sends out letters to it. The man who delivers the post there will be able to tell you something about it. Get the ‘phone to work at once. If it’s a hoax, we may as well know that at the earliest moment.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery well, sir,‚Äù said the Inspector, recognising that it was useless to convert Sir Clinton to his own view.

He picked up the telegram, put it in his pocket, and left the room.

When the Inspector had gone, Sir Clinton ran rapidly through his letters, and then turned to the documents in the wire baskets. He had the knack of working his mind by compartments when he chose, and it was not until Flamborough returned with his report that the Chief Constable gave any further thought to the Hassendean case. He knew that the Inspector could be trusted to get the last tittle of useful information when he had been ordered to do so.

‚ÄúThe Hassendeans have a bungalow on the Lizardbridge Road, sir,‚Äù Flamborough confessed when he came back once more. ‚ÄúI got the local postman to the ‘phone and he gave me as much as one could expect. Old Hassendean built the thing as a spec., hoping to get a good price for it. Ran it up just after the war. But it cost too much, and he’s been left with it on his hands. It’s just off the road, on the hill about half-way between here and the new place they’ve been building lately, that farm affair.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, there?‚Äù Sir Clinton answered. ‚ÄúI think I know the place. I’ve driven past it often: a brown-tiled roof and a lot of wood on the front of the house.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s it, sir. The postman described it to me.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnything more about it?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s empty most of the year, sir. The Hassendeans use it as a kind of summer place‚Äîshift up there in the late spring, usually, the postman said. It overlooks the sea and stands high, you remember. Plenty of fresh air. But it’s shut up just now, sir. They came back to town over two months ago‚Äîmiddle of September or thereabouts.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed to wake up suddenly.

‚ÄúThat fails to stir you, Inspector? Strange! Now it interests me devilishly, I can assure you. We’ll run up there now in my car.‚Äù

The Inspector was obviously disconcerted by this sudden desire for travel.

‚ÄúIt’s hardly worth your while to go all that way, sir,‚Äù he protested. ‚ÄúI can easily go out myself if you think it necessary.‚Äù

Sir Clinton signed a couple of documents before replying. Then he rose from his chair.

‚ÄúI don’t mind saying, Inspector, that two murders within three hours is too high an average for my taste when they happen in my district. It’s a case of all hands to the pumps, now, until we manage to get on the track. I’m not taking the thing out of your hands. It’s simply going on the basis that two heads are better than one. We’ve got to get to the bottom of the business as quick as we can.‚Äù

‚ÄúI quite understand, sir,‚Äù Flamborough acknowledged without pique. ‚ÄúThere’s no grudge in the matter. I’m only afraid that this business is a practical joke and you’ll be wasting your time.‚Äù

Sir Clinton dissented from the last statement with a movement of his hand.

‚ÄúBy the way,‚Äù he added, ‚Äúwe ought to take a doctor with us. If there’s anything in the thing at all, I’ve a feeling that Mr. Justice hasn’t disturbed us for a trifle. Let’s see. Dr. Steel will have his hands full with things just now; we’ll need to get someone else. That Ringwood man has his wits about him, from what I saw of him. Ring him up, Inspector, and ask him if he can spare the time. Tell him what it’s about, and if he’s the sportsman I take him for, he’ll come if he can manage it. Tell him we’ll call for him in ten minutes and bring him home again as quick as we can. And get them to bring my car round now.‚Äù

Twenty minutes later, as they passed up an avenue, Sir Clinton turned to Dr. Ringwood:

‚ÄúRecognise it, doctor?‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood shook his head.

‚ÄúNever seen it before to my knowledge.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou were here last night, though. Look, there’s Ivy Lodge.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo I see by the name on the gate-post. But remember it’s the first time I’ve seen the house itself. The fog hid everything last night.‚Äù

Sir Clinton swung the car to the left at the end of the avenue.

‚ÄúWe shan’t be long now. It’s a straight road out from here to the place we’re bound for.‚Äù

As they reached the outskirts of Westerhaven, Sir Clinton increased his speed, and in a very short time Dr. Ringwood found himself approaching a long low bungalow which faced the sea-view at a little distance from the road. It had been built in the shelter of a plantation, the trees of which dominated it on one side; and the garden was dotted with clumps of quick-growing shrubs which helped to give it the appearance of maturity.

Inspector Flamborough stepped down from the back seat of the car as Sir Clinton drew up.

‚ÄúThe gate’s not locked,‚Äù he reported, as he went up to it. ‚ÄúJust wait a moment, sir, while I have a look at the surface of the drive.‚Äù

He walked a short distance towards the house, with his eyes on the ground; then he returned and swung the leaves of the gate open for the car to pass.

‚ÄúYou can drive in, sir,‚Äù he reported. ‚ÄúThe ground was hard last night, you remember; and there isn’t a sign of anything in the way of footmarks or wheel-prints to be seen there.‚Äù

As the car passed him, he swung himself aboard again; and Sir Clinton drove up to near the house.

‚ÄúWe’ll get down here, I think, and walk the rest,‚Äù he proposed, switching off his engine. ‚ÄúLet’s see. Curtains all drawn. . . . Hullo! One of the small panes of glass on that front window has been smashed, just at the lever catch. You owe an apology to Mr. Justice, Inspector, I think. He’s not brought us here to an absolute mare’s nest, at any rate. There’s been housebreaking going on.‚Äù

Followed by the others, he walked over to the damaged window and examined it carefully.

‚ÄúNo foot-prints or anything of that sort to be seen,‚Äù he pointed out, glancing at the window-sill. ‚ÄúThe window’s been shut, apparently, after the housebreaker got in‚Äîif he did get in at all. That would be an obvious precaution, in case the open window caught someone’s eye.‚Äù

He transferred his attention to the casement itself. It was a steel-framed one, some four feet high by twenty inches wide, which formed part of a set of three which together made up the complete window. Steel bars divided it into eight small panes.

‚ÄúThe Burglar’s Delight!‚Äù Sir Clinton described it scornfully. ‚ÄúYou knock in one pane, just like this; then you put your hand through; turn the lever-fastener; swing the casement back on its hinges‚Äîand walk inside. There isn’t even the trouble of hoisting a sash as you have to do with the old-fashioned window. Two seconds would see you inside the house, with only this affair to tackle.‚Äù

He glanced doubtfully at the lever handle behind the broken glass.

‚ÄúThere might be finger-prints on that,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI don’t want to touch it. Just go round to the front door, Inspector, and see if it’s open by any chance. If not, we’ll smash the glass at the other end of this window and use the second casement to get in by, so as not to confuse things.‚Äù

When the Inspector had reported the front door locked, the Chief Constable carried out his proposal; the untouched casement swung open, and they prepared to enter the room, which hitherto had been concealed from them by the drawn curtains. Sir Clinton led the way, and as he pushed the curtain out of his road, his companions heard a bitten-off exclamation.

‚ÄúNot much of a mare’s nest, Inspector,‚Äù he continued in a cooler tone. ‚ÄúGet inside.‚Äù

The Inspector, followed by Dr. Ringwood, climbed through the open casement and stared in astonishment at the sight before them. The place they had entered was evidently one of the sitting-rooms of the bungalow, and the dust-sheets which covered the furniture indicated that the building had been shut up for the winter. In a big arm-chair, facing them as they entered, sat the body of a girl in evening dress with a cloak around her shoulders. A slight trail of blood had oozed from a wound in her head and marked her shoulder on the right side. On the floor at her feet lay an automatic pistol. One or two small chairs seemed to have been displaced roughly in the room, as though some struggle had taken place; but the attitude of the girl in the chair was perfectly natural. It seemed as though she had sat down merely to rest and death had come upon her without any warning, for her face had no tinge of fear in its expression.

‚ÄúI wasn’t far out in putting my money on Mr. Justice, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton said thoughtfully, as he gazed at the dead girl. ‚ÄúIt might have been days before we came across this affair without his help.‚Äù

He glanced round the room for a moment, biting his lip as though perplexed by some problem.

‚ÄúWe’d better have a general look round before touching the details,‚Äù he suggested, at last; and he led the way out of the room into the hall of the bungalow. ‚ÄúWe’ll try the rooms as we come to them.‚Äù

Suiting the action to the word, he opened the first door that came to hand. It proved to be that of a dismantled bedroom. The dressing-table was bare and everything had been removed from the bed expect a wire mattress. The second door led into what was obviously the dining-room of the bungalow; and here again the appearance of the room showed that the house had been shut up for the season. A third trial revealed a lavatory.

‚ÄúH’m! Clean towels hanging on the rail?‚Äù Sir Clinton pointed out. ‚ÄúThat’s unusual in an empty house, isn’t it?‚Äù

Without waiting for a minuter examination, he turned to the next door.

‚ÄúSome sort of store-room, apparently. These mattresses belong to the beds, obviously.‚Äù

Along one side of the little room were curtained shelves. Sir Clinton slid back the curtains and revealed the stacked house-napery, towels, and sheets.

‚ÄúSomebody seems to have been helping themselves here,‚Äù he indicated, drawing his companions‚Äô attention to one or two places where the orderly piling of the materials had been disturbed by careless withdrawals. ‚ÄúWe’ll try again.‚Äù

The next room provided a complete contrast to the rest of the house. It was a bedroom with all its fittings in place. The bed, fully made up, had obviously not been slept in. The dressing-table was covered with the usual trifles which a girl uses in her toilette. Vases, which obviously did not belong to the normal equipment of the room, had been collected here and filled with a profusion of expensive flowers. Most surprising of all, an electric stove, turned on at half power, kept the room warm.

‚ÄúShe’s been living here!‚Äù the Inspector exclaimed in a tone which revealed his astonishment.

Sir Clinton made a gesture of dissent. He crossed the room, and threw open the door of a cupboard wardrobe, revealing empty hooks and shelves.

‚ÄúShe’d hardly be living here with nothing but an evening frock in the way of clothes, would she?‚Äù he asked. ‚ÄúYou can look round if you like, Inspector; but I’m prepared to bet that she never set foot in this room. You won’t find much.‚Äù

He stepped over to the dressing-table and examined one by one the knick-knacks placed upon it.

‚ÄúThese things are all split-new, Inspector. Look at this face-powder box‚Äînot been opened, the band’s still intact on it. And the lip-stick’s unused. You can see that at a glance.‚Äù

Flamborough had to admit the truth of his superior’s statements.

‚ÄúH’m!‚Äù he reflected. ‚ÄúOf course it’s Mrs. Silverdale, I suppose, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúI should think so, but we can make sure about it very soon. In the meantime, let’s finish going round the premises.‚Äù

The rest of the survey revealed very little. The remainder of the house was obviously dismantled for the winter. Only once did Sir Clinton halt for any time, and that was in the pantry. Here he examined the cups suspended from hooks on the wall and pointed out to Flamborough the faint film of accumulated dust on each of them.

‚ÄúNone of that crockery has been used for weeks, Inspector. One can’t live in a house without eating and drinking, you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúA port of call, then?‚Äù the Inspector persisted. ‚ÄúShe and young Hassendean could drop in here without rousing any suspicion.‚Äù

‚ÄúPerhaps,‚Äù Sir Clinton conceded abstractedly. ‚ÄúNow we’ll get Dr. Ringwood to give his assistance.‚Äù

He led the way back to the room through which they had entered the house.

‚ÄúShe was dead before that shot was fired, of course,‚Äù he said as they crossed the threshold. ‚ÄúBut beyond that there ought to be something to be seen.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat makes you so sure that the shot didn’t kill her, sir?‚Äù the Inspector demanded.

‚ÄúBecause there wasn’t half enough blood scattered about the place. She was dead when the shot was fired‚Äîmust have been dead for some minutes, I suspect. There was no heart-action to lift the blood in her body, so consequently it sank under gravity and left her skull nearly empty of it. Then when the shot was fired, only the merest trickle came from the wound. I think that’s right, isn’t it, doctor?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s quite on the cards,‚Äù Dr. Ringwood agreed. ‚ÄúCertainly there wasn’t the normal amount of bleeding that one might have expected.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen the really important point is: how did she come to die. This is where we rely on you, doctor. Go ahead, please, and see what you make of it.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood went over to the arm-chair and began his examination of the dead girl. His glance travelled first to the open eyes, which seemed curiously dark; and a very brief inspection of their abnormal appearance suggested one possible verdict.

‚ÄúIt looks as if she’d had a dose of one of these mydriatic drugs‚Äîatropine, or something of that sort. The eye-pupils are markedly dilated,‚Äù he pronounced.

Sir Clinton refrained from glancing at the Inspector.

‚ÄúI suppose you couldn’t make a guess at the time of death?‚Äù he inquired.

Dr. Ringwood tested the stiffness of the limbs, but from his face they gathered that it was almost a purely formal experiment.

‚ÄúI’m not going to bluff about the thing. You know yourselves that rigor mortis is only the roughest test; and when there’s an unknown poison to complicate matters, I simply couldn’t give you a figure that would be worth the breath spent on it. She’s been dead for some hours‚Äîand you could have guessed that for yourselves.‚Äù

‚ÄúCongratulations, doctor! There are so few people in this world who have the honesty to say: ‚ÄòI don’t know,‚Äô when they’re questioned on their own speciality. Now you might have a look at the wound, if you don’t mind.‚Äù

While Dr. Ringwood was carrying out this part of his examination, Inspector Flamborough occupied himself in a search of the room. An ejaculation from him brought Sir Clinton to his side, and the Inspector pointed to a dark patch on the floor which had hitherto been concealed by one of the displaced chairs.

‚ÄúThere’s quite a big pool of blood here, sir,‚Äù he said tilting the chair so that the Chief Constable could see it better. ‚ÄúWhat do you make of that?‚Äù

Sir Clinton looked at him quizzically.

‚ÄúThink you’ve caught me tripping, Inspector? Not in this, I’m afraid. That’s not the girl’s blood at all. Unless I’m far out, it’s young Hassendean’s. Now, while you’re about it, will you have a good look for empty cartridge-cases on the floor. There ought to be three of them.‚Äù

The Inspector set to work, industriously grovelling on the floor as he searched under the heavier articles of furniture in the room.

‚ÄúWell, doctor, what do you make of it?‚Äù Sir Clinton asked, when he saw that Ringwood had completed his examination.

‚ÄúIt’s plain enough on the surface,‚Äù the doctor answered, as he turned away from the body. ‚ÄúShe must have been shot at quite close quarters, just above the ear. Her hair is singed with the flame of the powder. The bullet went clean through the head and then into the padded ear-piece of the chair. I expect it’s stuck there. You can see for yourself that the shot didn’t produce any twitch in the body; the position she’s sitting in shows that well enough. I’m quite prepared to bet that she was dead before the shot was fired.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe P.M. will clear that up for us definitely, if the poison can be detected,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered. ‚ÄúBut these vegetable poisons are sometimes the very devil to spot, if they’re at all out-of-the-way ones.‚Äù

He turned back to the Inspector, who was now on his feet again, dusting the knees of his trousers.

‚ÄúI’ve found three cartridge-cases sure enough, sir,‚Äù he reported. ‚ÄúTwo of them are under that couch over there; the third’s in the corner near the window. I didn’t pick them up. We’ll need to make a plan of this room, I expect; and it’s safest to leave things as they are, so as to be sure of the exact spots.‚Äù

Sir Clinton signified his approval.

‚ÄúOn the face of things, judging by the way an automatic ejects its cartridge, one might say that the single case near the window came from the shot that killed the girl. The other two, which landed somewhere near each other, might represent the two shots that made the wounds in young Hassendean’s lung. But that’s mere speculation. Let’s have a look at the pistol, Inspector.‚Äù

Flamborough put his hand into his waistcoat pocket, stooped down, picked up the pistol gingerly, and drew a rough outline of its position on the floor with a piece of chalk.

‚ÄúTry it for finger-prints, sir?‚Äù he inquired. ‚ÄúI’ve got an insufflator in the car.‚Äù

Receiving permission, he hurried off to procure his powder-sprayer, and in a few minutes he had treated the pistol with the revealing medium. As he did so, his face showed deepening disappointment.

‚ÄúNothing worth troubling about here, sir. Whoever it was handled this pistol last must have been wearing gloves. There’s nothing to be seen but a few smears of no use to us at all.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed in no wise depressed by the news.

‚ÄúThen just open it up, Inspector, and have a look at the magazine.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s three shots short of being full, sir, counting the cartridge that must be in the barrel now,‚Äù Flamborough explained, after he had slid the magazine from the butt.

‚ÄúThen you’ve found all the empty cases corresponding to the number of shots fired from this pistol, at any rate. We can leave someone else to hunt for extras when the plan’s being made. I don’t expect they’ll discover any. Now we’ll‚ÄîH’m! What’s this?‚Äù

He stepped swiftly across the room and lifted something which had rolled under a little book-case standing on four feet. As he picked it up, his companions saw that it was an amber cigarette holder. Flamborough’s face betrayed some mortification.

‚ÄúI could have sworn I looked under there,‚Äù he declared.

‚ÄúSo you did, Inspector; but it happened to be close up to one of the feet of the bookcase, and probably it was hidden from you in the position you were when you lay on the floor. It just happened to be in the right line from where I was standing a moment ago. Now let’s have a look at it.‚Äù

He held it out, handling it by the tip with the greatest precaution to avoid leaving his finger-prints upon the tube. At first sight, it seemed simply a cigarette-holder such as could be bought in any tobacconist’s shop; but as he rotated it between his finger and thumb, the other side of the barrel came into view and revealed a fly embedded in the material.

‚ÄúOne hears a lot about flies in amber,‚Äù Sir Clinton said, ‚Äúbut this is the first time I’ve seen one.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood bent over and examined the imprisoned insect.

‚ÄúThat ought to be easy enough to identify,‚Äù he commented. ‚ÄúI never saw a fly in amber before; and that one, with its wings half-spread, must be fairly well known to most of the owner’s friends.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt may have nothing to do with the case, though,‚Äù Inspector Flamborough put in. ‚ÄúIt’s quite on the cards that it was dropped there at the time the house was open for the summer. Some visitor may have lost it, for all one can tell. Or it may belong to either of the Hassendeans.‚Äù

Sir Clinton twisted the little object into a vertical position and peered into the cavity which had received the cigarettes‚Äô ends.

‚ÄúIt’s not a left-over from summer, Inspector. The tube’s got quite a lot of tarry liquid in it. That would have gone viscid if the thing had been lying there for a couple of months. No, it’s been used quite recently‚Äîwithin the last day or two, certainly.‚Äù

He moved towards the window.

‚ÄúJust bring that machine of yours, Inspector, and blow some powder over it, please.‚Äù

Flamborough obeyed; but the application of the powder revealed nothing except a few shapeless blotches on the stem of the holder.

‚ÄúNothing!‚Äù Ringwood exclaimed, with more than a tinge of disappointment in his tone.

‚ÄúNothing,‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted.

He handed the holder to Flamborough, who stowed it away safely.

‚ÄúWe’ve still to overhaul the body,‚Äù the Chief Constable suggested. ‚ÄúYou’d better do that, Inspector.‚Äù

‚ÄúNot much help in these modern dresses,‚Äù said Flamborough, eyeing the girl’s evening frock with a disparaging glance. ‚ÄúBut she ought to have a bag with her, surely. . . . Here it is!‚Äù

He plunged his hand between the body and the chair and withdrew a little bag, which he proceeded to open.

‚ÄúThe usual powder-box,‚Äù he began, enumerating the articles as they came to hand, ‚ÄúSmall mirror, silver-mounted, no initials on it. Small comb. Lipstick‚Äîbeen used once or twice. No money. No handkerchief.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou found Mrs. Silverdale’s handkerchief in the car last night,‚Äù Sir Clinton reminded him.

‚ÄúThen I suppose this must be her body, right enough, sir. Well, that seems to be all that’s here.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat about these rings she’s wearing,‚Äù the Chief Constable suggested. ‚ÄúSee if you can get them off. There may be some inscriptions on the inside; some women go in for that kind of thing.‚Äù

Fortunately the hands of the body were relaxed, and it was possible to remove the circlets from the fingers. Flamborough rose with three rings in his possession, which he examined with care.

‚ÄúYou’re on the mark there, sir, right enough. Here’s her wedding-ring. It’s engraved ‚Äò7‚Äì11‚Äì23‚Äô‚Äîthat’ll be the date of her marriage, I suppose. Then on each side of the date are initials. ‚ÄòY.S.‚Äô‚Äîthat’s for Yvonne Silverdale, obviously; and ‚ÄòF.S.‚Äô‚Äîthese’ll be her husband’s initials. Then there’s a diamond ring that she was using for a keeper. Let’s see. It’s got the same pairs of initials on each side of the date ‚Äò4‚Äì10‚Äì23.‚Äô That’ll be her engagement-ring, I expect. H’m! They don’t seem to have given themselves much time for second thoughts if the engagement lasted only a month and three days.‚Äù

He passed the two rings to Sir Clinton and picked the last one from his palm for examination.

‚ÄúThis is off the little finger. It’s a plain gold signet with Y and S intertwined on it. Evidently it’s Mrs. Silverdale right enough, sir. The inscription’s inside . . . H’m! there’s a variation here. The date’s ‚Äò15‚Äì11‚Äì25‚Äô here; but there’s only a single letter at each end: a Y at one side and a B at the other. That’s a bit of a puzzle,‚Äù he concluded, glancing at his superior to see if he could detect anything in his face.

‚ÄúI agree with you, Inspector,‚Äù was all that he elicited for his pains. ‚ÄúNow take off the bracelet, and that string of pearls round her neck. Anything of note on the bracelet?‚Äù

‚ÄúNothing whatever, sir,‚Äù the Inspector reported after a glance at it.

‚ÄúWell, you’d better put these in a safe place when we get back to town. Now does that finish us here?‚Äù

He glanced round the room and his eye was caught by the second window which looked out from the side of the bungalow. The curtains were still undrawn, and he noticed a minute gap through which the outer daylight could pass freely. A thought seemed to strike him as he ran his eyes over the fabric.

‚ÄúWe’ll just go outside for a minute,‚Äù he announced, and led the way through the hall and out of the front door. ‚ÄúLet’s see, that window’s round here, isn’t it. Keep back for a moment.‚Äù

He halted outside the window and scrutinised the ground with care for a few seconds.

‚ÄúSee that, Inspector?‚Äù he inquired. ‚ÄúThere aren’t any foot-prints that one could make anything out of; but someone has put his foot on the box edging of the path just in front of the window. It’s quite obviously crushed . . . and freshly crushed, too, by the look of it.‚Äù

Stepping softly on to the flower-bed which lay under the window-sill, he bent down until his eye was level with the chink between the curtains and peered through into the room.

‚ÄúThat’s interesting,‚Äù he said, as he turned again to face his companions. ‚ÄúOne gets quite a good view of the room from here; and it looks as if somebody had taken advantage of it last night. Nobody would attempt to look into a shut-up house in the dark, so presumably the lights were on when he took the trouble to put his eye to the crack.‚Äù

The Inspector made no pretence of concealing his delight.

‚ÄúIf we could only get hold of him. Perhaps he saw the murder actually done, sir.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed disinclined to rejoice too fervently.

‚ÄúIt’s all pure hypothesis,‚Äù he pointed out, rather frigidly.

Flamborough’s rectitude forced him into a semi-apology for past doubts.

‚ÄúYou were quite right about Mr. Justice, sir. He’s been a trump-card; and if we can only get hold of him and find out what he saw here last night, the rest ought to be as easy as kiss-your-hand.‚Äù

Sir Clinton could not restrain a smile.

‚ÄúYou’re devilish previous, Inspector, in spite of all I can do. This Peeping Tom may be Mr. Justice, or again he may not. There isn’t any evidence either way.‚Äù

He stepped back on to the path again.

‚ÄúNow, Inspector, we’ll have to leave you here in charge. It seems to be your usual r√¥le in these days. I’ll send a couple of men up to relieve you‚Äîthe fellow who makes our scale-models, too. You can set him to work. And I’ll make arrangements for the removal of Mrs. Silverdale’s body.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir. I’ll stay here till relieved.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen Dr. Ringwood and I had better get away at once.‚Äù

They walked round the bungalow to the car. As he drove away, Sir Clinton turned to the doctor.

‚ÄúWe must thank you again, doctor, for coming out here.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, that’s all right,‚Äù Ringwood assured him. ‚ÄúI got Ryder to look after my patients‚Äîat least the worst ones‚Äîthis morning. Very decent of him. He made no bones about it when he heard it was you who wanted me. It hasn’t been a pleasant job, certainly; but at least it’s been a change from the infernal grind of Carew’s practice.‚Äù

Sir Clinton drove for a few minutes in silence, then he put a question to the doctor.

‚ÄúI suppose it’s not out of the question that young Hassendean might have driven from the bungalow to Ivy Lodge with those wounds in his lungs?‚Äù

‚ÄúI see nothing against it, unless the P.M. shows something that makes it impossible. People with lung-wounds‚Äîeven fatal ones‚Äîhave managed to get about quite spryly for a time. Of course, it’s quite on the cards that his moving about may have produced fresh lesions in the tissues. What surprises me more is how he managed to find his way home through that fog last night.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat wouldn’t be so difficult,‚Äù Sir Clinton rejoined. ‚ÄúThis road runs right from the bungalow to the end of Lauderdale Avenue. He’d only to keep his car straight and recognise the turn when he came to it. It wasn’t a case of having to dodge through a network of streets.‚Äù

A thought seemed to occur to him.

‚ÄúBy the way, doctor, did you notice any peculiar coincidence in dates that we’ve come across?‚Äù

‚ÄúDates? No, can’t say I did. What do you mean?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell,‚Äù the Chief Constable pointed out deliberately, ‚Äúthe date on that scrap from the torn envelope we found in the drawer was 1925, and the figures on that mysterious signet-ring were 5‚Äì11‚Äì25. It just happened to strike me.‚Äù

His manner suggested that he had no desire to furnish any further information. Dr. Ringwood changed the subject.

‚ÄúBy the way, you didn’t examine the lever handle of the window for finger-prints,‚Äù he said, with a note of interrogation in his voice.

‚ÄúThe Inspector will do that. He’s very thorough. In any case, I don’t expect to find much on the lever.‚Äù

For a few moments Sir Clinton concentrated his attention on his driving, as they were now within the outskirts of Westerhaven. When he spoke again, his remark struck the doctor as obscure.

‚ÄúI wish that poor girl who was done in at Heatherfield last night hadn’t been such a tidy creature.‚Äù

Dr. Ringwood stared.

‚ÄúWhy?‚Äù he inquired.

‚ÄúBecause if she’d shirked her job and left those coffee-cups unwashed, it might have saved us a lot of bother. But when I looked over the scullery, everything had been washed and put away.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell . . . you don’t seem to miss much,‚Äù the doctor confessed. ‚ÄúI suppose it was what I repeated to you about Mrs. Silverdale looking queer when she came out of the drawing-room‚Äîthat put you on the track? You were thinking of drugs, even then?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat was it,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered. Then, after a moment he added: ‚ÄúAnd I’ve got a fair notion of what drug was used, too.‚Äù

Chapter VI

The Nine Possible Solutions

The police machinery under Sir Clinton’s control always worked smoothly, even when its routine was disturbed by such unpredictable events as murders. Almost automatically, it seemed, that big, flexible engine had readjusted itself to the abnormal; the bodies of Hassendean and the maid at Heatherfield had been taken into its charge and all arrangements had been made for dealing with them; Heatherfield itself had been occupied by a constabulary picket; the photographic department had been called in to take ‚Äúmetric photographs‚Äù showing the exact positions of the bodies in the two houses; inquiries had ramified through the whole district as to the motor-traffic during the previous night; and a wide-flung intelligence system was unobtrusively collecting every scrap of information which might have a bearing on this suddenly presented problem. Finally, the organism had projected a tentacle to the relief of Inspector Flamborough, marooned at the bungalow, and had replaced him by a police picket while arrangements were being made to remove Mrs. Silverdale’s body and to map the premises.

‚ÄúAnything fresh, Inspector?‚Äù Sir Clinton demanded, glancing up from his papers as his subordinate entered the room.

‚ÄúOne or two more points cleared up, sir,‚Äù Flamborough announced, with a certain satisfaction showing on his good-humoured face. ‚ÄúFirst of all, I tried the lever of the window-hasp for finger-prints. There weren’t any. So that’s done with. I could see you didn’t lay much stress on that part of the business, sir.‚Äù

The Chief Constable’s nod gave acquiescence to this, and he waited for Flamborough to continue.

‚ÄúI’ve hunted for more blood-traces about the house; and I’ve found two or three small ones‚Äîa track leading from the room to the front door. There was less blood than I expected, though.‚Äù

He produced a blood-soaked handkerchief.

‚ÄúThis was picked up near the corner of Lauderdale Avenue, sir, this morning after the fog cleared away. It has an H in one corner. You remember we found no handkerchief on Hassendean’s body. Evidently he was using this one to staunch his wounds, and he probably let it drop out of the car at the place where it was found. The doctor said there might be very little external bleeding, you remember; and the handkerchief’s mopped up a fair amount of what happened to ooze out.‚Äù

Sir Clinton again acquiesced, and the Inspector proceeded.

‚ÄúI’ve taken the finger-prints from all three bodies, sir. They’re filed for reference, if need be. And I’ve had a good look at that side-window at the bungalow. There’s no doubt that someone must have been standing there; but the traces are so poor that nothing can be done in the way of a permanent record.

‚ÄúOne can’t even see the shape of the man’s boot, let alone any fine details.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnything more?‚Äù Sir Clinton inquired. ‚ÄúYou seem to have been fairly putting your back into it.‚Äù

Flamborough’s face showed his appreciation of the compliment implied in the words.

‚ÄúI’ve drafted an advertisement‚Äîworded it very cautiously of course‚Äîasking Mr. Justice to favour us with some further information, if he has any in stock. That’s been sent off already; it’ll be in the Evening Observer to-night, and in both the morning papers to-morrow.‚Äù

‚ÄúGood! Though I shouldn’t get too optimistic over the results, if I were you, Inspector.‚Äù

Flamborough assented to this. Putting his hand into his breast pocket he produced a paper.

‚ÄúThen I’ve got a report from Detective-Sergeant Yarrow. I sent him down to the G.P.O. to find out about Mr. Justice’s telegram. It’s impossible to get a description of the sender, sir. The telegram wasn’t handed in over the counter: it was dropped into a pillar box in the suburbs in a plain envelope, along with the telegraph fee; and when it was taken to the G.P.O. they simply telegraphed it to our local office round the corner.‚Äù

‚ÄúH’m!‚Äù said Sir Clinton. ‚ÄúThere doesn’t seem much likelihood of your advertisement catching much, then. Mr. Justice is obviously a shy bird.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe is indeed, sir, as you’ll see in a moment. But I’ll finish Yarrow’s report first, if you don’t mind. When he heard this story at the G.P.O., he asked for the postman who had brought in the envelope and questioned him. It appears the thing was dropped into the pillar-box at the corner of Hill Street and Prince’s Street. That’s nowhere near the Lizardbridge Road, you remember‚Äîquite on the other side of the town.‚Äù

‚ÄúFive miles at least from the bungalow,‚Äù Sir Clinton confirmed. ‚ÄúYes, go on, Inspector.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe postman made his collection, which included this envelope, at 7 a.m. this morning. The previous collection from the same box was made at 8 p.m. last night, Yarrow elicited.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen all we really know is that the thing was dropped into the box between 8 p.m. and 7 a.m.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. Yarrow secured the original telegram form,‚Äù Flamborough continued with a glance at the paper in his hand. ‚ÄúThe envelope had been torn open carelessly and dropped into a waste-basket; but Yarrow succeeded in getting hold of it also. There’s no doubt about its identity, sir. Yarrow ascertained through whose hands the envelope and the enclosure had passed while they were in charge of the Post Office; and he persuaded all these people to let him have their finger-prints, which he took himself on the spot. He then brought all his material back here and had the envelope and its enclosure examined for finger-prints; and the two documents were photographed after the prints had been brought up on them with a powder.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd they found nothing helpful, I suppose?‚Äù

‚ÄúNothing, so far, sir. Every print that came out belonged to the postman or the sorter, or the telegraphist. There wasn’t one of them that could belong to Mr. Justice.‚Äù

‚ÄúI told you he was a shy bird, Inspector.‚Äù

The Inspector put his paper down on the desk before Sir Clinton.

‚ÄúHe’s all that, sir. He hasn’t even given us a scrap of his handwriting.‚Äù

The Chief Constable leaned forward and examined the document. It was an ordinary telegram despatch form, but the message: ‚ÄúTry hassendean bungalow lizardbridge road justice,‚Äù had been constructed by gumming isolated letters and groups of letters on to the paper. No handwriting of any sort had been used.

Sir Clinton scanned the type for a moment, running his eye over the official printed directions on the form as well.

‚ÄúHe’s simply cut his letters out of another telegram blank, apparently?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúRather ingenious, that, since it leaves absolutely no chance of identification. It’s useless to begin inquiring where a telegraphic blank came from, even if one could identify the particular sheet that he’s been using. He’s evidently got one of these rare minds than can see the obvious and turn it to account. I’d like to meet Mr. Justice.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, it certainly doesn’t leave much to take hold of, does it? Yarrow’s done his best; and I don’t see how he could have done more. But the result’s just a blank end.‚Äù

Sir Clinton looked at his watch, took out his case and offered the Inspector a cigarette.

‚ÄúSit down, Inspector. We’re talking unofficially now, you’ll note. I think we might do worse than clear the decks in this business as far as possible before we go any further. It may save time in the end.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough thought he saw a trap in front of him.

‚ÄúI’d like to hear what you think of it, sir.‚Äù

The Chief Constable’s smile showed that he understood what was passing in Flamborough’s mind.

‚ÄúI’d hate to ask a man to do something I didn’t dare to do myself,‚Äù he said, with a faint twinkle in his eye. ‚ÄúSo I’ll put my cards on the table for you to look at. If the spirit moves you, Inspector, you can do the same when your turn comes.‚Äù

The Inspector’s smile broadened into something like a grin.

‚ÄúVery good, sir. I understand that it’s purely unofficial.‚Äù

‚ÄúOn the face of it,‚Äù Sir Clinton began, ‚Äútwo people got their deaths at the bungalow last night. Young Hassendean didn’t actually die there, of course, but the shooting took place there.‚Äù

Flamborough refrained from interrupting, but gave a nod of agreement.

‚ÄúDeaths by violence fall under three heads, I think,‚Äù the Chief Constable pursued‚Äî‚Äúaccident, suicide, and homicide, including murder. Now at the bungalow you had two people put to death, and in each case the death must have been due to one or other of these three causes. Ever do permutations and combinations at school, Inspector?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir,‚Äù Flamborough confessed, rather doubtfully.

‚ÄúWell, taking the possible ways of two people dying one or other of three different deaths, there are nine different arrangements. We’ll write them down.‚Äù

He drew a sheet of paper towards him, scribbled on it for a moment or two, and then slid it across the table towards the Inspector. Flamborough bent over and read as follows:

Hassendean Mrs. Silverdale 1.‚ÄîAccident ….. Accident 2.‚ÄîSuicide …… Suicide 3.‚ÄîMurder ……. Murder 4.‚ÄîAccident ….. Suicide 5.‚ÄîSuicide …… Accident 6.‚ÄîAccident ….. Murder 7.‚ÄîMurder ……. Accident 8.‚ÄîSuicide …… Murder 9.‚ÄîMurder ……. Suicide

‚ÄúNow, since in that table we’ve got every possible arrangement which theoretically could occur,‚Äù Sir Clinton continued, ‚Äúthe truth must lie somewhere within the four corners of it.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, somewhere,‚Äù said Flamborough in an almost scornful tone.

‚ÄúIf we take each case in turn, we’ll get a few notions about what may have happened,‚Äù Sir Clinton pursued, unmoved by the Inspector’s obvious contempt for the idea. ‚ÄúBut let’s be clear on one or two points to start with. The girl, so far as one can see at present, died from poison and was shot in the head after death. Young Hassendean died from pistol-shots, of which there were two. Agreed?‚Äù

‚ÄúAgreed,‚Äù Flamborough conceded without enthusiasm.

‚ÄúThen let’s take the cases as we come to them. Case 1: The whole thing was accidental. To fit that, the girl must have swallowed a fatal dose of poison, administered by mischance either by herself or by someone else; and young Hassendean must either have shot himself twice by accident‚Äîwhich sounds unlikely‚Äîor else some third party unintentionally shot him twice over. What do you make of that?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt doesn’t sound very convincing, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúTake Case No. 2, then: A double suicide. What about that?‚Äù

‚ÄúThese lovers‚Äô suicide-pacts aren’t uncommon,‚Äù the Inspector admitted. ‚ÄúThat might be near the truth. And I suppose he might have put a bullet through her head before shooting himself, just in case the poison hadn’t worked.‚Äù

He drew a notebook from his pocket.

‚ÄúJust a moment, sir. I want to make a note to remind me to see about young Hassendean’s pistol license, if he had one. I think he must have had. I found a box and a half of ammunition in one of the drawers when I was searching the house after you’d gone.‚Äù

Sir Clinton paused while the Inspector made his jotting.

‚ÄúNow we can take the third case,‚Äù he continued, as Flamborough closed his pocket-book. ‚ÄúIt implies that Mrs. Silverdale was deliberately poisoned and that young Hassendean was shot to death intentionally, either by her before she died or by some third party.‚Äù

‚ÄúThree of them seems more likely than two,‚Äù the Inspector suggested. ‚ÄúThere’s the man who opened the window to be fitted in somewhere, you know, and there were signs of a struggle, too.‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite true, Inspector. I suppose you can fit the shot in Mrs. Silverdale’s head into the scheme also?‚Äù

Flamborough shook his head without offering any verbal comment on the question.

‚ÄúThen we’ll take Case 4,‚Äù the Chief Constable pursued. ‚ÄúMrs. Silverdale deliberately poisoned herself, and young Hassendean came by his end accidentally. In other words, he was shot by either Mrs. Silverdale or by a third party‚Äîbecause I doubt if a man could shoot himself twice over by accident.‚Äù

Flamborough shook his head again, more definitely this time.

‚ÄúIt doesn’t sound likely, sir.‚Äù

Then his face changed.

‚ÄúWait a bit, though,‚Äù he added quickly: ‚ÄúIf that’s what happened, she must have had a motive for suicide. Perhaps someone was on her track, somebody pretty dangerous; and she saw the game was up. I don’t profess to know how that could happen. But if the man on her heels was the fellow who did the work with the tourniquet at Heatherfield last night, she might have thought poison an easier way out of things. It’s a possibility, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt leaves us hunting for the clue to a purely hypothetical mystery, though, Inspector, I’m afraid. I don’t say you’re wrong, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúI daresay it’s complicated enough already,‚Äù Flamborough admitted without prejudice. ‚ÄúBesides, this Case 4 of yours has another flaw in it‚Äîseveral, in fact. Unless you take the idea I suggested, it’s hard to see why the girl should have had a supply of poison handy at all. It sounds a bit wild. And you’ve got to assume that a third party shot young Hassendean twice by accident, if a third party came into the business at all. To my mind, that won’t wash, sir. It’s not good enough. Whereas if it was a case of Mrs. Silverdale shooting him by accident, there was no need for her to commit suicide because of that. No one knew she was here. She could simply have walked out of the front door and got clear away with no questions asked. And if she’d already taken poison, she wouldn’t need to shoot herself in the head, would she?‚Äù

‚ÄúGrave objections,‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted. It amused him to see the Inspector entering so keenly into the game. ‚ÄúNow we proceed to Case 5.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, Case 5 is just bunkum,‚Äù the Inspector pronounced bluntly. ‚ÄúShe gets accidentally poisoned; then she gets accidentally shot; then young Hassendean suicides. It’s too thick altogether.‚Äù

‚ÄúI like the concise way you put it,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered with simulated admiration. ‚ÄúSo we go on to No. 6, eh? She was deliberately murdered and he was accidentally shot. What about that?‚Äù

‚ÄúI’d want to see some motive for the murder, sir, before accepting that as a possible basis. And if she was deliberately poisoned, what was the good of young Hassendean dragging her off to the bungalow? That would throw suspicion straight on to him if he poisoned her. . . .‚Äù

Flamborough broke off and seemed to think hard for a moment or two.

‚ÄúThat’s a fresh line,‚Äù he exclaimed suddenly. ‚ÄúI’ve been assuming all along that either she or young Hassendean used the poison. But it might have been a third party. I never thought of it in that light, sir.‚Äù

He pondered again, while Sir Clinton watched his face.

‚ÄúIt might have been someone else altogether, if the poison was a slow-acting one. Someone at Heatherfield perhaps.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere was only one available person at Heatherfield just then,‚Äù Sir Clinton pointed out.

‚ÄúYou mean the maid, sir? Of course! And that might help to account for her death, too. It might be a case of Judge Lynch, sir. Somebody squaring the account without bothering us about it.‚Äù

New horizons seemed to be opening up in the Inspector’s mind.

‚ÄúI’ll admit there’s something in this method of yours, after all, sir,‚Äù he conceded gracefully.

‚ÄúI like your ‚Äòafter all,‚Äô Inspector. But at any rate you seem to find the method suggestive, which is something, at least.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt certainly puts ideas into one’s mind that one mightn’t have thought about otherwise. What about the next case?‚Äù

‚ÄúCase 7? That’s the converse of the last one. He was shot deliberately and she died by accident. What about it?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat would mean, sir, that either she took an overdose of the drug by mistake or someone gave her a fatal dose, ditto. Then either she or a third party shot young Hassendean.‚Äù

‚ÄúSomething of the sort.‚Äù

‚ÄúH’m! It’s no worse than some of the other suggestions. I wonder, now. . . . She didn’t look like a dope-fiend, so far as I could see; but she might have been just a beginner and taken an overdose by accident. Her eye-pupils were pretty wide-open. That wouldn’t fit in with her snuffing morphine or heroin, but she might have been a cocaine addict, for all we know. . . . This method of yours is very stimulating, sir. It makes one think along fresh lines.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, have another think, Inspector. Case 8: he suicided and she was murdered.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat brings us up against the missing motive again, sir. I’d like to think over that later on.‚Äù

‚ÄúCase 9, then: He was murdered and she committed suicide. What about that?‚Äù

‚ÄúLet me take it bit by bit, sir. First of all, if he was murdered, then either she did it or a third party did it. If she did it, then she might have premeditated it, and had her dose of poison with her, ready to swallow when she’d shot young Hassendean. That’s that. If a third party murdered young Hassendean, she might have suicided in terror of what was going to happen to her; but that would imply that she was carrying poison about with her. Also, this third party‚Äîwhoever he was‚Äîmust have had his knife pretty deep in both of them. That’s one way of looking at it. But there’s another side to the thing as well. Suppose it was one of these suicide-pacts and she took the poison as her part of the bargain; then, before he can swallow his dose, the third party comes on the scene and shoots him. That might be a possibility.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd the third party obligingly removed the superfluous dose of poison, for some inscrutable reason of his own, eh?‚Äù

‚ÄúH’m! It seems silly, doesn’t it?‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course, unlikely things do happen,‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted. ‚ÄúI’m no stickler for probability in crime. One so seldom finds it.‚Äù

Flamborough took his notebook from his pocket and entered in it a copy of Sir Clinton’s classification.

‚ÄúI’ll have another think about this later on,‚Äù he said, as he finished writing. ‚ÄúI didn’t think much of it when you showed it to me at first, but it certainly seems to be one way of getting a few ideas to test.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow let’s look at the thing from another point of view,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested. ‚ÄúAssume that young Hassendean and Mrs. Silverdale were in the room of the bungalow. There were traces of somebody at the side-window, and someone certainly broke the glass of the front window. By the way, Inspector, when you went over young Hassendean’s clothes finally last night, did you find a key-ring or anything of that sort?‚Äù

‚ÄúHe had a few keys‚Äîthe latchkey of Ivy Lodge, and one or two more.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’ll need to make sure that the key of the bungalow was amongst them, because if it wasn’t, then he may have had to break in‚Äîwhich would account for the window. But I’m pretty certain he didn’t do that. He’d been up beforehand with these flowers in the afternoon, getting the place ready. It’s most improbable that he hadn’t the key of the front door with him.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ll see to it,‚Äù the Inspector assured him.

‚ÄúIn the meantime, just let’s assume that the broken window represents the work of a third party. What do you make of things on that basis?‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat is there to make out of them except one thing?‚Äù Flamborough demanded. ‚ÄúAt the side window you had somebody whom you christened Peeping Tom; at the front window was a second person who got so excited that he broke into the room. You’re not trying to make out that these two characters were filled by one person, are you, sir? There would be no point in Peeping Tom leaving his window and walking round to the front one before breaking in. Either window was good enough for that. He’d no need to shift his ground.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù Sir Clinton assured him in a thoughtful tone, ‚ÄúI wasn’t looking at it from that angle. I was merely wondering where Mr. Justice came in.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou mean whether he was Peeping Tom or t’other?‚Äù

‚ÄúSomething of that sort,‚Äù the Chief Constable answered. Then, changing the subject, he added: ‚ÄúWhat bits of information are you going to hunt for next, Inspector?‚Äù

Flamborough ran over some points in his mind and cleared his throat before speaking.

‚ÄúFirst of all, I want to know what this poison was, where it came from, and how long it takes to act. I expect to get something from the P.M. results, and we can always send some of the organs for analysis.‚Äù

Sir Clinton nodded his agreement.

‚ÄúI think we’ll get two people on to that part of the thing independently. Say a London man and perhaps one of the chemists at the Croft-Thornton Institute here. We’ll need to see this fellow Markfield in any case, just to check the statements that Ringwood gave us, and when we’re doing that we can find out if there’s anyone capable of doing the analysis for us. Perhaps Markfield himself might take it on.‚Äù

The Inspector, seeing that Sir Clinton was waiting for him to continue, proceeded with his list of evidence required.

‚ÄúI’ll put Yarrow on to the matter of young Hassendean’s pistol license. That won’t take long to look up, and it will help to clinch the fact that it really was his pistol that we found on the floor. I don’t suppose for a moment that it was brought in from the outside. The loose ammunition in the drawer seems convincing on that point.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m quite with you there,‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted.

‚ÄúThen I want to look into the maid’s affairs and see if she had any grudge against Mrs. Silverdale. It’s a pity the second maid’s so ill. We can’t get anything out of her for a while, I’m afraid. And I want her for another thing: to see if Mrs. Silverdale doped herself at all. But I expect, if she did, that I’ll be able to pick up some hint of it somewhere or other. And of course, if the poison turns out to be a non-dope kind, that line of inquiry drops into a subsidiary place.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes?‚Äù the Chief Constable encouraged him.

‚ÄúThen I’ll send a man up to try the keys we found in young Hassendean’s pocket on the lock of the bungalow door, just to clear up the broken window matter. That won’t take long.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd then?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, I suppose I’ll need to make a try at finding out who Peeping Tom was and also your Mr. Justice.‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite a lot of suggestions you seem to have extracted from my little list of possibilities, Inspector. I think you owe it an apology for the rather contemptuous way you approached it at first.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, it’s been more suggestive than I expected, I admit.‚Äù

‚ÄúOne thing’s certain, Inspector. The solution of the affair must lie somewhere on that little table. It’s simply a matter of picking out the proper case. The odds at most are eight to one and they’re really less than that if one discards some of the very improbable combinations.‚Äù

The desk-telephone rang sharply, and Sir Clinton listened to the message.

‚ÄúThat interests you, Inspector. A report’s come in that Mr. Silverdale came home and has gone down to the Croft-Thornton. He mentioned where he was going to the constable in charge at Heatherfield, and he very thoughtfully suggested that as the Croft-Thornton is quite near here, it would be easy for us to interview him there if we desired to do so. The perfect little gentleman, in fact. Well, what about it, Inspector?‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose I’d better go at once,‚Äù Flamborough proposed after a glance at his watch.

‚ÄúI think I’ll include myself in the invitation,‚Äù Sir Clinton volunteered. ‚ÄúAnd, by the way, you’d better take that fly-in-the-amber cigarette-holder with you, if they’ve finished with it downstairs. Young Hassendean was working at the Croft-Thornton and someone there may be able to identify it for us if it was his. I’m not anxious to trouble his relations in the matter.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir,‚Äù Flamborough acquiesced. ‚ÄúYou’ll want your car. I’ll give the order for it now.‚Äù

Chapter VII

The Fly in the Amber

At the door of the big block of buildings which formed the Croft-Thornton Institute, Inspector Flamborough made inquiries from the porter and obtained a guide through the labyrinth of stairs and corridors.

‚ÄúThis is Dr. Markfield’s laboratory, sir,‚Äù their pilot finally informed them as he knocked on a door. ‚ÄúTwo gentlemen to see you, sir,‚Äù he announced, standing aside to allow Sir Clinton and the Inspector to enter.

As they walked into the laboratory, Trevor Markfield came towards them from one of the benches at which he had been occupied. His face betrayed his slight surprise at finding two strangers before him.

‚ÄúWhat can I do for you!‚Äù he inquired politely, but without any needless effusiveness.

Flamborough, in response to an almost imperceptible gesture from his superior, stepped to the front.

‚ÄúThis is Sir Clinton Driffield, the Chief Constable, Dr. Markfield. I’m Inspector Flamborough. We’ve called to see if you could give us some expert assistance in a case.‚Äù

Markfield, after a glance at a water-bath on which a flask was being heated, led the way to a little office which adjoined the laboratory and closed the door behind the party.

‚ÄúWe shall be more private here,‚Äù he said, inviting them with a gesture to take chairs. ‚ÄúOne of my assistants will be back shortly, and I take it that your business is likely to be confidential.‚Äù

The Inspector agreed with a nod.

‚ÄúIt’s a poisoning case and we’ll need some help in detecting the poison.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s a bit vague,‚Äù Markfield commented with a smile. ‚ÄúThere are so many kinds of poisons, you know. If it’s arsenic or anything of that sort, a first-year student could spot it for you; but if it’s one of the organic lot, it’ll be a stiff business most likely.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt looks like one of the mydriatic alkaloids,‚Äù Sir Clinton put in. ‚ÄúAtropine, or something akin to it. The eye-pupils of the body were dilated.‚Äù

Markfield considered for a moment.

‚ÄúI’ve done some alkaloid work in my time,‚Äù he explained, ‚Äúbut I suppose in a case of this kind you ought to have the best man. Some of the alkaloids are the very devil to spot when you’ve only a small quantity. I’d like the fee for the case, of course,‚Äù he added with a faint smile, ‚Äúbut the truth is that Dr. Silverdale, my chief, is an alkaloid specialist. He’s worked on them for years, and he could give me points all along the line. I’ll take you along to his room now.‚Äù

He rose from his chair, but a gesture from Flamborough arrested him.

‚ÄúI’m afraid that would hardly do, Dr. Markfield. As a matter of fact, it’s Mrs. Silverdale’s death that we’re inquiring into!‚Äù

Markfield could not repress an exclamation at the Inspector’s statement.

‚ÄúMrs. Silverdale? You don’t mean to say that anything’s happened to her? Good God! I knew the girl quite well. Nobody could have a grudge against her.‚Äù

He glanced from one official to the other, as though doubting his ears.

‚ÄúWait a bit,‚Äù he added, after a moment’s pause. ‚ÄúPerhaps I’ve taken you up wrong. Do you mean Yvonne Silverdale?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù the Inspector confirmed.

Markfield’s face showed a struggle between incredulity and belief.

‚ÄúBut that girl hadn’t an enemy in the world, man,‚Äù he broke out at last. ‚ÄúThe thing’s clean impossible.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ve just seen her body,‚Äù said the Inspector curtly.

The blunt statement seemed to have its effect.

‚ÄúWell, if that’s so, you can count on me for any work you want me to do. I’m quite willing to take it on.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s very satisfactory, Dr. Markfield,‚Äù Sir Clinton interposed. ‚ÄúNow, perhaps you could give us help in another line as well. You seem to have been a friend of Mrs. Silverdale’s. Could you tell us anything about her‚Äîanything you think might be useful to us?‚Äù

A fresh thought seemed to pass through Markfield’s mind and a faint suggestion of distrust appeared on his face.

‚ÄúWell, I’m ready to answer any questions you care to put,‚Äù he said, though there seemed to be a certain reluctance in his voice.

Sir Clinton’s attitude indicated that it was the turn of the Inspector. Flamborough pulled out his notebook.

‚ÄúFirst of all, then, Dr. Markfield, could you tell us when you first became acquainted with Mrs. Silverdale?‚Äù

‚ÄúShortly after she and her husband came to Westerhaven. That’s about three years ago, roughly.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou knew her fairly well?‚Äù

‚ÄúI used to see her at dances and so forth. Lately, I’ve seen less of her. She picked up other friends, naturally; and I don’t dance much nowadays.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe danced a good deal, I understand. Can you tell me any particular people who associated with her frequently in recent times?‚Äù

‚ÄúI daresay I could give you a list of several. Young Hassendean was one. She used him as a kind of dancing-partner, from all I heard; but I go out so little nowadays that I can’t speak from much direct knowledge on the point.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat sort of person was Mrs. Silverdale, in your judgment?‚Äù

Markfield took a little time to consider this question.

‚ÄúShe was French, you know,‚Äù he replied. ‚ÄúI always found her very bright. Some people called her frivolous. She was out to enjoy herself, of course. Naturally she was a bit out of place in a backwater like this. She got some people’s backs up, I believe. Women didn’t like her being so smartly-dressed and all that.‚Äù

‚ÄúHave you any reason to suppose that she took drugs?‚Äù

Markfield listened to this question with obvious amazement.

‚ÄúDrugs? No. She’d never touch drugs. Who’s been putting that lie around?‚Äù

Flamborough tactfully disregarded this question.

‚ÄúThen from what you know of her, you would say that suicide would be improbable in her case?‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite, I should say.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe had no worries that you know of, no domestic troubles, for instance?‚Äù

Markfield’s eyes narrowed slightly at the question.

‚ÄúHardly my business to discuss another man’s affairs, is it?‚Äù he demanded, obviously annoyed by the Inspector’s query. ‚ÄúI don’t think I’m called upon to repeat the tittle-tattle of the town.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou mean you don’t know anything personally?‚Äù

‚ÄúI mean I’m not inclined to gossip about the domestic affairs of a colleague. If you’re so keen on them, you can go and ask him direct.‚Äù

It was quite evident that Markfield had strong views on the subject of what he called ‚Äútittle-tattle‚Äù; and the Inspector realised that nothing would be gained by pursuing the matter. At the same time, he was amused to see that Markfield, by his loyalty to his colleague, had betrayed the very thing which he was trying to conceal. It was obvious that things had not gone smoothly in the Silverdale household, or Markfield would have had no reason for burking the question.

‚ÄúYou mentioned young Hassendean’s name,‚Äù Flamborough continued. ‚ÄúYou know that he’s been murdered, of course?‚Äù

‚ÄúI saw it in the paper this morning. He’s no great loss,‚Äù Markfield said brutally. ‚ÄúWe had him here in the Institute, and a more useless pup you’d be hard put to it to find.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat sort of person was he?‚Äù the Inspector inquired.

‚ÄúOne of these bumptious brats who think they ought to have everything they want, just for the asking. He’d a very bad swelled head. Herring-gutted, too, I should judge. He used to bore me with a lot of romantic drivel until I sat on him hard once or twice. I couldn’t stand him.‚Äù

It was evident that young Hassendean had rasped Markfield’s nerves badly.

‚ÄúHad anyone a grudge against him, do you think?‚Äù

‚ÄúI shouldn’t be surprised, knowing him as I did. He would have put a saint’s back up with his bounce and impertinence. But if you mean a grudge big enough to lead to murder, I can’t say. I saw as little of him as possible even in working hours, and I had no interest in his private affairs.‚Äù

It was quite evident that nothing of real value was to be elicited along this line. The Inspector abandoned the subject of young Hassendean’s personality and turned to a fresh field.

‚ÄúYoung Hassendean smoked cigarettes, didn’t he?‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ve seen him smoking them.‚Äù

‚ÄúIs this his holder, by any chance?‚Äù

Flamborough produced the fly-in-amber holder as he spoke and laid it on the table. As he did so, he glanced at Markfield’s face and was surprised to see the swift change of expression on it. A flash of amazement followed by something that looked like dismay, crossed his features; then, almost instantaneously, he composed himself, and only a faint trace of misgiving showed in his eyes.

‚ÄúNo, that isn’t young Hassendean’s holder,‚Äù he answered.

‚ÄúYou recognise it?‚Äù

Markfield bent forward to inspect the article, but it was evident that he knew it well.

‚ÄúDo I need to answer these questions of yours?‚Äù he demanded, uncomfortably.

‚ÄúYou’ll have that question put to you at the inquest when you’re on your oath,‚Äù said the Inspector sharply. ‚ÄúYou may as well answer now and save trouble.‚Äù

Markfield stared for a moment longer at the fly in the amber.

‚ÄúWhere did you pick this thing up?‚Äù he demanded, without answering the Inspector’s question.

But Flamborough saw that he had got on the track of something definite at last, and was not inclined to be put off.

‚ÄúThat’s our business, sir,‚Äù he said brusquely. ‚ÄúYou recognise the thing, obviously. Whose is it? It’s no use trying to shield anyone. The thing’s too conspicuous; and if you don’t tell us about it, someone else will. But it doesn’t look well to find you trying to throw dust in our eyes.‚Äù

Markfield could not help seeing that the Inspector attached special importance to the holder; and he evidently recognised that further shuffling was out of the question.

‚ÄúI’m not going to identify it for you,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúYou’ve let slip that it’s an important clue; and I don’t know it well enough to make assertions about it. I’ll send for a man now who’ll be able to swear definitely, one way or another. That’s all I see my way to do for you.‚Äù

He put his hand on a bell-push and they waited in silence until a boy came in answer to the summons.
