‚ÄúSend Gilling to me at once,‚Äù Markfield ordered.

Then, when the boy had withdrawn, he turned to the two officials again.

‚ÄúGilling is our head mechanic. You can question him about it. He’s an intelligent man.‚Äù

In a few minutes the mechanic appeared at the door.

‚ÄúYou wanted me, sir?‚Äù he asked.

Markfield introduced the Inspector with a gesture, and Flamborough put his questions.

‚ÄúYou’ve seen this thing before?‚Äù

The mechanic came forward to the table and examined the holder carefully.

‚ÄúYes, sir. I made it myself.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’re quite sure of that?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo mistake about it. I know my own work.‚Äù

‚ÄúTell us what you know about it,‚Äù the Inspector demanded.

The mechanic thought for a moment or two.

‚ÄúIt was about three months ago, sir. If you want it, I can look up the exact date in my workshop notebook where I keep a record of each day’s work. I made two of them for Dr. Silverdale at that time.‚Äù

Flamborough shot a glance at Markfield’s downcast face. It was pretty obvious now who was being shielded; and the Inspector remembered how Markfield had fenced in the matter of the domestic troubles of the Silverdales.

‚ÄúTell us exactly what happened then,‚Äù Flamborough encouraged the mechanic.

‚ÄúDr. Silverdale came to me one morning with some bits of stuff in his hand‚Äîamber-looking, same as this holder. He told me he’d been manufacturing some new stuff‚Äîa condensate like Bakelite. He wanted me to see if it could be filed and turned and so on. I remember his showing me the fly, there. He’d put it into the stuff as a joke‚Äîa fly to prove that the thing was genuine amber, and take people in when he showed the stuff to them. The condensate stuff was in sticks, two of them, about six inches long by an inch thick, so he suggested that I’d better make two cigarette-holders and see if the thing would stand being worked on a lathe without splitting or cracking. So I made the two holders for him. I remember the trouble I had to steer clear of the fly while I was shaping the thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd what happened to the holders after that?‚Äù

‚ÄúDr. Silverdale used the one with no fly in it for a bit and kept the other one for show. Then he lost the plain one‚Äîhe’s always leaving his holders about the place on the benches‚Äîand he took to using the one with the fly in it. He’s been smoking with it for a month or more, now. I remember just last week asking him whether it was wearing well, when he came into the workshop with it in his mouth.‚Äù

‚ÄúHave another good look at it,‚Äù Flamborough suggested. ‚ÄúI want to be sure there’s no mistake.‚Äù

Gilling examined the holder once more.

‚ÄúThat’s the one I made, sir. I could swear to it.‚Äù

He hesitated a moment as if wishing to ask a question; but Flamborough, having got his information, dismissed the mechanic without more ado. When the man had gone, he turned back to Markfield.

‚ÄúI don’t quite like your way of doing things, Dr. Markfield. You might have given us the information at once without all this shuffling, for I could see at a glance you had recognised this cigarette-holder. If you’re trying to shield your colleague from a reasonable investigation, I’ll take the liberty of reminding you that one can become an accessory after the fact as well as before it.‚Äù

Markfield’s face grew stormy as he listened to the Inspector’s warning.

‚ÄúI’d have a look at the law on slander, if I were you, Inspector, before you start flinging accusations about. If you remember the facts, it’ll help. I’ve only seen this holder at a distance when Dr. Silverdale was using it. I’ve never had a good look at it until you produced it. Naturally, although I had very little doubt about whose it was, still I wasn’t going to assert that it was Silverdale’s. But I got you a man who could identify it properly. What more do you want?‚Äù

Flamborough’s face showed that he found this defence quite unsatisfactory. Markfield’s obvious fencing with him at the start had left its impression on his mind.

‚ÄúWell, when you do this analysis for us, remember that you’ll have to testify about it in the witness-box,‚Äù he said, bluntly. ‚ÄúWe can’t have any qualifications and fine distinctions then, you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ll be quite prepared to stand over any results I get,‚Äù Markfield asserted with equal bluntness. ‚ÄúBut I don’t guarantee to find a poison if it isn’t there, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere is something there, according to the doctor,‚Äù Flamborough declared. ‚ÄúNow I think I’d like to see Dr. Silverdale, if you can tell us where to find him.‚Äù

Markfield’s temper was evidently still ruffled, and he was obviously glad to be rid of the Inspector. He conducted them along a passage, pointed out a door, and then took leave of them in the curtest fashion.

They entered the room which had been shown to them; and while Flamborough was explaining who they were, Sir Clinton had leisure to examine Silverdale. He saw an alert, athletic man with a friendly manner, who looked rather younger than his thirty-five years. Whatever Silverdale’s domestic troubles might have been, he showed few outward signs of them. When they disturbed him, he had been sitting before a delicate balance; and as he rose, he slid the glass front down in order to protect the instrument. Apart from his surroundings, it would have been difficult to determine his profession; for he had an open-air skin which certainly did not suggest the laboratory. He carried himself well, and only a yellow stain of picric acid on the right-hand side of his old tweed laboratory jacket detracted from his spruceness and betrayed the chemist.

‚ÄúI’ve been expecting you, Inspector Flamborough,‚Äù he said, as soon as he realised who his visitors were. ‚ÄúThis has been a dreadful business last night. It was a bolt from the blue to me when I got home this morning.‚Äù

He paused, and looked inquiringly at the Inspector.

‚ÄúHave you any notion why that unfortunate maid of mine was murdered? It’s a complete mystery to me. A dreadful business.‚Äù

Flamborough exchanged a glance with the Chief Constable. As Silverdale had ignored his wife’s death, it seemed to the Inspector that the news of it might be broken to him later, when the other case had been dealt with. Silverdale, of course, could hardly have picked up any hint about the affairs at the bungalow, since a knowledge of them was still confined to the police and Dr. Ringwood.

‚ÄúWe’re rather at a loss at present,‚Äù Flamborough admitted frankly. ‚ÄúAs things stand, it looks rather like a case of a detected burglar who killed the woman when she disturbed him at his work. Had you any stock of valuables on your premises which might have attracted gentry of that sort?‚Äù

Silverdale shook his head.

‚ÄúMy wife had a certain amount of jewellery, but I don’t think any burglar would have found it worth while to go the length of murder for the sake of it.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhere did Mrs. Silverdale keep her jewellery?‚Äù

‚ÄúI rather think it’s kept in one of the drawers of an old chest-of-drawers in her room‚Äîthe drawer that the man broke into. But she may have other things elsewhere. We had different rooms, you know; and I never troubled to find out where she put things in her own room.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose you couldn’t give us a list of your wife’s jewellery?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, I really don’t know what she has. I could tell you one or two things, of course; but I couldn’t guarantee to remember them all.‚Äù

Flamborough switched off to a fresh line.

‚ÄúThis maid of yours was reliable? I mean, she couldn’t have been a confederate of the burglar by any chance?‚Äù

Silverdale shook his head.

‚ÄúQuite out of the question, I should say. That maid had been with us ever since we were married; and before that she’d been in service with an aunt of mine who died. She’d always had a good character, and she was old enough not to do anything silly.‚Äù

‚ÄúAn old family retainer? I see, sir. And you never had any friction with her, I suppose?‚Äù

‚ÄúCertainly not.‚Äù

Flamborough returned to his earlier line of inquiry.

‚ÄúYou can’t think of anything else a burglar might have had his eye on in your house, sir? Apart from the jewellery, I mean.‚Äù

Silverdale seemed taken aback by the question.

‚ÄúI don’t quite understand, I’m afraid. What could a burglar want except jewellery or plate? And he might take all the plate I keep away with him and not be much the richer.‚Äù

Flamborough seemed unable to think of any fresh question to put on that particular subject. His face took on a new expression.

‚ÄúI’m afraid we’ve got worse news for you, sir,‚Äù he began, and in a few sentences he put Silverdale in possession of the barest outline of the bungalow tragedy. Sir Clinton, watching the manner in which the bereaved husband received the news, had to confess to himself that he could make nothing of what he saw. Silverdale’s manner and words were just what might have been expected in the circumstances.

Flamborough allowed a decent interval to elapse before he came directly to business once more.

‚ÄúNow, Dr. Silverdale, I’m sorry I’ve got to ask some awkward questions; but I’m sure you’ll give us your best help in clearing up this affair. I hate to worry you‚ÄîI’m sure you understand that‚Äîbut it’s essential that we should get certain information at the earliest possible moment. That’s my excuse.‚Äù

Before Silverdale could reply, the door of the laboratory opened, and a slim, graceful girl came into the room. At the sight of the two strangers, she halted shyly. Sir Clinton caught a gleam in Silverdale’s expression as he turned towards the girl: a touch of something difficult to define.

‚ÄúJust a moment, Miss Deepcar, please. I’m engaged just now.‚Äù

‚ÄúI only came to tell you that I’d taken that mixed melting-point. It’s hyoscine picrate, as you thought it was.‚Äù

‚ÄúThanks,‚Äù Silverdale returned. ‚ÄúI’ll come round to your room in a few minutes. Please wait for me.‚Äù

Something in the brief exchange of information seemed to have attracted Sir Clinton’s attention. He glanced at the girl as she turned to leave the room; then he appeared to re-concentrate his mind upon Flamborough’s questions.

‚ÄúNow, Dr. Silverdale,‚Äù Flamborough went on, ‚Äúthis is a very nasty business, and I don’t mind admitting that we’re in the dark just now. Can you think of anything which might connect the deaths of the maid and Mrs. Silverdale?‚Äù

Silverdale stared at the floor for a time, as though turning possibilities over in his mind.

‚ÄúI can’t imagine how there could be any connection whatever,‚Äù he said at last.

Flamborough decided to approach the most awkward part of his subject. It was impossible to tell from his manner what was coming next, but it was clear that he had something important to ask.

‚ÄúNow, Dr. Silverdale, I want to be as tactful as I can; but if I go over the score, I hope you’ll take the will for the deed.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, you can be as blunt as you like,‚Äù Silverdale retorted, with the first signs of impatience which he had shown. ‚ÄúAsk what you choose.‚Äù

‚ÄúThanks,‚Äù the Inspector answered with apparent relief. ‚ÄúThen I’ll come straight to the point. What precisely were the relations existing between Mrs. Silverdale and young Hassendean?‚Äù

Silverdale’s face paled slightly and his lips tightened as this blunt response to his offer fell on his ears. He seemed to consider his reply carefully.

‚ÄúI suppose you mean: ‚ÄòWas she unfaithful to me with young Hassendean?‚Äô Then my answer would be: ‚ÄòSo far as my information goes, no.‚Äô She flirted with the young cub certainly; and they behaved, to my mind, very injudiciously; but to the best of my knowledge it went no further than that. I’d have brought them up with a round turn if they’d given me cause.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s your candid opinion?‚Äù the Inspector demanded. ‚ÄúYou’re keeping back nothing?‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy, man, I’d have given . . .‚Äù Silverdale broke out. Then he stopped short in mid-sentence. ‚ÄúIt’s my candid opinion, as you put it,‚Äù he ended tamely.

Flamborough, it seemed, had extracted the information he wanted. He left the subject and took up a fresh one.

‚ÄúDo you recall anything important which happened in the year 1925?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, I left London and took up my post here.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou were married in 1923, weren’t you?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

‚ÄúHad your wife any relations in this country? She was French, wasn’t she?‚Äù

‚ÄúShe had a brother, Octave Renard, who was in business in London. Still is, as a matter of fact. An old aunt is the only other relation I know of.‚Äù

‚ÄúBefore you left London, had you any difficulties with Mrs. Silverdale‚ÄîI mean anything like young Hassendean?‚Äù

‚ÄúNothing that came to my notice,‚Äù Silverdale answered, after consulting his memory.

‚ÄúCan you recall any friend of yours or of hers who had the initial B? Either in the Christian name or the surname, I mean. It might be either a man or a woman.‚Äù

This question evidently surprised Silverdale.

‚ÄúThe initial B?‚Äù he repeated. ‚ÄúNo. I can’t recall anyone to fit that.‚Äù

He seemed to be running over a list of people in his mind, but at the end of half-a-minute he shook his head decidedly.

‚ÄúNo. I can’t think of anyone with that initial.‚Äù

Flamborough’s face betrayed his dissatisfaction. He had evidently built some hopes on getting the information.

‚ÄúNow, another point, Dr. Silverdale. Have you any reason to suppose that Mrs. Silverdale was addicted to drugs?‚Äù

This time, Silverdale’s surprise at the question was quite unfeigned:

‚ÄúDrugs? Of course not! Unless you count cocktails as drugs. What on earth put that into your mind?‚Äù

The Inspector rather shamefacedly abandoned this line of inquiry, and turned to something else.

‚ÄúI’d like to hear anything you can tell me about young Hassendean, sir. He worked here in the Institute, didn’t he?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat depends a good deal on what precise meaning you attach to the word ‚Äòwork,‚Äô Inspector. He certainly loafed about the premises, but he did as little as he could.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell,‚Äù said Flamborough, impatiently, ‚Äúcan you tell me anything else about him? Everyone I’ve interviewed yet has told me he was idle. I’d rather have something more to the point.‚Äù

Silverdale thought for a moment or two.

‚ÄúHe was a nuisance from the start. When he came here first‚Äîsome three years ago‚Äîhe spent his time hanging round one of the girl-assistants: Miss Hailsham. He interfered with her work, and I had to speak to him about it several times. Then she got engaged to him. Some time after, my wife took him up, and he broke off his engagement to Miss Hailsham‚Äîpossibly to please my wife. I remember it made things rather unpleasant here when the engagement was broken, because Miss Hailsham took it rather badly. She’d every reason to do so, though she wasn’t losing much, it seemed to me.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough pricked up his ears at this information.

‚ÄúIs this Miss Hailsham still an assistant here?‚Äù he asked.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Silverdale explained. ‚ÄúShe’s one of my private assistants. I have several girls who do routine work; but Miss Hailsham and Miss Deepcar‚Äîthe girl who came in here a moment ago‚Äîare a shade better than the usual run.‚Äù

‚ÄúCould you make an excuse to let me have a look at Miss Hailsham?‚Äù Flamborough inquired.

‚ÄúShe’s not here to-day,‚Äù Silverdale answered. ‚ÄúOff with a sore throat, or something of that sort. But if you’ll come back another time, I can take you to her room if you wish. You can pose as a visitor whom I’m showing round, if you don’t want to appear officially.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir. I’ll drop in some other day. Now, another point, if you don’t mind. Mrs. Silverdale wore a signet ring. Can you tell me anything about it? Did she get it from you or did she buy it herself?‚Äù

‚ÄúI didn’t make her a present of it,‚Äù Silverdale answered promptly. ‚ÄúI believe she got it made by some jeweller or other. I remember a few years ago she took it into her head to seal all her letters‚Äîsome passing fad in the crowd she used to associate with, I suppose. But once she started doing it, she kept it up. I think she must have got the signet ring made for that purpose.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough nodded thoughtfully as though he attached some importance to this information. Then, in a casual tone he inquired:

‚ÄúYou weren’t at home last night, of course? Where were you?‚Äù

‚ÄúI was‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

Suddenly a thought seemed to cross Silverdale’s mind and he halted abruptly in his sentence. Then he amended his statement most obviously.

‚ÄúI spent the night working here.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough noted the words in his pocket-book with marked deliberation. Then he looked round the room and seemed dissatisfied with something. As though to give himself time to think before asking another question, he moved over to the window and gazed down thoughtfully into the main thoroughfare below. Whatever his reflections may have been, the result of them was singularly feeble. He turned back to Silverdale and put a final question:

‚ÄúI suppose you can’t think of any other point that might help us to throw light on this business, sir?‚Äù

Silverdale shook his head decidedly.

‚ÄúI’m quite in the dark about it all.‚Äù

The Inspector looked him up and down deliberately for a moment.

‚ÄúWell, in that case, sir, I don’t think we need take up any more of your time. I’ll remove the police from your house. It’s been disinfected already by the sanitary people, so you can go back there any time you choose, now. Thanks for the help you’ve given us.‚Äù

Flamborough did not speak to Sir Clinton until they had put the length of a corridor between themselves and Silverdale’s laboratory.

‚ÄúI think I’ll drop in and see Dr. Markfield again, sir,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúI’m not at all satisfied about some things.‚Äù

‚ÄúDo so, Inspector. I quite agree with you!‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ll make an excuse about the arrangements for this analysis. Not that I’ll lay much stress on Markfield’s results when we get them, sir. He’s made a bad impression on me over that evidence he gave us before. People shouldn’t equivocate in a murder case merely to shield their friends. We’ve troubles enough without that sort of thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, handle him tactfully, Inspector, or he may turn stubborn. If he takes refuge in ‚ÄòI don’t remember,‚Äô or anything of that sort, you’ll not get much out of him.‚Äù Sir Clinton observed.

‚ÄúI shan’t frighten him,‚Äù Flamborough assured him, as they approached Markfield’s room.

As they entered, Markfield looked up in surprise at seeing them once more.

‚ÄúIt’s just occurred to me that I forgot to make arrangements about handing that stuff over to you for analysis,‚Äù Flamborough said, as he went forward. ‚ÄúIt’ll be in sealed jars, of course; and I’d prefer to hand it over to you personally. I suppose I could always get hold of you either here or at your house?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’d better come here. My housekeeper’s away just now nursing some relation who’s down with ‘flu, and my house is empty except when I happen to be at home myself. You’ll find me here between nine in the morning and six at night‚Äîexcept for lunch-time, of course. I generally clear out of here at six and dine down town.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose you have a long enough day of it,‚Äù the Inspector said in a casual tone. ‚ÄúYou don’t come back here and work in the evening?‚Äù

‚ÄúSometimes, if there’s something interesting that brings me back. But I haven’t done that for weeks past.‚Äù

‚ÄúThis place is shut up at night, isn’t it? I mean, you don’t keep a porter or a watchman on the premises?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo. But each of the seniors has a private key, of course. I can get in any time I wish. It’s the same at the Research Station.‚Äù

The Inspector seemed to be struck by an idea.

‚ÄúAny valuable stuff on the premises, by any chance?‚Äù

‚ÄúNothing a thief could make much out of. There’s a thousand or fifteen hundred pounds worth of platinum, dishes, electrodes, and so forth, in the safe. I believe the man on the beat is supposed to give special attention to the place and notify anything suspicious immediately; but I’ve never known anything of the sort to happen.‚Äù

‚ÄúRather a difficult position for our men if the staff can come and go freely at night,‚Äù the Inspector pointed out. ‚ÄúIf a constable sees a light in the window, what’s he to make of it? Does Dr. Silverdale work late often?‚Äù

‚ÄúI really couldn’t tell you.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou don’t see much of him privately, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúVery little,‚Äù Markfield answered. ‚ÄúOnly when I run across him by accident down town, like last night.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou met him, did you?‚Äù

‚ÄúHardly even that. I happened to drop into the Grosvenor for dinner after I left here. I can’t get meals at home just now unless I cook ’em myself. As I was finishing my coffee, Silverdale came into the dining-room with Miss Deepcar and took a table in the window recess. I didn’t disturb them, and I don’t think they noticed me.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen they were just beginning dinner when you left the place? What time was that, can you tell me?‚Äù

Markfield looked suspiciously at the Inspector.

‚ÄúYou’re trying to get me to say something that you want to use against‚Äîwell, someone else, shall we say? I don’t care about it, frankly. But since you could get the information from the waiter who served them, there’s no harm done. I went to the Grosvenor at 6.35 or thereabouts. I was going down to the Research Station afterwards to pick up some notes, so I dined early that night. Silverdale and Miss Deepcar came in just as I was finishing dinner‚Äîthat would be about a quarter past seven or thereby. I expect they were going on to some show afterwards.‚Äù

‚ÄúWas she in evening dress?‚Äù

‚ÄúAsk me another. I never can tell whether a girl’s in evening dress or not, nowadays, with these new fashions.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough closed his notebook and took his leave, followed by Sir Clinton. When they reached the street again and had got into the waiting car, the Chief Constable turned to his subordinate.

‚ÄúYou collected a lot of interesting information that time.‚Äù

‚ÄúI noticed you left it all to me, sir; but I think I got one or two things worth having. It’s a bit disconnected; and it’ll take some thinking before it’s straightened out.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat’s your main inference, as things stand?‚Äù Sir Clinton inquired.

‚ÄúWell, sir, it’s a bit early yet. But I’ve been wondering about one thing, certainly.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd that is?‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd that is whether Peeping Tom’s name wasn’t Thomasina,‚Äù Flamborough announced gravely.

‚ÄúThere are two sexes, of course,‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted with equal gravity. ‚ÄúAnd inquisitiveness is supposed to be more strongly developed in the female than in the male. The next thing will be to consider whether Mr. Justice shouldn’t be rechristened Justitia. One ought to take all possibilities into account.‚Äù

Chapter VIII

The Hassendean Journal

When Ronald Hassendean’s journal was found to consist of four bulky volumes of manuscript, Sir Clinton hastily disclaimed any desire to make its acquaintance in extenso and passed over to Inspector Flamborough the task of ploughing through it in detail and selecting those passages which seemed to have direct bearing on the case. The Inspector took the diary home with him and spent a laborious evening, lightened at times by flashes of cynical enjoyment when the writer laid bare certain aspects of his soul. Next day Flamborough presented himself at Sir Clinton’s office with the books under his arm; and the paper slips which he had used as markers made a formidable array as they projected from the edges of the volumes.

‚ÄúGood Lord!‚Äù exclaimed the Chief Constable in consternation. ‚ÄúDo you mean to say I ought to read through about a hundred and fifty passages in inferior handwriting? Life’s too short for that. Take ’em away, Inspector, and get someone to write me a pr√©cis.‚Äù

Flamborough’s lips opened into a broad smile under his toothbrush moustache.

‚ÄúIt’s not really so bad as it looks, sir,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúThe white slips were put in to mark anything that seemed to bear remotely on the business; but the passages directly relevant to the affair are indicated by red slips. I think you ought to glance through that last lot. There aren’t really very many of them.‚Äù

He deposited the volumes on Sir Clinton’s desk so that the marking-tabs projected towards his superior. Sir Clinton eyed them without any enthusiasm.

‚ÄúWell, I suppose duty calls, Inspector. I’ll go over them with you, just in case you want to give me any special points drawn from your general reading in the Works of Hassendean. If you’ve got a morbid craving for voluminous writers, you’d better start on the Faerie Queene. It, also, leads up to the death of a Blatant Beast.‚Äù

‚ÄúI read a bit of it at school, sir. I’m keeping the rest for a rainy day.‚Äù

Sir Clinton again eyed the four stout volumes with unconcealed aversion. Quite obviously he was ready to catch at anything in order to postpone the examination of them, even now that he had decided to submit to the Inspector’s ruling.

‚ÄúBefore I start on this stuff, there are one or two points I want to get cleared up. First of all, did you get any reports in reply to our inquiries about young Hassendean’s car being seen on the roads that night?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir. The only motor information we got was about one car that was stolen under cover of the fog. It’s being looked into. Oh, yes, and there was an inquiry for the name and address of the owner of a car. It seems somebody got hit by a motor and managed to take its number. I don’t think any real damage was done. It’s just one of these try-on cases.‚Äù

‚ÄúSomething more important now. Did you find out from the man on the beat whether there was a light in Silverdale’s room at the Croft-Thornton on the night of the murders?‚Äù

‚ÄúHow did you come to think of that, sir? I didn’t mention it to you.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was just a long shot, Inspector. As soon as Silverdale stated that he had been working all that night at the Croft-Thornton, I was pretty sure he was lying. So were you, I guessed. Then you walked across to the window and looked down. As I was wondering myself whether the window was visible from the street, it didn’t take much mind-reading to see what you were driving at. And from your questions to Markfield later on, I couldn’t help inferring that you had the constable on the beat at the back of your thoughts. Obviously you meant to check Silverdale’s story by asking the constable on duty if he’d noticed a light in Silverdale’s room that night. There was no light, of course?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir. There wasn’t a light anywhere in the building, that night. I made the constable look up his notebook.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen you’ve caught Master Silverdale in a very bad lie. By the way, I suppose you noticed that girl who came into his room while we were talking to him: the Miss Deepcar who dined with him down town that night. What did you make of her?‚Äù

‚ÄúPretty girl, sir, very pretty indeed. The quiet sort, I’d judge. One of the kind that a man might do a good deal to get hold of, if he was keen on her.‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s expression showed that he did not disagree with the Inspector’s summing up.

‚ÄúBy the way,‚Äù he continued, ‚Äúdid you take any note of what she said to Silverdale at that time?‚Äù

‚ÄúNot particularly, sir. It was all Greek to me‚Äîtoo technical.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt interested me, though,‚Äù Sir Clinton confessed. ‚ÄúI’ve a chemical friend‚Äîthe London man who’s going to act as a check on Markfield for us in the search for the poison, as a matter of fact‚Äîand he talks to me occasionally about chemistry. You don’t know what a ‚Äòmixed melting-point‚Äô is, I suppose?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir. It sounds confused,‚Äù said the Inspector mischievously.

The Chief Constable treated this as beneath contempt.

‚ÄúI’ll explain the point,‚Äù he pursued, ‚Äúand then you’ll know as much as I do. A pure substance melts at a higher temperature than it does when it’s contaminated by even a trace of some foreign material. Suppose that you had been given a stuff which you thought was pure quinine and you had no chemicals handy to do the ordinary tests for quinine. What you’d do would be this. You’d take the melting-point of your sample first of all. Then to the sample you’d add a trace of something which you knew definitely was quinine‚Äîa specimen from your laboratory stock, say. Then you’d take the melting-point of this mixture. Suppose the second melting-point is lower than the first, then obviously you’ve been adding an impurity to your original sample. And since something, that you know definitely to be quinine, has acted as an impurity, then clearly the original stuff isn’t quinine. On the other hand, if the addition of your trace of quinine to the sample doesn’t lower the melting-point, then your original sample is proved to be quinine also. That mixing of the two stuffs and taking the melting-point is what they call ‚Äòtaking a mixed melting-point.‚Äô Does that convey anything to you?‚Äù

‚ÄúNot a damn, sir,‚Äù Flamborough admitted crudely, in a tone of despair. ‚ÄúCould you say it all over again slowly?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s hardly worth while at this stage,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered, dismissing the subject. ‚ÄúI’ll take it up again with you later on, perhaps, after we get the P.M. results. It was an illuminating conversation, though, Inspector, if my guess turns out to be right. Now there’s another matter. Have you any idea when the morning papers get into the hands of the public‚ÄîI mean the earliest hour that’s likely in the normal course?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt happens that I do know that, sir. The local delivery starts at 7 a.m. In the suburbs, it’s a bit later, naturally.‚Äù

‚ÄúJust make sure about it, please. Ring up the publishing departments of the Courier and the Gazette. You needn’t worry about the imported London papers.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir. And now about this journal, sir?‚Äù the Inspector added with a touch of genial impishness in his voice.

‚ÄúEvidently you won’t be happy till I look at it,‚Äù Sir Clinton grumbled with obvious distaste for the task. ‚ÄúLet’s get it over, then, since you’re set on the matter.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo far as I can see, sir,‚Äù Flamborough explained, ‚Äúthere are only three threads in it that concern us: the affair he had with that girl Hailsham; his association with Mrs. Silverdale; and his financial affairs‚Äîwhich came as a surprise to me, I must admit.‚Äù

Sir Clinton glanced up at the Inspector’s words; but without replying, he drew the fat volumes of the journal towards him and began his examination of the passages to which Flamborough’s red markers drew attention.

‚ÄúHe didn’t model his style on Pepys, evidently,‚Äù he said as he turned the leaves rapidly, ‚ÄúThere seems to be about ten per cent. of ‚ÄòI’s‚Äô on every page. Ah! Here’s your first red marker.‚Äù

He read the indicated passage carefully.

‚ÄúThis is the description of his feelings on getting engaged to Norma Hailsham,‚Äù he commented aloud. ‚ÄúIt sounds rather superior, as if he felt he’d conferred a distinct favour on her in the matter. Apparently, even in the first flush of young love, he thought that he wasn’t getting all that his merits deserved. I don’t think Miss Hailsham would have been flattered if she’d been able to read this at the time.‚Äù

He passed rapidly over some other passages without audible comment, and then halted for a few moments at an entry.

‚ÄúNow we come to his meeting with Mrs. Silverdale, and his first impressions of her. It seems that she attracted him by her physique rather than by her brains. Of course, as he observes: ‚ÄòWhat single woman could fully satisfy all the sides of a complex nature like mine?‚Äô However, he catalogues Mrs. Silverdale’s attractions lavishly enough.‚Äù

Flamborough, with a recollection of the passage in his mind, smiled cynically.

‚ÄúThat side of his complex nature was highly developed, I should judge,‚Äù he affirmed. ‚ÄúIt runs through the stuff from start to finish.‚Äù

Sir Clinton turned over a few more pages.

‚ÄúIt seems as though Miss Hailsham began to have some inklings of his troubles,‚Äù he said, looking up from the book. ‚ÄúThis is the bit where he’s complaining about the limitations in women’s outlooks, you remember. Apparently he’d made his fianc√©e feel that his vision took a wider sweep than she imagined, and she seems to have suggested that he needn’t spend so much time in staring at Mrs. Silverdale. It’s quite characteristic that in this entry he’s suddenly discovered that the Hailsham girl’s hands fail to reach the standard of beauty which he thinks essential in a life-companion. He has visions of sitting in suppressed irritation while these hands pour out his breakfast coffee every day through all the years of marriage. It seems to worry him quite a lot.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’ll find that kind of thing developing as you go on, sir. The plain truth is that he was tiring of the girl and he simply jotted down everything he could see in her that he didn’t find good enough for him.‚Äù

Sir Clinton glanced over the next few entries.

‚ÄúSo I see, Inspector. Now it seems her dancing isn’t so good as he used to think it was.‚Äù

‚ÄúAny stick to beat a dog with,‚Äù the Inspector surmised.

‚ÄúNow they seem to have got the length of a distinct tiff, and he rushes at once to jot down a few bright thoughts on jealousy with a quotation from Mr. Wells in support of his thesis. It appears that this ‚Äòentanglement,‚Äô as he calls it, is cramping his individuality and preventing the full self-expression of his complex nature. I can’t imagine how we got along without that word ‚Äòself-expression‚Äô when we were young. It’s a godsend. I trust the inventor got a medal.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe next entry’s rather important, sir,‚Äù Flamborough warned him.

‚ÄúAh! Here we are. We come to action for a change instead of all this wash of talk. This is the final burst-up, eh? H’m!‚Äù

He read over the entry thoughtfully.

‚ÄúWell, the Hailsham girl seems to have astonished him when it came to the pinch. Even deducting everything for his way of looking at things, she must have been fairly furious. And Yvonne Silverdale’s name seems to have entered pretty deeply into the discussion. ‚ÄòShe warned me she knew more than I thought she did; and that she’d make me pay for what I was doing.‚Äô And again: ‚ÄòShe said she’d stick at nothing to get even with me.‚Äô It seems to have been rather a vulgar scene, altogether. ‚ÄòShe wasn’t going to be thrown over for that woman without having her turn when it came.‚Äô You know, Inspector, it sounds a bit vindictive, even when it’s filtered through him into his journal. The woman scorned, and hell let loose, eh? I’m not greatly taken with the picture of Miss Hailsham.‚Äù

‚ÄúA bit of a virago,‚Äù the Inspector agreed. ‚ÄúWhat I was wondering when I read that stuff was whether she’d keep up to that standard permanently or whether this was just a flash in the pan. If she’s the kind that treasures grievances. . . .‚Äù

‚ÄúShe might be an important piece in the jigsaw, you mean? In any case, I suppose we’ll have to get her sized up somehow, since she plays a part in the story.‚Äù

The Chief Constable turned back to the journal and skimmed over a number of the entries.

‚ÄúDo you know,‚Äù he pointed out after a time, ‚Äúthat young fellow had an unpleasant mind.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou surprise me,‚Äù the Inspector retorted ironically. ‚ÄúI suppose you’ve come to the place where he gets really smitten with Mrs. Silverdale’s charms?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes. There’s a curious rising irritation through it all. It’s evident that she led him on, and then let him down, time after time.‚Äù

‚ÄúFor all his fluff about his complex character and so forth, he really seems to have been very simple,‚Äù was Flamborough’s verdict. ‚ÄúShe led him a dance for months; and anyone with half an eye could see all along that she was only playing with him. It’s as plain as print, even in his own account of the business.‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite, I admit. But you must remember that he imagined he was out of the common‚Äîirresistible. He couldn’t bring himself to believe things were as they were.‚Äù

‚ÄúTurn to the later entries,‚Äù the Inspector advised; and Sir Clinton did so.

‚ÄúThis is the one you mean? Where she turned him down quite bluntly, so that even he got an inkling of how matters really stood?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes. Now go on from there,‚Äù Flamborough directed.

Sir Clinton passed from one red marker to the other, reading the entries indicted at each of the points.

‚ÄúThe tune changes a bit; and his irritation seems to be on the up-grade. One gets the impression that he’s casting round for a fresh method of getting his way and that he hasn’t found one that will do? Is that your reading of it?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Flamborough confirmed. ‚ÄúHe talks about getting his way ‚Äòby hook or by crook,‚Äô and one or two other phrases that come to the same thing.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, that brings us up to a week ago. There seems to be a change in his tone, now. More expectation and less exasperation, if one can put it that way.‚Äù

‚ÄúI read it that by that time he’d hit on his plan. He was sure of its success, sir. Just go on to the next entry please. There’s something there about his triumph, as he calls it.‚Äù

Sir Clinton glanced down the page and as he did so his face lit up for a moment as though he had seen one of his inferences confirmed.

‚ÄúThis what you mean?‚Äù he asked. ‚Äú‚ÄòAnd only I shall know of my triumph‚Äô?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s it, sir. High-falutin and all that; but it points to his thinking he had the game in his hands. I’ve puzzled my brains a bit over what he really meant by it, though. One might read it that he meant to murder the girl in the end. That would leave him as the only living person who knew what had happened, you see?‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not in a position to contradict that assumption,‚Äù Sir Clinton confessed. ‚ÄúBut so far as that goes, I think you’ll find the point cleared up in a day or two at the rate we’re going.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou’re very optimistic, sir,‚Äù was all the Inspector found to reply. ‚ÄúNow I’ve left one matter to the end, because it may have no bearing on the case at all. The last year of that journal is full of groans about his finances. He seems to have spent a good deal more than he could afford, in one way and another. I’ve noted all the passages if you want to read them, sir. They’re among the set marked with white slips.‚Äù

‚ÄúJust give me the gist of them,‚Äù the Chief Constable suggested. ‚ÄúFrom that, I can see whether I want to wade through the whole thing or not.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s simple enough, sir. He’s been borrowing money on a scale that would be quite big for his resources. And I gather from some of the entries that he had no security that he could produce. It seems he daren’t go to his uncle and ask him to use his capital as security‚ÄîI mean young Hassendean’s own capital which was under his uncle’s control as trustee. So he was persuaded to insure his life in favour of his creditor for a good round sum‚Äîfigure not mentioned.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo in the present circumstances the moneylender will rake in the whole sum insured, after paying only a single premium?‚Äù

‚ÄúUnless the insurance company can prove suicide.‚Äù

Sir Clinton closed the last volume of the journal.

‚ÄúI’ve heard of that sort of insurance racket before. And of course you remember that shooting affair in Scotland thirty years ago when the prosecution made a strong point out of just this very type of transaction. Have you had time to make any inquiries along that line yet?‚Äù

Flamborough was evidently glad to get the opportunity of showing his efficiency.

‚ÄúI took it up at once, sir. In one entry, he mentioned the name of the company: the Western Medical and Mercantile Assurance Co. I put a trunk call through to their head office and got the particulars of the policy. It’s for ¬£5,000 and it’s in favour of Dudley Amyas Guisborough & Co.‚Äîthe moneylender.‚Äù

‚ÄúSounds very aristocratic,‚Äù the Chief Constable commented.

‚ÄúOh, that’s only his trade sign. His real name’s Spratton.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo claim been made yet?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir. I don’t suppose he’s hurrying. The inquest was adjourned, you remember; and until they bring in some verdict excluding suicide, Spratton can’t do much. There’s a suicide clause in the policy, I learned. But if it pans out as a murder, then Spratton’s ¬£5,000 in pocket.‚Äù

‚ÄúIn fact, Inspector, Mr. Justice is doing a very good bit of work for Dudley Amyas Guisborough & Co.‚Äù

Flamborough seemed struck by an idea.

‚ÄúI’ll go and pay a call on Mr. Spratton, I think. I’ll do it now.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, he’s a local light, is he?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. He was mixed up in a case last year. You won’t remember it, though. It never came to much. Just an old man who fell into Spratton’s hands and was driven to suicide by the damnable rapacity of that shark. Inspector Ferryside had to look into the matter, and I remember talking over the case with him. That’s how it sticks in my memory.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, see what you can make of him, Inspector. But I shan’t be disappointed if you come back empty-handed. Even if he were mixed up in this affair, he’ll have taken good care not to leave a straight string leading back to his front door. If it was a case of murder for profit, you know, there would be plenty of time to draw up a pretty good scheme beforehand. It wouldn’t be done on the spur of the moment.‚Äù

Chapter IX

The Creditor

Inspector Flamborough’s orderly mind found something to respect in the businesslike appearance of the moneylender’s premises. As he waited at the counter of the outer office while his card was submitted to the principal, he was struck by the spick-and-span appearance of the fittings and the industry of the small staff.

‚ÄúQuite impressive as a fly-trap,‚Äù he ruminated. ‚ÄúLooks like a good solid business with plenty of money to spend. And the clerks have good manners, too. Spratton’s evidently bent on making a nice impression on new clients.‚Äù

He was not kept waiting more than a minute before the clerk returned and ushered him into a room which had very little of the office in its furnishings. As he entered, a clean-shaven man in the late thirties rose from an arm-chair beside the fire. At the first glance, his appearance seemed to strike some chord in the Inspector’s memory; and Flamborough found himself pursuing an elusive recollection which he failed to run to ground.

The moneylender seemed to regard the Inspector’s visit as a perfectly normal event. His manner was genial without being effusive.

‚ÄúCome in, Inspector,‚Äù he invited, with a gesture towards one of the comfortable chairs. ‚ÄúTry a cigarette?‚Äù

He proffered a large silver box, but Flamborough declined to smoke.

‚ÄúAnd what can I do for you?‚Äù Spratton inquired pleasantly, replacing the box on the mantelpiece. ‚ÄúMoney’s very tight these days.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not a client,‚Äù Flamborough informed him, with a slightly sardonic smile. ‚ÄúSorry to disappoint you.‚Äù

The moneylender’s eyes narrowed, but otherwise he showed no outward sign of his feelings.

‚ÄúThen I’m rather at a loss to know what you want,‚Äù he confessed, without any lapse from his initial geniality. ‚ÄúI run my business strictly within the four corners of the Act. You’ve no complaint about that?‚Äù

The Inspector had no intention of wasting time.

‚ÄúIt’s this affair of young Hassendean,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúThe young fellow who was murdered the other day. You must have seen the case in the papers. I understand he was a client of yours.‚Äù

A flash of intelligence passed over the moneylender’s face, but he suppressed it almost instantly.

‚ÄúHassendean?‚Äù he repeated, as though cudgelling his memory. ‚ÄúI’ve some recollection of the name. But my business is a large one, and I don’t profess to carry all the details in my head.‚Äù

He stepped over to the bell and rang it. When a clerk appeared in answer to the summons, the moneylender turned to give an order:

‚ÄúI think we had some transactions with a Mr. Hassendean‚ÄîMr. Ronald Hassendean, isn’t it?‚Äù he glanced at Flamborough for confirmation, and then continued: ‚ÄúJust bring me that file, Plowden.‚Äù

It did not take the Inspector long to make up his mind that this by-play was intended merely to give Spratton time to find his bearings; but Flamborough waited patiently until the clerk returned and placed a filing-case on the table. Spratton turned over the leaves for a few moments, as though refreshing his memory.

‚ÄúThis fellow would have made a good actor,‚Äù Flamborough reflected with a certain admiration. ‚ÄúHe does it deuced well. But who the devil does he remind me of?‚Äù

Spratton’s nicely-calculated interlude came to an end, and he turned back to the Inspector.

‚ÄúYou’re quite right. I find that he had some transactions with us!‚Äù

‚ÄúThey began about eleven months ago, didn’t they?‚Äù

The moneylender nodded in confirmation.

‚ÄúI find that I lent him ¬£100 first of all. Two months after that‚Äîhe not having repaid anything‚ÄîI lent him ¬£200. Then there was a further item of ¬£300 in April, part of which he seems to have paid back to me later on in order to square up for the interest which he hadn’t paid.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat security had you for these loans?‚Äù

Again the moneylender’s eyes narrowed for a moment; but his manner betrayed nothing.

‚ÄúUp to that time, I was quite satisfied with his prospects.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd after that he borrowed more from you?‚Äù

‚ÄúApparently.‚Äù Spratton made a pretence of consulting the file. ‚ÄúHe came to me in June for another ¬£500, and of course the interest was mounting up gradually.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe must have been making the money fly,‚Äù Flamborough suggested with a certain indifference. ‚ÄúI wish I could see my way to splash dibs at that rate. It would be a new experience. But when it came to figures of that size, I suppose you expected something better in the way of security?‚Äù

Despite the Inspector’s casual tone, the moneylender seemed to suspect a trap.

‚ÄúWell, by that time he was in my books for well over a thousand.‚Äù

He appeared to feel that frankness would be best.

‚ÄúI arranged matters for him,‚Äù he continued. ‚ÄúHe took out a policy on his life with the Western Medical and Mercantile. I have the policy in my safe if you wish to see it.‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course you allowed a reasonable margin for contingencies, I suppose?‚Äù Flamborough inquired sympathetically.

‚ÄúOh, naturally I expected him to go on borrowing, so I had to allow a fair margin for contingencies. The policy was for ¬£5,000.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo you’re about ¬£4,000 in pocket, now that he’s dead,‚Äù Flamborough commented enviously. ‚ÄúSome people are lucky.‚Äù

‚ÄúAgainst that you’ve got to offset the bad debts I make,‚Äù Spratton pointed out.

Flamborough could not pretend to himself that he had managed to elicit much of importance during his call; but he had no excuse for prolonging the interview. He rose to his feet.

‚ÄúI don’t suppose we shall need any of these facts if it comes to trying anyone,‚Äù he said, as he prepared to leave. ‚ÄúIf we do, you’ll have plenty of warning, of course.‚Äù

The moneylender opened a door which allowed a direct exit into the corridor, and Flamborough went out. As he walked along the passage, he was still racking his memory to discover who Spratton resembled; and at last, as he reached the pavement outside, it flashed into his mind.

‚ÄúOf course! It’s the Chief! Put a moustache on to that fellow and dye his hair a bit and he might pass for Driffield in the dusk. He’s not a twin-brother; but there’s a resemblance of sorts, undoubtedly.‚Äù

He returned to headquarters feeling that he had wasted his time over the moneylender. Except that he had now seen the man in the flesh and had an opportunity of sizing him up, he was really no further forward than he had been before; for the few actual figures of transactions which he had obtained were obviously of little interest in themselves.

As he entered the police station, a constable came forward.

‚ÄúThere’s a gentleman here, Inspector Flamborough. He’s called about the Silverdale case and he wants to see you. He’s a foreigner of the name of Renard.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery well. Send him along to me,‚Äù Flamborough ordered.

In a few moments, the constable ushered in a small man with a black moustache and a shock of stiffly-brushed hair which gave him a foreign appearance. The Inspector was relieved to find that he spoke perfect English, though with a slight accent.

‚ÄúMy name is Octave Renard,‚Äù he introduced himself. ‚ÄúI am the brother of Mrs. Yvonne Silverdale.‚Äù

Flamborough, with a certain admiration for the fortitude of the little man in the tragic circumstances, made haste to put him at his ease by expressing his sympathy.

‚ÄúYes, very sad,‚Äù said the little Frenchman, with an obvious effort to keep himself under control. ‚ÄúI was very fond of my sister, you understand. She was so gay, so fond of life. She enjoyed herself every moment of the day. And now‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

A gesture filled out the missing phrase.

Flamborough’s face betrayed his commiseration; but he was a busy man, and could ill afford to waste time.

‚ÄúYou wished to see me about something?‚Äù

‚ÄúAll I know is what was printed in the newspapers,‚Äù Renard explained. ‚ÄúI would like to learn the truth of the case‚Äîthe real facts. And you are in charge of the case, I was told. So I come to you.‚Äù

Flamborough, after a moment’s hesitation, gave him an outline of the bungalow tragedy, softening some of the details and omitting anything which he thought it undesirable to make public. Renard listened, with an occasional nervous twitch which showed that his imagination was at work, clothing the bare bones of the Inspector’s narrative with flesh.

‚ÄúIt is a bad business,‚Äù he said, shaking his head mournfully as Flamborough concluded. ‚ÄúTo think that such a thing should have happened just when she had had her great stroke of good-fortune! It is incredible, the irony of Fate.‚Äù

The Inspector pricked up his ears.

‚ÄúShe’d had a piece of good luck, lately, you say, Mr. Renard? What was that?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou do not know?‚Äù the little man inquired in surprise. ‚ÄúBut surely her husband must have told you? No?‚Äù

Flamborough shook his head.

‚ÄúThat is strange,‚Äù Renard continued. ‚ÄúI do not quite understand that. My sister was the favourite of her aunt. She was down in her will, you understand? And my aunt was a very wealthy woman. Pots of money, as you English say. For some time my aunt has been in feeble health. She has been going downhill for the last year or more. A heart trouble, you understand. And just a fortnight ago, puff!‚Äîshe went out like that. Like a blown-out candle.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes?‚Äù the Inspector prompted.

‚ÄúHer will was in the keeping of her lawyer and he communicated the contents to myself and my sister. We were trustees, you see. I had a little bequest to myself; but the principal sum went to my sister. I was surprised; I had not thought that my aunt had so much money‚Äîmostly in American stocks and shares. In your English money it came to about ¬£12,000. In francs, of course, it is colossal‚Äîa million and a half at least.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh!‚Äù interjected Flamborough, now keenly interested. ‚ÄúAnd your sister knew of this?‚Äù

‚ÄúShe learned it from me just two days before her death. And you understand, there was no grief with it. My aunt had suffered terribly in the last few months. Angina pectoris, very painful. We were quite glad to see her suffering at an end.‚Äù

Flamborough felt that this fresh piece of information needed consideration before he ventured on to the ground which had been disclosed.

‚ÄúAre you staying in Westerhaven, Mr. Renard?‚Äù he inquired.

‚ÄúYes, for a few days yet, I expect,‚Äù the little man answered. ‚ÄúI have some legal matters in my hands which need my presence on the spot. As my sister is now dead, there is the disposal of this money to be considered. I find difficulties which I had not expected.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd your address during your stay will be?‚Äù

‚ÄúI am at the Imperial Hotel. You can always find me there.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, Mr. Renard, I’d like to have a talk with you later on, if I may. Just at present, I’m very busy. Perhaps you could spare a few minutes when my hands are free.‚Äù

‚ÄúI shall be delighted,‚Äù Renard acquiesced. ‚ÄúWhenever you wish to see me, send a message. I am much worried, you understand?‚Äù he concluded, with a quiver in his voice which pierced through the official coating of Flamborough and touched the softer material inside.

Chapter X

Information Received

For the next day or two, Sir Clinton’s interest in the Hassendean case appeared to have faded out; and Inspector Flamborough, after following up one or two clues which eventually proved useless, was beginning to feel perturbed by the lack of direct progress which the investigation showed. Rather to his relief, one morning the Chief Constable summoned him to his office. Flamborough began a somewhat apologetic account of his fruitless investigations; but Sir Clinton cut him short with a word or two of appreciation of his zeal.

‚ÄúHere’s something more definite for you to go on,‚Äù he suggested. ‚ÄúI’ve just had a preliminary report from the London man whom we put on to search for the poison. I asked him to let me have a private opinion at the earliest possible moment. His official report will come in later, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúHas he spotted it, sir?‚Äù the Inspector inquired eagerly.

‚ÄúHe’s reached the same conclusion as I did‚Äîand as I suppose you did also,‚Äù Sir Clinton assured him.

Flamborough looked puzzled.

‚ÄúI didn’t spot it myself,‚Äù he confessed diffidently. ‚ÄúIn fact, I don’t see how there was anything to show definitely what stuff it was, barring dilatation of the eye-pupils, and that might have been due to various drugs.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou should never lose an opportunity of exercising your powers of inference, Inspector. I mustn’t rob you of this one. Now put together two things: the episode of the mixed melting-point and the phrase about his ‚Äòtriumph‚Äô that young Hassendean wrote in his journal. Add the state of the girl’s pupils as a third point‚Äîand there you are!‚Äù

Flamborough pondered for a while over this assortment of information, but finally shook his head.

‚ÄúI don’t see it yet, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúIn that case,‚Äù Sir Clinton declared, with the air of one bestowing benevolence, ‚ÄúI think we’d better let it dawn on you slowly. You might be angry with yourself if you realised all of a sudden how simple it is.‚Äù

He rose to his feet as he spoke.

‚ÄúI think we’ll pay a visit to the Croft-Thornton Institute now, and see how Markfield has been getting along with his examination. We may as well have a check, before we begin to speculate too freely.‚Äù

They found Markfield in his laboratory, and Sir Clinton came to business at once.

‚ÄúWe came over to see how you were getting on with that poison business, Dr. Markfield. Can you give us any news?‚Äù

Markfield indicated a notebook on his desk.

‚ÄúI’ve got it out, I think. It’s all there; but I haven’t had time to write a proper report on it yet. It was‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

‚ÄúHyoscine?‚Äù Sir Clinton interrupted.

Markfield stared at him with evident appreciation.

‚ÄúYou’re quite right,‚Äù he confirmed, with some surprise. ‚ÄúI suppose you’ve got private information.‚Äù

The Chief Constable evaded the point.

‚ÄúI’m asking this question only for our own information; you won’t be asked to swear to it in court. What amount of hyoscine do you think was in the body, altogether? I mean, judging from the results you obtained yourself.‚Äù

Markfield considered for a moment.

‚ÄúI’m giving you a guess, but I think it’s fairly near the mark. I wouldn’t, of course, take my oath on it. But the very smallest quantity, judging from my results, would be somewhere in the neighbourhood of seven or eight milligrammes.‚Äù

‚ÄúHave you looked up anything about the stuff‚Äîmaximum dose, and so forth?‚Äù Sir Clinton inquired.

‚ÄúThe maximum dose of hyoscine hydrobromide is down in the books as six-tenths of a milligramme‚Äîabout a hundredth of a grain in apothecaries‚Äô weights.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen she must have swallowed ten or twelve times the maximum dose,‚Äù Sir Clinton calculated, after a moment or two of mental arithmetic.

He paused for a space, then turned again to Markfield.

‚ÄúI’d like to see the hyoscine in your store here, if you can lay your hands on it easily.‚Äù

Markfield made no objection.

‚ÄúIf you’d come in yesterday, the bottle would have been here, beside me. I’ve taken it back to the shelf now.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose you borrowed it to do a mixed melting-point?‚Äù Sir Clinton asked.

‚ÄúYes. When there’s only a trace of a stuff to identify, it’s the easiest method. But you seem to know something about chemistry?‚Äù

‚ÄúAbout enough to make mistakes with, I’m afraid. It simply happened that someone described the mixed melting-point business to me once; and it stuck in my mind. Now suppose we look at this store of yours.‚Äù

Markfield led them along a passage and threw open a door at the end.

‚ÄúIn here,‚Äù he said.

‚ÄúYou don’t keep it locked?‚Äù Sir Clinton inquired casually, as he passed in, followed by the Inspector.

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù Markfield answered in some surprise. ‚ÄúIt’s the general chemical store for this department. There’s no point in keeping it locked. All our stuffs are here, and it would be a devilish nuisance if one had to fish out a key every time one wanted some chloroform or benzene. We keep the duty-free alcohol locked up, of course. That’s necessary under the Customs‚Äô regulations.‚Äù

Sir Clinton readily agreed.

‚ÄúYou’re all trustworthy people, naturally,‚Äù he admitted, ‚ÄúIt’s not like a place where you have junior students about who might play thoughtless tricks.‚Äù

Markfield went over to one of the cases which lined the room, searched along a shelf, and took down a tiny bottle.

‚ÄúHere’s the stuff,‚Äù he explained, holding it out to the Chief Constable. ‚ÄúThat’s the hydrobromide, of course‚Äîa salt of the alkaloid itself. This is the compound that’s used in medicine.‚Äù

Now that he had got it, Sir Clinton seemed to have little interest in the substance. He handed it across to Flamborough who, after looking at it with would-be sagacity, returned it to Markfield.

‚ÄúThere’s just one other point that occurs to me,‚Äù the Chief Constable explained, as Markfield returned the poison-bottle to its original place. ‚ÄúHave you, by any chance, got an old notebook belonging to young Hassendean on the premises? Anything of the sort would do.‚Äù

The Inspector could make nothing of this demand and his face betrayed his perplexity as he considered it. Markfield thought for a few moments before replying, evidently trying to recall the existence of any article which would suit Sir Clinton’s purpose.

‚ÄúI think I’ve got a rough notebook of his somewhere in my room,‚Äù he said at last. ‚ÄúBut it’s only a record of weighings and things like that. Would it do?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe very thing,‚Äù Sir Clinton declared, gratefully. ‚ÄúI’d be much obliged if you could lay your hands on it for me now. I hope it isn’t troubling you too much.‚Äù

It was evident from Markfield’s expression that he was as much puzzled as the Inspector; and his curiosity seemed to quicken his steps on the way back to his room. After a few minutes‚Äô hunting, he unearthed the notebook of which he was in search and laid it on the table before Sir Clinton. Flamborough, familiar with young Hassendean’s writing, had no difficulty in seeing that the notes were in the dead man’s hand.

Sir Clinton turned over the leaves idly, examining an entry here and there. The last one seemed to satisfy him, and he put an end to his inspection. Flamborough bent over the table and was mystified to find only the following entry on the exposed leaf:

Weight of potash bulb = 50.7789 grs. Weight of potash bulb + CO‚ÇÇ = 50.9825 grs. ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ Weight of CO‚ÇÇ = 0.2046 grs.

‚ÄúBy the way,‚Äù said Sir Clinton casually, ‚Äúdo you happen to have one of your own notebooks at hand‚Äîsomething with the same sort of thing in them?‚Äù

Markfield, obviously puzzled, went over to a drawer and pulled out a notebook which he passed to the Chief Constable. Again Sir Clinton skimmed over the pages, apparently at random, and then left the second book open beside the first one. Flamborough, determined to miss nothing, examined the exposed page in Markfield’s notebook, and was rewarded by this:‚Äî

Weight of U-tube = 24.7792 gms. Weight of U-tube + H‚ÇÇO = 24.9047 gms. ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ Weight of H‚ÇÇO = 0.1255 gms.

‚ÄúDamned if I see what he’s driving at,‚Äù the Inspector said savagely to himself. ‚ÄúIt’s Greek to me.‚Äù

‚ÄúA careless young fellow,‚Äù the Chief Constable pronounced acidly. ‚ÄúMy eye caught three blunders in plain arithmetic as I glanced through these notes. There’s one on this page here,‚Äù he indicated the open book. ‚ÄúHe seems to have been a very slapdash sort of person.‚Äù

‚ÄúAn unreliable young hound!‚Äù was Markfield’s slightly intensified description. ‚ÄúIt was pure influence that kept him here for more than a week. Old Thornton, who put up most of the money for building this place, was interested in him‚Äîknew his father, I think‚Äîand so we had to keep the young pup here for fear of rasping old Thornton’s feelings. Otherwise. . . .‚Äù

The gesture accompanying the aposiopesis expressed Markfield’s idea of the fate which would at once have befallen young Hassendean had his protector’s influence been withdrawn.

The Chief Constable appeared enlightened by this fresh information.

‚ÄúI couldn’t imagine how you came to let him have the run of the place for so long,‚Äù he confessed. ‚ÄúBut, of course, as things were, it was evidently cheaper to keep him, even if he did no useful work. One can’t afford to alienate one’s benefactors.‚Äù

After a pause, he continued, reverting apparently to an earlier line of thought:

‚ÄúLet’s see. You made out that something like twelve times the normal dose of hyoscine had been administered?‚Äù

Markfield nodded his assent, but qualified it in words:

‚ÄúThat’s a rough figure, remember.‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course,‚Äù Sir Clinton agreed. ‚ÄúAs a matter of fact, the multiple I had in my mind was 15. I suppose it’s quite possible that some of the stuff escaped you and that your figure is an under-estimate?‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite likely,‚Äù Markfield admitted frankly. ‚ÄúI gave you the lowest figure, naturally‚Äîa figure I could swear to if it came to the point. As it’s a legal case, it’s safer to be under than over the mark. But quite probably, as you say, I didn’t manage to isolate all the stuff that was really present; and I wouldn’t deny that the quantity in the body may have run up to ten milligrammes or even slightly over it.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, it’s perhaps hardly worth bothering about,‚Äù the Chief Constable concluded. ‚ÄúThe main thing is that even at the lowest estimate she must have swallowed enough of the poison to kill her in a reasonably short time.‚Äù

With this he seemed satisfied, and after a few questions about the preparation and submission of Markfield’s official report, he took his leave. As he turned away, however, a fresh thought seemed to strike him.

‚ÄúBy the way, Dr. Markfield, do you know if Miss Hailsham’s here this morning?‚Äù

‚ÄúI believe so,‚Äù Markfield answered. ‚ÄúI saw her as I came in.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’d like to have a few words with her,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested.

‚ÄúOfficially?‚Äù Markfield demanded. ‚ÄúYou’re not going to worry the girl, are you? If it’s anything I can tell you about, I’d be only too glad, you know. It’s not very nice for a girl to have the tale going round that she’s been hauled in by the police in a murder case.‚Äù

The Chief Constable conceded the point without ado.

‚ÄúThen perhaps you could send for her and we could speak to her in here. It would be more private, and there need be no talk about it outside.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery well,‚Äù Markfield acquiesced at once. ‚ÄúI think that would be better. I’ll send for her now.‚Äù

He rang a bell and despatched a boy with a message. In a few minutes a tap on the door sounded, and Markfield ushered Norma Hailsham into the room. Inspector Flamborough glanced at her with interest, to see how far his conception of her personality agreed with the reality. She was a girl apparently between twenty and twenty-five, dressed with scrupulous neatness. Quite obviously, she spent money freely on her clothes and knew how to get value for what she spent. But as his eyes travelled up to her face, the Inspector received a more vivid impression. Her features were striking rather than handsome, and Flamborough noted especially the squarish chin and the long thin-lipped flexible mouth.

‚ÄúH’m!‚Äù he commented to himself. ‚ÄúShe might flash up in a moment, but with that jaw and those lips she wouldn’t cool down again in a hurry. I was right when I put her down as a vindictive type. Shouldn’t much care to have trouble with her myself.‚Äù

He glanced at Sir Clinton for tacit instructions, but apparently the Chief Constable proposed to take charge of the interview.

‚ÄúWould you sit down, Miss Hailsham,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested, drawing forward a chair for the girl.

Flamborough noticed with professional interest that by his apparently casual courtesy, the Chief Constable had unobtrusively man≈ìuvred the girl into a position in which her face was clearly illuminated by the light from the window.

‚ÄúThis is Inspector Flamborough,‚Äù Sir Clinton went on, with a gesture of introduction. ‚ÄúWe should like to ask you one or two questions about an awkward case we have in our hands‚Äîthe Hassendean business. I’m afraid it will be painful for you; but I’m sure you’ll give us what help you can.‚Äù

Norma Hailsham’s thin lips set in a hard line at his first words, but the movement was apparently involuntary, for she relaxed them again as Sir Clinton finished his remarks.

‚ÄúI shall be quite glad to give any help I can,‚Äù she said in a level voice.

Flamborough, studying her expression, noticed a swift shift of her glance from one to the other of the three men before her.

‚ÄúShe’s a bit over-selfconscious,‚Äù he judged privately. ‚ÄúBut she’s the regular look-monger type, anyhow; and quite likely she makes play with her eyes when she’s talking to any man.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed to be making a merit of frankness:

‚ÄúI really haven’t any definite questions I want to ask you, Miss Hailsham,‚Äù he confessed. ‚ÄúWhat we hoped was that you might have something to tell us which indirectly might throw some light on this affair. You see, we come into it without knowing anything about the people involved, and naturally any trifle may help us. Now if I’m not mistaken, you knew Mr. Hassendean fairly well?‚Äù

‚ÄúI was engaged to him at one time. He broke off the engagement for various reasons. That’s common knowledge, I believe.‚Äù

‚ÄúCould you give us any of the reasons? I don’t wish to pry, you understand; but I think it’s an important point.‚Äù

Miss Hailsham’s face showed that he had touched a sore place.

‚ÄúHe threw me over for another woman‚Äîbrutally.‚Äù

‚ÄúMrs. Silverdale?‚Äù Sir Clinton inquired.

‚ÄúYes, that creature.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh! Now I’d like to put a blunt question. Was your engagement, while it lasted, a happy one? I mean, of course, before he was attracted to Mrs. Silverdale.‚Äù

Norma Hailsham sat with knitted brows for a few moments before answering.

‚ÄúThat’s difficult to answer,‚Äù she pointed out at last. ‚ÄúI must confess that I always felt he was thinking more of himself than of me, and it was a disappointment. But, you see, I was very keen on him; and that made a difference, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat led to the breaking of your engagement?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou mean what led up to it? Well, we were having continual friction over Yvonne Silverdale. He was neglecting me and spending his time with her. Naturally, I spoke to him about it more than once. I wasn’t going to be slighted on account of that woman.‚Äù

There was no mistaking the under-current of animosity in the girl’s voice in the last sentence. Sir Clinton ignored it.

‚ÄúWhat were your ideas about the relations between Mr. Hassendean and Mrs. Silverdale?‚Äù

Miss Hailsham’s thin lips curled in undisguised contempt as she heard the question. She made a gesture as though averting herself from something distasteful.

‚ÄúIt’s hardly necessary to enter into that, is it?‚Äù she demanded. ‚ÄúYou can judge for yourself.‚Äù

But though she verbally evaded the point, the tone in which she spoke was sufficient to betray her private views on the subject. Then with intense bitterness mingled with a certain malicious joy, she added:

‚ÄúShe got what she deserved in the end. I don’t pretend I’m sorry. I think they were both well served.‚Äù

Then her temper, which hitherto she had kept under control, broke from restraint:

‚ÄúI don’t care who knows it! They deserved all they got, both of them. What business had she‚Äîwith a husband of her own‚Äîto come and lure him away? She made him break off his engagement to me simply to gratify her own vanity. You don’t expect me to shed tears over them after that? One can forgive a good deal, but there’s no use making a pretence in things like that. She hit me as hard as she could, and I’m glad she’s got her deserts. I warned him at the time that he wouldn’t come off so well as he thought; and he laughed in my face when I said it. Well, it’s my turn to laugh. The account’s even.‚Äù

And she actually did laugh, with a catch of hysteria in the laughter. It needed no great skill in psychology to see that wounded pride shared with disappointed passion in causing this outbreak.

Sir Clinton checked the hysteria before it gained complete hold over her.

‚ÄúI’m afraid you haven’t told us anything that was new to us, Miss Hailsham,‚Äù he said, frigidly. ‚ÄúThis melodramatic business gets us no further forward.‚Äù

The girl looked at him with hard eyes.

‚ÄúWhat help do you expect from me?‚Äù she demanded. ‚ÄúI’m not anxious to see him avenged‚Äîfar from it.‚Äù

Sir Clinton evidently realised that nothing was to be gained by pursuing that line of inquiry. Whether the girl had any suspicions or not, she certainly did not intend to supply information which might lead to the capture of the murderer. The Chief Constable waited until she had become calmer before putting his next question:

‚ÄúDo you happen to know anything about an alkaloid called hyoscine, Miss Hailsham?‚Äù

‚ÄúHyoscine?‚Äù she repeated. ‚ÄúYes, Avice Deepcar’s working on it just now. She’s been at it for some time under Dr. Silverdale’s direction.‚Äù

Flamborough, glancing surreptitiously at Markfield, noted an angry start which the chemist apparently could not suppress. Put on the alert by this, the Inspector reflected that Markfield himself must have had this piece of information, and had refrained from volunteering it.

‚ÄúI meant as regards its properties,‚Äù Sir Clinton interposed. ‚ÄúI’m not an expert in these things like you chemical people.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not an alkaloid expert,‚Äù Miss Hailsham objected. ‚ÄúAll I can remember about it is that it’s used in Twilight Sleep.‚Äù

‚ÄúI believe it is, now that you mention it,‚Äù Sir Clinton agreed, politely. ‚ÄúBy the way, have you a car, Miss Hailsham?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes. A Morris-Oxford four-seater.‚Äù

‚ÄúA saloon?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, a touring model. Why do you ask?‚Äù

‚ÄúSomeone’s been asking for information about a car which seems to have knocked a man over on the night of the last fog. You weren’t out that night, I suppose, Miss Hailsham?‚Äù

‚ÄúI was, as it happened. I went out to a dance. But I’d a sore throat; and the fog made it worse; so I came away very early and got home as best I could. But it wasn’t my car that knocked anyone down. I never had an accident in my life.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou might have been excused in that fog, I think, even if you had a collision. But evidently it’s not your car we’re after. What was the number of the car we heard about, Inspector?‚Äù

Flamborough consulted his notebook.

‚ÄúGX.9074, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúSay that again,‚Äù Markfield demanded, pricking up his ears.

‚ÄúGX.9074 was the number.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s the number of my car,‚Äù Markfield volunteered.

He thought for some time, apparently trying to retrace his experiences in the fog. At last his face lighted up.

‚ÄúOh, I guess I know what it is. When I was piloting Dr. Ringwood that night, a fellow nearly walked straight into my front mudguard. I may have hurt his feelings by what I said about his brains, but I swear I didn’t touch him with the car.‚Äù

‚ÄúNot our affair,‚Äù Sir Clinton hastened to assure him. ‚ÄúIt’s a matter for your insurance company if anything comes of it. And I gathered from Dr. Ringwood that you didn’t exactly break records in your trip across town, so I doubt if you need worry.‚Äù

‚ÄúI shan’t,‚Äù said Markfield, crossly. ‚ÄúYou can refer him to me if he comes to you again.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe’d nothing to do with the matter,‚Äù Sir Clinton pointed out. ‚ÄúHe was told he’d get the owner’s address from the County Council. I expect he got into a calmer frame of mind when he’d had time to think.‚Äù

He turned to Miss Hailsham, who seemed to have recovered complete control over herself during this interlude.

‚ÄúI think that’s all we need worry you with, Miss Hailsham. I’m sorry that we put you to so much trouble.‚Äù

As a sign that the interview was at an end, he moved over to open the door for her.

‚ÄúI certainly don’t wish you success,‚Äù she said icily, as she left the room.

‚ÄúWell, I think that’s all we have to do here, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton said as he turned back from the open door. ‚ÄúWe mustn’t take up any more of Dr. Markfield’s time. I don’t want to hurry you too much,‚Äù he added to Markfield, ‚Äúbut you’ll let us have your official report as soon as you can, won’t you?‚Äù

Markfield promised with a nod, and the two officials left the building. When they reached headquarters again, Sir Clinton led the way to his own office.

‚ÄúSit down for a moment or two, Inspector,‚Äù he invited. ‚ÄúYou may as well glance over the London man’s report when you’re about it. Here it is‚Äînot for actual use, of course, until we get the official version from him.‚Äù

He passed over a paper which Flamborough unfolded.

‚ÄúBy the way, sir,‚Äù the Inspector inquired before beginning to read, ‚Äúis there any reason for keeping back this information? These infernal reporters are all over me for details; and if this poison affair could be published without doing any harm, I might as well dole it out to them to keep them quiet. They haven’t had much from me in the last twenty-four hours, and it’s better to give them what we can.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed to attach some importance to this matter, for he considered it for a few seconds before replying.

‚ÄúLet them have the name of the stuff,‚Äù he directed at last. ‚ÄúI don’t think I’d supply them with any details, though. I’m quite satisfied about the name of the drug, but the dose is still more or less a matter of opinion, and we’d better not say anything about that.‚Äù

Flamborough glanced up from the report in his hand.

‚ÄúMarkfield and the London man both seem to put the dose round about the same figure‚Äîeight milligrammes,‚Äù he said.

‚ÄúBoth of them must be super-sharp workers,‚Äù Sir Clinton pointed out. ‚ÄúI don’t profess to be a chemist, Inspector, but I know enough about things to realise that they’ve done a bit of a feat there. However, let’s get on to something more immediately interesting. What did you make of the Hailsham girl?‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat did I make of her?‚Äù Flamborough repeated, in order to gain a little time. ‚ÄúI thought she was more or less what I’d expected her to be, sir. A hard vixen with a good opinion of herself‚Äîand simply mad with rage at being jilted: that’s what I made of her. Revengeful, too. And a bit vulgar, sir. No decent girl would talk like that about a dead man to a set of strangers.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe hadn’t much to tell us that was useful,‚Äù Sir Clinton said, keeping to the main point. ‚ÄúAnd I quite agree with you as to the general tone.‚Äù

Flamborough turned to a matter which had puzzled him during their visit to the Institute:

‚ÄúWhat did you want young Hassendean’s notebook for, sir? I didn’t quite make that out.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy, you saw what I got out of it: arithmetical errors which proved conclusively that he was a careless worker who didn’t take any trouble at all to verify his results.‚Äù

‚ÄúI had a kind of notion that you got more out of it than that, sir, or you wouldn’t have asked to see Markfield’s notebook as well. It doesn’t take someone else’s notebook to spot slips in a man’s arithmetic, surely.‚Äù

Sir Clinton gazed blandly at his subordinate:

‚ÄúNow that you’ve got that length, it would be a pity to spoil your pleasure in the rest of the inference. Just think it out and tell me the result, to see if we both reach the same conclusion independently. You’ll find a weights-and-measures conversion table useful.‚Äù

‚ÄúConversion table, sir?‚Äù asked the Inspector, evidently quite at sea.

‚ÄúYes. ‚ÄòOne metre equals 39¬∑37 inches,‚Äô and all that sort of thing. The sort of stuff one used at school, you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúToo deep for me, sir,‚Äù the Inspector acknowledged ruefully. ‚ÄúYou’ll need to tell me the answer. And that reminds me, what made you ask whether the dose could have been fifteen times the maximum?‚Äù

The Chief Constable was just about to take pity on his subordinate when the desk-telephone rang sharply. Sir Clinton picked up the receiver.

‚Äú. . . Yes. Inspector Flamborough is here.‚Äù

He handed the receiver across to the Inspector, who conducted a disjointed conversation with the person at the other end of the wire. At length Flamborough put down the instrument and turned to Sir Clinton with an expression of satisfaction on his face.

‚ÄúWe’re on to something, sir. That was Fossaway ringing up from Fountain Street. It seems a man called there a few minutes ago and began fishing round to know if there was any likelihood of a reward being offered in connection with the bungalow case. He seemed as if he might know something, and they handed him over to Detective-Sergeant Fossaway to see what he could make of him. Fossaway’s fairly satisfied that there’s something behind it, though he could extract nothing whatever from the fellow in the way of definite statements.‚Äù

‚ÄúHas Fossaway got him there still?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir. He’d no power to detain him, of course; and the fellow turned stubborn in the end and went off without saying anything definite.‚Äù

‚ÄúI hope they haven’t lost him.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, no, sir. They know him quite well.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat sort of person is he, then?‚Äù

‚ÄúA nasty type, sir. He keeps one of these little low-down shops where you can buy a lot of queer things. Once we nearly had him over the sale of some postcards, but he was too clever for us at the last moment. Then he was up in an assault case: he’d been wandering round the Park after dark, disturbing couples with a flash-lamp. A thoroughly low-down little creature. His name’s Whalley.‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s face showed very plainly his view of the activities of Mr. Whalley.

‚ÄúWell, so long as they can lay their hands on him any time we need him, it’s all right. I think we’ll persuade him to talk. By the way, was this lamp-flashing stunt of his done for √¶sthetic enjoyment, or was he doing a bit of blackmailing on the quiet?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, nobody actually lodged a complaint against him; but there’s no saying whether people paid him or not. His record doesn’t make it improbable that he might do something in that line, if he could manage to pull it off.‚Äù
