‚ÄúThen I’ll leave Mr. Whalley to your care, Inspector. He sounds interesting, if you can induce him to squeak.‚Äù

Chapter XI

The Code Advertisement

On the following morning, Inspector Flamborough was summoned to the Chief Constable’s room and, on his arrival, was somewhat surprised to find his superior poring over a copy of the Westerhaven Courier. It was not Sir Clinton’s habit to read newspapers during office hours; and the Inspector’s eyebrows lifted slightly at the unwonted spectacle.

‚ÄúHere’s a little puzzle for you, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton greeted him as he came in. ‚ÄúJust have a look at it.‚Äù

He folded the newspaper to a convenient size and handed it over, pointing as he did so to an advertisement to which attention had been drawn by a couple of crosses in pen and ink. Flamborough took the paper and scanned the advertisement:

DRIFFIELD. AAACC. CCCDE. EEEEF. HHHHH. IIIIJ. NNNNO. OOOOO. RRSSS. SSTTT. TTTTT. TTUUW. Y.

‚ÄúIt doesn’t seem exactly lucid, sir,‚Äù he confessed, as he read it a second time. ‚ÄúA lot of letters in alphabetical order and divided into groups of five‚Äîbar the single letter at the end. I suppose it was your name at the front that attracted your eye?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered. ‚ÄúThis copy of the paper came to me through the post, marked as you see it. It came in by the second delivery. Here’s the wrapper. It’ll probably suggest something to you.‚Äù

Flamborough looked at it carefully.

‚ÄúOrdinary official stamped wrapper. There’s no clue there, since you can buy ’em by the hundred anywhere.‚Äù

Then a glance at the address enlightened him.

‚ÄúSame old game, sir? Letters clipped from telegraph forms and gummed on to the wrapper. It looks like Mr. Justice again.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe chances are in favour of it,‚Äù Sir Clinton agreed, with a faint tinge of mockery in his voice at the Inspector’s eager recognition of the obvious. ‚ÄúWell, what about it?‚Äù

Flamborough scanned the advertisement once more, but no sign of comprehension lightened his face.

‚ÄúLet’s clear up one point before we tackle the lettering,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested. ‚ÄúThat’s to-day’s issue of the Courier; so this advertisement was received at the newspaper office yesterday. Since the thing reached me by the second post, this copy of the paper may have been bought in the normal way‚Äîfirst thing in the morning‚Äîand posted at once.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s sound, sir. It’s among the ordinary advertisements‚Äînot in the ‚ÄòToo Late For Classification‚Äô section.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt may be a hoax, of course,‚Äù Sir Clinton mused, ‚Äúbut the telegram-form business would hardly occur to a practical joker. I think one can take it as a genuine contribution until it’s proved to be a fake. Now what do you make of it?‚Äù

The Inspector shook his head.

‚ÄúCyphers are not my long suit, sir. Frankly, it seems to me just a jumble, and I don’t think I’d make it anything else if I tried.‚Äù

Sir Clinton reflected for a minute or two in silence, his eyes fixed on the advertisement.

‚ÄúI’ve a notion that this is only Chapter I, Inspector. There’s more to come, in all probability. If it’s Mr. Justice, he’s not the man to waste time. By the way, did you give the reporters the information you were talking about yesterday?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. It was printed in last night’s Evening Herald, and I think both the Courier and the Gazette have got it this morning.‚Äù

Sir Clinton was still scrutinising the advertisement.

‚ÄúI’m like you, Inspector‚Äîno great shakes on cyphers. But this affair looks to me more like the letters of a plain message arranged in ordinary alphabetical order. I think that most likely we shall get the key from the writer in some form or other before long. In the meantime, though, we might have a dash at interpreting the affair, if we can.‚Äù

Flamborough’s face showed that he thought very poorly of the chances of success.

‚ÄúEver read Jules Verne or Poe?‚Äù Sir Clinton demanded. ‚ÄúNo? Well, Poe has an essay on cryptography in its earlier stages‚Äînothing like the stuff you’ll find in Gross or Reiss, of course, and mere child’s play compared with the special manuals on the subject. But he pointed out that in cypher-solving you have to pick the lock instead of using the normal key. And Jules Verne puts his finger on the signature of a cypher-communication as a weak point, if you’ve any idea who the sender is. That’s assuming, of course, that there is a signature at all to the thing.‚Äù

The Inspector nodded his comprehension of this.

‚ÄúYou mean, sir, that ‚ÄòJustice‚Äô would be the signature here, like in the wire we got?‚Äù

‚ÄúWe can but try,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested. ‚ÄúNot that I’m over-hopeful. Still, it’s worth a shot. Suppose we hook out the letters of ‚ÄòJustice‚Äô and see what that leaves us. And we may as well disregard the groups of five for the moment and simply collect the remaining letters under A, B, C, etc.‚Äù

He tore a sheet of paper into small squares and inscribed one letter of the message on each square.

‚ÄúNow we take out ‚ÄòJustice,‚Äô‚Äù he said, suiting the action to the word, ‚Äúand simply leave the rest in alphabetical groups.‚Äù

The Inspector, following the operation, found himself faced with the arrangement:

AAA CCCC D EEEE F HHHHH III NNNN OOOOOO RR SSSS TTTTTTTTT U W Y JUSTICE.

‚ÄúIt doesn’t seem much clearer, sir,‚Äù Flamborough pointed out with a certain tinge of enjoyment in his tone. It was not often that he had a chance of crowing over his superior.

‚ÄúWait a moment, Inspector. Just let’s reflect for a bit. At any rate, the letters of ‚ÄòJustice‚Äô are there; and that’s always better than a complete blank end. Now consider what Mr. Justice might be burning to tell us about in his unobtrusive way. He had time to see the news printed in last night’s Herald before he composed this little affair. Let’s suppose that he got some fresh ideas from that‚Äîsince this communication falls pat after the publication and he hasn’t bothered us for days before that. The crucial thing was the identification of the hyoscine. We’ll see if we can get the word out here.‚Äù

He sifted out the letters rapidly; and the jumble then took the form:

HYOSCINE AAA CCC D EEE F HHHH II NNN OOOOO RR SSS TTTTTTTTT U W JUSTICE.

‚ÄúIt fits, so far,‚Äù Sir Clinton said, surveying his handiwork doubtfully, ‚Äúbut we might have got a couple of words like that out of a random jumble of fifty-six letters. It’s encouraging, but far from convincing, I admit.‚Äù

He glanced over the arrangement with knitted brows.

‚ÄúThere seem to be a devil of a lot of T’s in the thing, if we’re on the right track. Now what do you associate with hyoscine in your mind, Inspector? Quick, now! Don’t stop to think.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe Croft-Thornton Institute,‚Äù said the inspector, promptly.

‚ÄúBull’s eye, I believe,‚Äù the Chief Constable ejaculated. ‚ÄúYou could hardly jam more T’s together in English than there are in these three words. Let’s sift ’em out.‚Äù

The Inspector bent eagerly forward to see if the necessary letters could be found. Sir Clinton separated the ones which he required for the three words, and the arrangement stood thus:

HYOSCINE THE CROFT-THORNTON INSTITUTE AAA CC D E HH OO SS TT W JUSTICE.

‚ÄúI think this is getting outside the bounds of mere chance,‚Äù Sir Clinton adjudged, with more optimism in his tone. ‚ÄúNow we might go a step further without straining things, even if it’s only a short pace. Let’s make a guess. Suppose that it’s meant to read: ‚ÄúHyoscine at the Croft-Thornton Institute.‚Äù That leaves us with the jumble here:

AA CC D E HH OO SS T W

‚ÄúWhat do you make of that, Inspector?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe start of it looks like ACCEDE‚Äîno, there’s only one E,‚Äù Flamborough began, only to correct himself.

‚ÄúIt’s not ACCEDE, obviously, Let’s try ACCESS and see if that’s any use.‚Äù

The Chief Constable shifted the letters while the Inspector, now thoroughly interested, watched for the result.

‚ÄúIf it’s ACCESS then it ought to be ACCESS TO,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested. ‚ÄúAnd that leaves A, D, HH, O, W.‚Äù

One glance at the six letters satisfied him.

‚ÄúIt’s panned out correctly, Inspector. There isn’t a letter over. See!‚Äù

He rearranged the lettering, and the inspector read the complete message:

WHO HAD ACCESS TO HYOSCINE AT THE CROFT-THORNTON INSTITUTE. JUSTICE.

‚ÄúThe chances of an anagram working out so sensibly as that are pretty small,‚Äù Sir Clinton said, with satisfaction. ‚ÄúIt’s a few million to one that we’ve got the correct version. H’m! I don’t know that Mr. Justice has really given us much help this time, for the Croft-Thornton was an obvious source of the drug. Still, he’s doing his best, evidently; and he doesn’t mean to let us overlook even the obvious, this time. I’m prepared to bet that we get the key to this thing by the next post. Mr. Justice wouldn’t leave the matter to the mere chance of our working the thing out. Still it’s some satisfaction to feel that we’ve done without his assistance.‚Äù

Flamborough occupied himself with copying the cypher and its solution into his notebook. When he had finished, Sir Clinton lit a cigarette and handed his case to the Inspector.

‚ÄúLet’s put officialism aside for a few minutes,‚Äù the Chief Constable proposed. ‚ÄúNo notes, or anything of that sort. Now I don’t mind confessing, Inspector, that we aren’t getting on with this business at all well. Short of divination, there seems no way of discovering the truth, so far as present information goes. And we simply can’t afford to let this affair go unsolved. Your Whalley person seems to be our best hope.‚Äù

The Inspector evidently found a fresh train of thought started in his mind by Sir Clinton’s lament.

‚ÄúI’ve been thinking over that set of alternatives you put down on paper the other day, sir,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúI think they ought to be reduced from nine to six. It’s practically out of the question that young Hassendean was shot twice over by pure accident; so it seems reasonable enough to eliminate all that class from your table.‚Äù

He put his hand in his pocket and produced a sheet of paper which had evidently been folded and unfolded fairly often since it had been first written upon.

‚ÄúIf you reject accident as a possibility in Hassendean’s case,‚Äù he continued, ‚Äúthen you bring the thing within these limits here.‚Äù

He put his paper down on the table and Sir Clinton read the following:

Hassendean Mrs. Silverdale A‚ÄîSuicide ……. Suicide B‚ÄîMurder …….. Murder C‚ÄîSuicide ……. Accident D‚ÄîMurder …….. Accident E‚ÄîSuicide ……. Murder F‚ÄîMurder …….. Suicide

‚ÄúNow I think it’s possible to eliminate even further than that, sir, for this reason. There’s a third death‚Äîthe maid’s at Heatherfield‚Äîwhich on the face of it is connected in some way with these others. I don’t see how you can cut the Heatherfield business away from the other two.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m with you there, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton assured him.

Flamborough, obviously relieved to find that he was not going to be attacked in the flank, pursued his exposition with more confidence.

‚ÄúWho killed the maid? That’s an important point. It wasn’t young Hassendean, because the maid was seen alive by Dr. Ringwood immediately after young Hassendean had died on his hands. It certainly wasn’t Mrs. Silverdale, because everything points to her having died even before young Hassendean left the bungalow to go home and die at Ivy Lodge. Therefore, there was somebody afoot in the business that night who wouldn’t stick at murder to gain his ends, whatever they were.‚Äù

‚ÄúNobody’s going to quarrel with that, Inspector.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir,‚Äù Flamborough continued. ‚ÄúNow, with that factor at the back of one’s mind, one might review these six remaining cases in the light of what we do know.‚Äù

‚ÄúGo ahead,‚Äù Sir Clinton urged him, covertly amused to find the Inspector so completely converted to the method which at first he had decried.

‚ÄúCase A, then,‚Äù Flamborough began. ‚ÄúA double suicide. Now I don’t cotton much to that notion, for this reason. If it was suicide, then one or other of them must have had possession of hyoscine in quantity sufficient to kill both of them. So I judge from the quantity found in her body. Now no hyoscine was in young Hassendean’s system. His eyes were quite normal and there was no trace of the stuff in the stomach, as they found when they sent to your London friend on the question. From what I’ve seen of young Hassendean’s diary, and from what we’ve picked up about him from various sources, he wasn’t the sort of person to go in for needless pain. If he’d shot himself at all, it would have been in the head. And if he’d had hyoscine at hand, he wouldn’t have shot himself at all. He’d have swallowed a dose of the poison instead, and gone out painlessly.‚Äù

‚ÄúCorrect inference, I believe,‚Äù Sir Clinton confirmed. ‚ÄúI don’t say it’s certain, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, then, what holds in Case A, ought to hold also in the other two cases‚ÄîC and E‚Äîwhere it’s also a question of young Hassendean’s suicide. So one can score them off as well.‚Äù

‚ÄúNot so fast,‚Äù Sir Clinton interrupted. ‚ÄúI don’t say you’re wrong; but your assumption doesn’t cover the cases. In Case A you assumed that Mrs. Silverdale committed suicide‚Äîergo, she had hyoscine in her possession. But in Case C, the assumption is that she died by accidental poisoning; and before you can eliminate suicide on young Hassendean’s part, you’ve got to prove that he had the hyoscine in his possession. I’m not saying that he hadn’t. I’m merely keeping you strictly to your logic.‚Äù

Flamborough considered this for a few moments.

‚ÄúStrictly speaking, I suppose you’re right, sir. And in Case E, I’d have to prove that he poisoned her wilfully, in order to cover the case of his having hyoscine in his possession. H’m!‚Äù

After a pause, he took up the table afresh.

‚ÄúLet’s go back to Case B, then: a double murder. That brings in this third party‚Äîthe person who did for the maid at Heatherfield, we’ll say; and the fellow who broke the window. There were signs of a struggle in that room at the bungalow, you remember. Now it seems to me that Case B piles things on too thick, if you understand what I mean. It means that Mrs. Silverdale was murdered by poison and that young Hassendean was shot to death. Why the two methods when plain shooting would have been good enough in both cases? Take the obvious case‚Äîit’s been at the back of my mind, and I’m sure it’s been at the back of yours too, that Silverdale surprised the two of them at the bungalow and killed them both. Where does the poison come in? To my mind we ought to put a pencil through Case B. It’s most improbable.‚Äù

Rather to his relief, Sir Clinton made no objection. The Inspector drew his pencil through the first two lines of the table, then let it hover over the last line.

‚ÄúWhat about Case F, sir? She suicided and he was murdered. If she suicided, it was a premeditated affair‚Äîotherwise they wouldn’t have had the hyoscine at hand. But if it was one of these lovers‚Äô suicide-pacts, they’d have had a dose ready for him as well‚Äîand there wasn’t a trace of the stuff spilt on the floor or anywhere about the bungalow. Score out Case F, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ve no objections to your putting your pencil through it if you like, Inspector, though my reasons are rather different from the ones you give.‚Äù

Flamborough looked up suspiciously, but gathered from Sir Clinton’s face that there was nothing further to be expected.

‚ÄúWell, at least that’s narrowed down the possibilities a bit,‚Äù he said with relief. ‚ÄúYou started out with nine possible solutions to the affair‚Äîcovering every conceivable combination. Now we’re down to three.‚Äù

He picked up his paper and read out the residual scheme, putting fresh identifying letters to the three cases:

Hassendean Mrs. Silverdale X‚ÄîSuicide ……. Accident Y‚ÄîMurder …….. Accident Z‚ÄîSuicide ……. Murder

‚ÄúYou agree to that, sir?‚Äù Flamborough demanded.

‚ÄúOh, yes!‚Äù Sir Clinton admitted, in a careless tone. ‚ÄúI think the truth probably lies somewhere among those three solutions. The bother will be to prove it.‚Äù

At this moment a constable entered the room, bringing some letters and a newspaper in a postal wrapper.

‚ÄúCome by the next post, as I expected,‚Äù the Chief Constable remarked, picking up the packet and removing the wrapper with care. ‚ÄúThe usual method of addressing, you see: letters cut from telegraph forms and gummed on to the official stamped wrapper. Well, let’s have a look at the news.‚Äù

He unfolded the sheet and glanced over the advertisement pages in search of a marked paragraph.

‚ÄúIngenious devil, Inspector,‚Äù he went on. ‚ÄúThe other advertisement was in the Courier, this is a copy of to-day’s Gazette. That makes sure that no one reading down a column of advertisements would be struck by a resemblance and start comparisons. I begin to like Mr. Justice. He’s thorough, anyhow. . . . Ah, here we are! Marked like the other one. Listen, Inspector:

‚ÄúCLINTON: Take the letters in the following order. 55. 16. 30. 17. 1. 9. 2. 4. 5. 10. 38. 39. 43. 31. 18. 56. 32. 40. 6. 21. 26. 11. 3. 44. 45. 19. 12. 7. 36. 33. 15. 46. 47. 20. 34. 37. 27. 48. 35. 28. 22. 29. 41. 49. 23. 50. 53. 51. 13. 25. 54. 42. 52. 24. 8. 14.

Now that was why he split up his letters into groups of five in the first advertisement‚Äîto make it easy for us to count. I really like this fellow more and more. A most thoughtful cove.‚Äù

He placed the two advertisements side by side on the table.

‚ÄúJust run over this with me, Inspector. Call the first A number 1, the second A number 2, and so on. There are fifty-six letters in all, so number 55 is the W. Number 16 is the first letter in the fourth quintette‚ÄîH. Number 30 is the last letter in the sixth quintette‚ÄîO. So that spells WHO. Just go through the lot and check them please.‚Äù

Flamborough ploughed through the whole series and ended with the same solution as Sir Clinton had obtained earlier in the morning: ‚ÄúWHO HAD ACCESS TO HYOSCINE AT THE CROFT-THORNTON INSTITUTE?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, it’s pleasant to hit the mark,‚Äù the Chief Constable confessed. ‚ÄúBy the way, you had better send someone down to the Courier and Gazette Offices to pick up the originals of these advertisements. But I’m sure it’ll be just the same old telegram stunt; and the address which has to be given as a guarantee of good faith will be a fake one.‚Äù

Chapter XII

The Silverdale Wills

‚ÄúThis is Mr. Renard, sir.‚Äù

Flamborough held open the door of Sir Clinton’s office and ushered in the little Frenchman. The Chief Constable glanced up at the interruptors.

‚ÄúMrs. Silverdale’s brother, isn’t it?‚Äù he asked courteously.

Renard nodded vigorously, and turned toward the Inspector, as though leaving explanations to him. Flamborough threw himself into the breach:

‚ÄúIt appears, sir, that Mr. Renard isn’t entirely satisfied with the state of things he’s unearthed in the matter of his sister’s will. It’s taken him by surprise; and he came to see what I thought about it. He’d prefer to lay the point before you, so I’ve brought him along. It seems just as well that you should hear it at first-hand, for it looks as though it might be important.‚Äù

Sir Clinton closed his fountain pen and invited Renard to take a seat.

‚ÄúI’m at your disposal, Mr. Renard,‚Äù he said briskly. ‚ÄúLet’s hear the whole story, if you please, whatever it is. Inspector Flamborough will make notes, if you don’t mind.‚Äù

Renard took the chair which Sir Clinton indicated.

‚ÄúI shall be concise,‚Äù he assured the Chief Constable. ‚ÄúIt is not a very complicated affair, but I should like to have it thrashed out, as you English say.‚Äù

He settled himself at ease and then plunged into his tale.

‚ÄúMy sister, Yvonne Renard, as you know, married Mr. Silverdale in 1923. I was not altogether pleased with the alliance, not quite satisfied, you understand? Oh, there was nothing against Mr. Silverdale! But I knew my sister, and Silverdale was not the right man for her: he was too serious, too intent on his profession. He had not the natural gaiety which was needed in a husband for Yvonne. Already I was in doubt, at the very moment of the marriage. There were incompatibilities, you understand. . . . ?‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s gesture assured him that he had made himself sufficiently clear.

‚ÄúI have nothing to say against my brother-in-law, you follow me?‚Äù Renard went on. ‚ÄúIt was a case of ‚ÄòMarry in haste and repent at leisure,‚Äô as your English proverb says. They were unsuited to each other, but that was no fault of theirs. When they discovered each other‚Äîtheir real selves‚Äîit is clear that they decided to make the best of it. I had nothing to say. I was sorry that my sister had not found a husband more suited to her temperament; but I am not one who would make trouble by sympathising too much.‚Äù

‚ÄúI quite understand, Mr. Renard,‚Äù Sir Clinton intervened, with the obvious intention of cutting short this elaborate exposition of the self-evident.

‚ÄúNow I come to the important point,‚Äù Renard went on. ‚ÄúAt the time of the marriage, or shortly afterwards‚ÄîI do not know your English law about testaments very well‚Äîmy brother-in-law transferred part of his property in stocks and shares to my sister. It was some question of Death Duties, I was told. If he died first, then she would have had to pay on his whole estate; but by transferring some of his property to her, this could be avoided. In case of his death, she would have to pay only on what he had retained in his own name. It is, I understand, a usual precaution in the circumstances.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s often done,‚Äù Sir Clinton confirmed. ‚ÄúBy the way, Mr. Renard, can you give me some idea of how much he transferred to her on their marriage?‚Äù

‚ÄúI cannot give you the precise figures,‚Äù Renard explained. ‚ÄúI have seen the lawyer’s accounts, of course; but they were involved, and I have no good memory for figures. It was only a few hundred pounds‚Äîa mere drop in the bucket, as you would say in English. My brother-in-law is not a rich man, not by any means. But the sum itself is of little importance. It is the sequel which is of more interest, as you shall see.‚Äù

He leaned forward in his chair as though to fix Sir Clinton’s attention.

‚ÄúWhen my brother-in-law transferred this little property to my sister, they each made their testament. That, I believe, was on the advice of a lawyer. By his will, my brother-in-law left all his property to my sister. He had no relations, so far as I have learned; and that seemed very fair. The second will, my sister’s, was in identical terms, so far as the principal clauses went. All her property in stocks, shares, and money, went to my brother-in-law. There was a little provision at the end which left to me a few small souvenirs, things of sentimental value only. It seemed very fair in the circumstances. I suggest nothing wrong. How could there be anything wrong?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt seems a normal precaution in the circumstances,‚Äù Sir Clinton assured him. ‚ÄúNaturally, if she died first, he would expect to get his own property back again‚Äîless the Death Duties, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was a very small affair,‚Äù Renard emphasised. ‚ÄúIf I had been consulted, I should certainly have advised it. But I was not consulted. It was no business of mine, except that I was made a trustee. I am not one who mixes himself up with affairs which do not concern him.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhere is this leading to, Mr. Renard?‚Äù Sir Clinton asked patiently. ‚ÄúI don’t see your difficulty as yet, I must confess.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere is no difficulty. It is merely that I wish to lay some further information before you. Now, I proceed. My aunt had been ill for a long time. A disease of the heart, it was: angina pectoris. She was bound to die in a spasm, at a moment’s notice. One expected it, you understand? And less than three weeks ago, she had the spasm which we had so long anticipated, and she died.‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s face expressed his sympathy, but he made no attempt to interrupt.

‚ÄúAs I told Inspector Flamborough when I saw him last,‚Äù Renard continued, ‚Äúthe figure of her fortune came as a surprise to me. I had no idea she was so rich. She lived very simply, very parsimoniously, even. I had always thought of her as hard-up, you understand? Figure to yourself my astonishment when I learned that she had accumulated over ¬£12,000! That is a great sum. Many people would do almost anything to acquire ¬£12,000.‚Äù

He paused for a moment as though in rapt contemplation of the figures.

‚ÄúHer testament was very simple,‚Äù he proceeded. ‚ÄúMy sister Yvonne was her favourite. My aunt had always put her in front of me. I make no complaint, you understand? Someone must be preferred. I had a little bequest under my aunt’s testament; but Yvonne secured almost the whole of my aunt’s fortune. That was how things stood a fortnight ago.‚Äù

He hitched himself in his chair as though preparing for a revelation.

‚ÄúMy sister and I were the trustees under my aunt’s testament. The lawyer who had charge of the will communicated with me and forwarded a copy of the document. These legal documents are not easy to understand. But I soon saw that my sister had acquired the whole of my aunt’s capital in stocks and shares‚Äîabout a million and a half francs. I am not very good at legal affairs. It took me some time to understand what all this meant; but I thought it out. It is really quite simple, very easy. My sister had gained ¬£12,000 under my aunt’s will; but if she died without any change in the circumstances, then under the will which she signed after her marriage, my brother-in-law would inherit the whole of that money. Figure to yourself, he had never even seen my aunt, and all that ¬£12,000 would pour into his lap. And I, who had been almost like a son to my aunt, I would get nothing! I make no complaint, of course.‚Äù

Sir Clinton’s face betrayed nothing whatever of his views on the question. He merely waited in silence for Renard to continued his story.

‚ÄúWhen I understood the position,‚Äù Renard resumed, ‚ÄúI sat down and wrote a letter to my sister. ‚ÄòHere is the state of affairs,‚Äô I said. ‚ÄòOur good aunt is dead, and she has named you as her heiress. A whole million and a half francs! To me she has left some little things, enough at least to buy a suit of mourning. I have no complaints to make: our good aunt had the right to dispose of her money as she chose.‚Äô That was how I began, you understand? Then I went on thus: ‚ÄòThings are for the best for the present,‚Äô I said, ‚Äòbut one must think of the future as well. Recall the will which you made at the time of your marriage. All is to go to your husband, should anything happen to you. Now,‚Äô I wrote, ‚Äòthat seems to me hardly as it should be. If you should die‚Äîa motor accident might happen any day‚Äîthen all the money of our aunt would pass into the hands of your husband, this husband with whom you have so little in common and who had no relations with our good aunt. And I, who am your nearest in kin, would receive not one penny. Think of that,‚Äô I wrote, ‚Äòand consider whether it would be fair. Is the fortune of our family to pass into the hands of strangers and we ourselves to be left without a share in it?‚Äô‚Äù

Renard looked from the Inspector to Sir Clinton and back to the Inspector, as though seeking for sympathy. Apparently finding nothing very satisfying in their expressions, he continued his tale.

‚ÄúI put it to her that this state of affairs was not as it should be. I did not plead for myself, of course. That is not my way. I tried to show her that as things stood, injustice would be done if she should happen to die. And I urged her very strongly to make a fresh will. ‚ÄòSee,‚Äô I wrote, ‚Äòhow things would fall out. To you, it would mean nothing, very naturally. You would be far beyond all cares. But this money would be left. Would you desire that it should fall into the hands of this husband of yours, with whom you cannot find anything in common? Or would you not prefer that it should be left to your brother who has always been good to you?‚Äô That is how I put it to her. I asked her to take swift action and to call in a lawyer who could aid her to draw up a fresh will which would be fair to both her husband and myself. I desired to be fair, you understand? merely to be fair. He would have received back his own stocks and shares which he had given to her at the time of their marriage. I would have gained the fortune which descended from my aunt. That seemed reasonable, surely.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Sir Clinton confirmed, ‚Äúit sounds quite reasonable in the circumstances. And what happened?‚Äù

‚ÄúI have been to see the lawyers,‚Äù Renard went on. ‚ÄúFigure to yourself what I discovered. My poor Yvonne was not a woman of affairs. She had no business-like habits. If a thing seemed likely to give her trouble, she would put it aside for as long as she could, before dealing with it. Affairs bored her. It was her temperament, like that. So when she received my letter, she put it aside for some days. One cannot blame her. It was not in her nature to go to great trouble over a thing like that. Besides, death was not in her thoughts. One day was as good as another.‚Äù

He paused, as though wishing to heighten the interest of his narrative; for it was evident that he had produced but little impression on Sir Clinton.

‚ÄúShe had a good heart, my poor sister. She understood the position well enough, it seems. And she had no wish to see her good brother left out in the cold, as you English put it. But she delayed and delayed in the affair. And in the end she delayed too long.‚Äù

Again he hitched himself forward in his chair, as though he were approaching something important.

‚ÄúI went to the lawyers. What did I find? This. My poor Yvonne had not forgotten her good brother. She had the intention of setting things right. One day she rang up the lawyers on the telephone and made an appointment with them for the following afternoon. She informed them that she proposed to alter her will; but of course, over the telephone, she said nothing about her wishes on the point. That is to be understood. But she said she would jot down the points to be embodied in the new will and bring that paper with her. That is all the lawyers know. That is all I know myself. For before the next afternoon, when she had made her appointment with the lawyers‚Äîmy poor Yvonne was dead! Is it not distressing? Twelve thousand pounds! A million and a half francs! And they slip through my fingers just by a few hours. But I make no complaint, of course. I do not grumble. It is not my way. These things happen, and one has to bear them.‚Äù

If he had expected to read any sympathy in Sir Clinton’s face, he must have been disappointed. The Chief Constable betrayed nothing of the feelings in his mind.

‚ÄúWas it not most inopportune?‚Äù Renard continued. ‚ÄúOr most opportune indeed, for Silverdale, that things fell out as they have done. A coincidence, of course. Life is full of these things. I have seen too many to be astonished, myself. But is it not most apt that she should die just at that juncture? Another day of life, and the twelve thousand pounds goes into one pocket; a death, and the money falls into other hands. I am something of a philosopher. One has to be, in this world. And these strange chances have an attraction for my mind. I know there is nothing behind them, nothing whatever, you understand? And yet, is it not most striking that things fall out as they do?‚Äù

The Chief Constable declined to be drawn into a general discussion on the Universe.

‚ÄúI am afraid it is scarcely a matter for the police, Mr. Renard. Wills hardly fall into our province, you know, unless a case of forgery turns up; and in this case there’s nothing of that sort. The only advice I could give you would be to consult a lawyer, but as you’ve already had the legal position made clear, I don’t see that there’s anything to be done.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough took his cue and, without more ado he hinted to Renard very plainly that enough time had been spent on the matter. At length the little Frenchman withdrew, leaving the two officials together.

‚ÄúI don’t much care for his way of telling his story, sir,‚Äù Flamborough remarked, ‚Äúbut I’m not sure, if I were in his shoes, that I wouldn’t feel much the same as he seems to do. It must be a bit galling to lose ¬£12,000 by a few hours‚Äô delay. And he’s quite reasonably suspicious, evidently.‚Äù

Sir Clinton refused to be drawn.

‚ÄúDon’t let’s be too much influenced by the stop press news, Inspector. Renard’s evidence is the latest we have; but that adds nothing to its value, remember. Look at the case as a whole and try to reckon up the people who could conceivably gain anything by the crime. Then you can assess the probabilities in each case‚Äîapart altogether from the order in which the facts have come to light.‚Äù

The Inspector had evidently considered the matter already from this stand-point. He hardly paused before offering his views.

‚ÄúWell, sir, if you ask me, Silverdale had at least two sound motives for committing murder. By getting his wife out of the way, he opened the road to a marriage with the Deepcar girl, whom he’s obviously keen on. Also, if Renard’s story’s true, the death of his wife at that particular juncture put ¬£12,000 into his pocket, which he’d have lost if Mrs. Silverdale had lived a day or two longer.‚Äù

‚ÄúOne has to admit that he hadn’t evidence to get a divorce, which would have been an obvious alternative to murder,‚Äù Sir Clinton acknowledged. ‚ÄúAnd the cash affair makes the death of Mrs. Silverdale peculiarly opportune. It’s no use burking the plain fact that either money or a woman might tempt a man to murder; and when you’ve got both of them together, one can’t brush them aside cavalierly. But go on with your list, Inspector.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere’s that money-lender, Spratton,‚Äù Flamborough pursued. ‚ÄúIf young Hassendean’s death can be proved to be a murder, then Spratton lifts some thousands out of the pocket of the insurance company in return for the payment of a single premium. That’s a motive, certainly.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s a sound motive for proving that it was a case of murder and not suicide; and it’s a possible motive for murder, I admit. But the position of a gentleman who commits a murder for gain and can only collect the money by proving that murder was done . . . Well, it sounds a bit complicated, doesn’t it?‚Äù

‚ÄúUnless he can be sure of fixing the murder on someone else, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s a bit difficult in practice to produce a frame-up of that description, isn’t it?‚Äù

The Inspector refrained from betraying any opinion on this point.

‚ÄúThen there’s the Hailsham girl, sir. She’s a vindictive type; and she quite obviously had the worst kind of grudge against both of them. Revenge might have been at the back of the business for all one can tell. I don’t say it’s likely; but I’m considering possibilities, not necessarily probabilities.‚Äù

‚ÄúI don’t think Miss Hailsham can reckon me among her admirers,‚Äù Sir Clinton confessed. ‚ÄúBut that’s hardly evidence against her in a murder case. We’d need something a bit more concrete.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe admitted that she left the dance early that night and took her car home, sir. She hasn’t got a clean alibi for the time the murder was committed.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo I noticed when she told her story. But the absence of an alibi doesn’t establish murderous intent, you know. Go ahead.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, there’s the Deepcar girl. She’s keen on Silverdale. It’s always a motive.‚Äù

‚ÄúSave me from being mixed up in any murder case that you have charge of, Inspector. My character wouldn’t escape, I see. You’ll need to have something better than that before you start arresting anyone.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not talking about arresting anyone, sir,‚Äù the Inspector replied in an injured tone. ‚ÄúI’m just reviewing possible motives.‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite true. Can’t one make a feeble joke without rasping your susceptibilities? Now is that the end of your list?‚Äù

‚ÄúI think so, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh! You didn’t think of including someone with the initial ‚ÄòB,‚Äô then? You remember the ‚ÄòB‚Äô on the bracelet?‚Äù

The Inspector seemed rather startled.

‚ÄúYou mean this fellow B. might have been a discarded lover of Mrs. Silverdale’s who was out for revenge like the Hailsham girl? I hadn’t thought of that. It’s possible, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow let’s turn to a fresh side of the case,‚Äù Sir Clinton suggested. ‚ÄúOne thing’s certain; hyoscine played a part in the affair. What about Mr. Justice’s pertinent inquiry: ‚ÄòWho had access to hyoscine at the Croft-Thornton Institute?‚Äô‚Äù

‚ÄúEvery blessed soul in the place, so far as I could see,‚Äù the Inspector confessed, rather ruefully. ‚ÄúSilverdale, Markfield, young Hassendean, and the two girls: they all had equal chances of helping themselves from that bottle in the store. I don’t think that leads very far. That hyoscine was common property so far as access to it went. Anyone might have taken some.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen push the thing a little further. Out of all that list, who had an opportunity of administering hyoscine to Mrs. Silverdale‚Äîdirectly or indirectly‚Äîon the night she died?‚Äù

‚ÄúDirectly or indirectly?‚Äù Flamborough mused. ‚ÄúThere’s something in that perhaps. On the face of it, only three people could have administered the drug directly, since there were only three people at Heatherfield in a fit state to do it. I take it that she swallowed the stuff at Heatherfield, sir, because I found no trace of a paper which might have held it, either at the bungalow or on the bodies of young Hassendean and Mrs. Silverdale.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s sound, I believe,‚Äù Sir Clinton acquiesced. ‚ÄúShe swallowed the stuff at Heatherfield before going out. Now who are your three suspects?‚Äù

‚ÄúMrs. Silverdale herself might have taken it, sir, either on purpose or by mistake.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut she had no access to hyoscine that we know of.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, sir, but both Silverdale and young Hassendean had. She may have taken it in mistake for a headache powder or something of that sort. And it might have been added to a headache powder by either Silverdale or young Hassendean.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s a good enough suggestion, Inspector. But I didn’t see any sign of a powder paper in her room when I searched it; and you remember she came straight downstairs and went out of the house, according to the maid’s evidence. Any other view?‚Äù

‚ÄúThen it must have been administered in the coffee, sir, by either young Hassendean or the maid.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe maid? Where would she get hyoscine?‚Äù

‚ÄúFrom Silverdale, sir. It’s just occurred to me. Silverdale wanted a divorce; but he couldn’t get evidence because his wife was simply playing with young Hassendean and keeping well within the limits. But if she were drugged, then young Hassendean might seize the chance that was offered to him, and if Silverdale was prepared beforehand, he’d have his evidence at the cost of watching them for an hour or two.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo Silverdale gave the maid the drug to put in one of the cups of coffee and ordered her to give that cup to Mrs. Silverdale, you think?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s possible, sir. I don’t put it higher. That maid was a simple creature‚Äîlook how the doctor pumped her on the pretence of getting medical information that night. She was devoted to Silverdale; he told us that himself. She’d swallow any talk he chose to hand out to her. Suppose he faked up some yarn about Mrs. Silverdale needing a sedative but refusing to take it. The maid would believe that from Silverdale, and she’d put the hyoscine into the cup quite innocently. If the worst came to the worst, and the cups got mixed, then young Hassendean would get the dose instead.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s asking a bit too much, I’m afraid. Remember it was a heavy over-dose that was given.‚Äù

‚ÄúEverybody’s liable to make a mistake, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúTrue. And I suppose you’d say that after the murder at the bungalow Silverdale awoke to the fact that the maid’s evidence about the hyoscine would hang him, probably; so he went back and murdered her also.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was someone well known to her who did her in, sir. That’s clear enough.‚Äù

‚ÄúIn the meantime, you’ve left aside the possibility that young Hassendean may have administered the stuff. How does that strike you?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s possible, sir,‚Äù the Inspector admitted cautiously. ‚ÄúBut there’s no evidence for it.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, I shouldn’t like to go so far as that,‚Äù Sir Clinton said, chaffingly. ‚ÄúI’ll tell you what evidence there is on the point. There’s Hassendean’s own diary, first of all. Then there’s what we found in young Hassendean’s laboratory notebook.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut that was just some stuff about weighing potash-bulbs, whatever they may be.‚Äù

‚ÄúQuite correct. That was what it was.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, I’m no chemist, sir. It’s off my beat.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere’s no chemistry in it. I gave you the key to it at the time. Then there’s other evidence. Young Hassendean was a careless worker. Everyone agreed on that; and his notebook confirmed it. Next, there’s what Miss Hailsham said about hyoscine, which is more or less common knowledge, nowadays, of course. And there’s young Hassendean’s interference in the serving of coffee at Heatherfield, that night. Finally, there’s what the maid said about Mrs. Silverdale’s appearance when she was going out of the house. Put all these points together, and I’ll engage to satisfy a jury that young Hassendean administered the hyoscine to Mrs. Silverdale in her coffee, with a definite purpose‚Äîbut not murder‚Äîin view.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ll need to think over all that, sir. You seem pretty sure about it.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m practically certain. Now look at the business from another stand-point. Who had a grudge against the two victims, either separately or together?‚Äù

‚ÄúSilverdale, obviously.‚Äù

‚ÄúObviously, as you say. That’s if you take them together, of course. Now for a final problem. Who is Mr. Justice? He seems to be in the know, somehow. If we could lay hands on him, we might be near the centre of things. He knew before anyone else that something had happened at the bungalow. He knew about the hyoscine at the Institute‚Äîalthough as Silverdale’s a fairly well-recognised authority on alkaloids, that might have been just a shot aimed on chance. Anyhow, look at it as you choose, Mr. Justice has information, and he seems to have a motive. Who is he, can you guess?‚Äù

‚ÄúSomebody who won’t come out into the open until he’s dragged there, evidently. It might be an unwilling accomplice, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s possible. Anyone else?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt might be Spratton. He’s got an interest in establishing that it was a case of murder and not suicide.‚Äù

‚ÄúObviously true. Anyone else?‚Äù

‚ÄúI can’t think of anyone else who would fit the case, sir. By the way, I’ve got the originals of these advertisements‚Äîthe code ones. I sent down to the newspaper offices and got hold of them.‚Äù

He produced two sheets of paper from his pocket-book and handed them to the Chief Constable. Sir Clinton glanced over them.

‚ÄúH’m! The first one‚Äîthe letters‚Äîis built up as usual from telegram forms. The one with the numbers is fitted together from numbers printed in a newspaper; it might have been clipped from one of these lists of the results of drawings of bonds for redemption‚ÄîUnderground Electric Railways, and that kind of thing. These advertisements have columns and columns of figures out of which he’d be able to pick what he wanted easily enough. Now what about this address that he’s put down‚Äîthe usual guarantee of good faith at the bottom. It’s fictitious, of course?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. There’s no such place.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt’s in writing. It looks like a girl’s writing. This is a dangerous game for Mr. Justice; but I suppose if he’d put all the advertisement in clipped-out letters the newspaper people might have got suspicious and refused to print it. What about this handwriting, Inspector?‚Äù

Flamborough’s expression showed that he felt he had done his work thoroughly.

‚ÄúI managed to get hold of specimens of the writing of Miss Hailsham and Miss Deepcar. It isn’t either of them. Then I tried to get it recognised‚Äîand I succeeded, sir. Miss Hailsham recognised it at once. It’s Mrs. Silverdale’s own writing!‚Äù

‚ÄúA forgery, then? That’s very neat of Mr. Justice. I feel inclined to take off my hat to that fellow. He thinks of everything.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, it’s a blank end for us, so far as I can see, sir.‚Äù

Sir Clinton seemed to be so lost in admiration of Mr. Justice’s ingenuity that he failed to notice Flamborough’s dissatisfaction. When he spoke again, it was on a different topic.

‚ÄúWhat about your friend, Mr. Whalley, Inspector? It seems to me we ought to have him up and put him through it as quick as possible. Quite obviously he knows something.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ve tried to get hold of him, sir. But he’s left the town and I can’t get on his track. He’s gone off to some race-meeting or other, I expect. He often goes off like that and leaves no address. I’ll lay hands on him as soon as he comes back to Westerhaven.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe’s an essential witness, I suspect; so don’t let him slip through your fingers. You’d better ask for assistance from the local police in likely places.‚Äù

‚ÄúVery good, sir.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd now, Inspector, how are you getting along with the game of eliminations? How low have you brought the possibles out of the original nine solutions?‚Äù

Flamborough produced his often-unfolded scrap of paper and scanned it once more.

‚ÄúIf one accepts what you said a minute or two ago, sir, then the drugging of Mrs. Silverdale was meant to be plain drugging and wasn’t wilful murder. So the last case drops out.‚Äù

He put his pencil through the line of writing.

‚ÄúThat leaves only two alternatives:

Hassendean Mrs. Silverdale X‚ÄîSuicide ……. Accident Y‚ÄîMurder …….. Accident

And young Hassendean, from all accounts, was hardly the lad to suicide by shooting himself twice in the body‚Äîtoo painful for him. So it really looks rather like Case Y. Certainly it’s coming down to brass tacks quicker than I thought it would.‚Äù

Chapter XIII

The Murder of the Informer

As he entered Sir Clinton’s office on the following morning, Inspector Flamborough blurted out bad news without any preliminary beating about the bush.

‚ÄúThere’s been another murder, sir,‚Äù he announced, with a tinge of what seemed grievance in his tone.

Sir Clinton looked up from the mass of papers upon his desk.

‚ÄúWho is it, this time?‚Äù he demanded curtly.

‚ÄúIt’s that fellow Whalley, sir‚Äîthe man who seemed to have some information about the bungalow affair.‚Äù

The Chief Constable leaned back in his chair and gazed at Flamborough with an expressionless face.

‚ÄúThis is really growing into a wholesale trade,‚Äù he said, drily. ‚ÄúFour murders in quick succession, and we’ve nothing to show for it. We can’t go on waiting until all the population of Westerhaven, bar one individual, is exterminated; and then justify ourselves by arresting the sole survivor on suspicion. The public’s getting restive, Inspector. It wants to know what we do for our money, I gather.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough looked resentful.

‚ÄúThe public’ll have to lump it, if it doesn’t like it,‚Äù he said crudely. ‚ÄúI’ve done my best. If you think I ought to hand the thing over to someone else, sir, I’ll be only too glad to do so.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not criticising you, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton reassured him. ‚ÄúNot being a member of the public‚Äîfor this purpose, at least‚ÄîI know enough to appreciate your difficulties. There’s no burking the fact that whoever’s at the back of this affair is a sharper man than the usual clumsy murderer. He hasn’t left you much of a chance to pick up usable clues.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’ve followed up every one that he did leave,‚Äù Flamborough argued. ‚ÄúI don’t think I’ve been exactly idle. But I can’t arrest Silverdale merely because I picked up his cigarette-holder in suspicious surroundings. Confound the public! It doesn’t understand the difference between having a suspicion and being able to prove a case.‚Äù

‚ÄúLet’s hear the details of this latest affair,‚Äù Sir Clinton demanded, putting aside the other subject.

‚ÄúI’ve been trying to get hold of this fellow Whalley for the last day or two, sir, so as to follow up that line as soon as possible,‚Äù the Inspector began. ‚ÄúBut, as I told you, he’s been away from Westerhaven‚Äîhasn’t been seen anywhere in his usual haunts. I’ve made repeated inquiries at his lodgings, but could get no word of him except that he’d gone off. He’d left no word about coming back; but he obviously did mean to turn up again, for he left all his traps there and said nothing about giving up his bedroom.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou didn’t get on his track elsewhere?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, I hardly expected it. He’s a very average-looking man and one couldn’t expect people to pick him out of a crowd at a race-meeting by his appearance.‚Äù

Sir Clinton nodded as a permission to the Inspector to continue his narrative.

‚ÄúThis morning, shortly before seven o’clock,‚Äù Flamborough continued, ‚Äúthe driver of a milk-lorry on the Lizardbridge Road noticed something in the ditch by the roadside. It was about half an hour before sunrise, so I expect he still had his lamps alight. It’s pretty dark, these misty mornings. Anyhow, he saw something sticking up out of the ditch and he stopped his lorry. Then he made out that it was a hand and arm; so he got down from his seat and had a closer look. I expect he took it for a casual drunk sleeping things off quietly. However, when he got up to the side of the road, he found the body of a man in the ditch, face downward.

‚ÄúThis milkman was a sensible fellow, it seems. He felt the flesh where he could get at it without moving the body; and the coldness of it satisfied him that he’d got a deader on his hands. So instead of muddling about and trampling all over the neighbourhood, he very sensibly got aboard his lorry again and drove in towards town in search of a policeman. When he met one, he and the constable went back on the lorry to the dead man; and the constable stood on guard whilst the milkman set off with the lorry again to give the alarm.‚Äù

‚ÄúDid you go down yourself, by any chance, Inspector?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. The constable happened to recognise Whalley from what he could see of him‚ÄîI told you he was pretty well known to our men‚Äîand knowing that I’d been making inquiries about the fellow, they called me up, and I went down at once.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes?‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen I got there, sir,‚Äù the Inspector continued, ‚Äúit didn’t take long to see what was what. It was a case of the tourniquet again. Whalley had been strangled, just like the maid at Heatherfield. Quite obvious symptoms: face swollen and congested; tongue swollen, too; eyes wide open and injected a bit, with dilated pupils; some blood on the mouth and nostrils. And when I had a chance of looking for it, there was the mark of the tourniquet on his neck sure enough.‚Äù

Flamborough paused, as though to draw attention to his next point.

‚ÄúI hunted about in the ditch, of course. And there, lying quite openly, was the tourniquet itself. Quite a complicated affair this time; he’s evidently improved his technique.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, what about it?‚Äù Sir Clinton demanded rather testily, as though impatient of the Inspector’s comments.

‚ÄúHere it is, sir.‚Äù

Flamborough produced the lethal instrument with something of a flourish.

‚ÄúYou see, sir, it’s made out of a banjo-string threaded through a bit of rubber tubing. The handles are just bits of wood cut from a tree-branch, the same as before; but the banjo-string and the rubber tube are a vast improvement on the bit of twine he used last time, at Heatherfield. There’d be no chance of the banjo-string breaking under the strain; and the rubber tube would distribute the pressure and prevent the wire cutting into the flesh as it would have done if it had been used bare.‚Äù

Sir Clinton picked up the tourniquet and examined it with obvious interest.

‚ÄúH’m! I don’t say you’ve much to go on, but there’s certainly more here than there was in the other tourniquet. The banjo-string’s not much help, of course; one can buy ’em in any musical-instrument shop. But the rubber tubing might suggest something to you.‚Äù

Inspector Flamborough scrutinised it afresh.

‚ÄúIt’s very thick-walled, sir, with a much smaller bore than one would expect from the outside diameter.‚Äù

Sir Clinton nodded.

‚ÄúIt’s what they call ‚Äòpressure-tubing‚Äô in a chemical laboratory. It’s used when you’re pumping out vessels or working under reduced pressures generally. That’s why it’s made so thick-walled: so that it won’t collapse flat under the outside air-pressure when you’ve pumped all the gas out of the channel in the middle.‚Äù

‚ÄúI see,‚Äù said the Inspector, fingering the tubing thoughtfully. ‚ÄúSo it’s the sort of thing one finds in a scientific place like the Croft-Thornton Institute?‚Äù

‚ÄúAlmost certainly,‚Äù Sir Clinton agreed. ‚ÄúBut don’t get too sure about your rubber tubing. Suppose someone is trying to throw suspicion on one of the Croft-Thornton staff, wouldn’t this be an excellent way of doing it? One can buy pressure-tubing in the open market. It’s not found exclusively in scientific institutes, you know.‚Äù

Flamborough seemed a shade crestfallen at the loss of what he had evidently regarded as a promising line.

‚ÄúOh, indeed?‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI suppose you’re right, sir. Still it’s a bit uncommon, isn’t it?‚Äù

‚ÄúNot what you’d expect the ordinary criminal to hit on straight off, I suppose you mean? But this fellow isn’t an ordinary criminal. He’s got plenty of brains. Now doesn’t it strike you as strange that he should go to the trouble of leaving this tourniquet for your inspection? He could have slipped it into his pocket easily enough and it wouldn’t have bulged much.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, a glance at the body would show anyone that something of the sort had been used. He wasn’t giving much away by leaving the thing itself, was he?‚Äù

Sir Clinton did not seem altogether satisfied with the Inspector’s view.

‚ÄúThe less a murderer leaves behind, the more difficult it is to catch him, Inspector. That’s a truism. Now this fellow is no fool, as I’ve frequently remarked to you. Hence one might have anticipated that he’d leave as few traces as possible. But here he presents us with the actual weapon, and a weapon that has fairly salient peculiarities of its own. Queer, isn’t it?‚Äù

‚ÄúThen you think it’s a non-scientific murderer using scientific appliances so as to suggest that the crime was done by someone in the scientific line‚ÄîSilverdale, I mean?‚Äù

Sir Clinton was silent for a moment or two, then he said thoughtfully:

‚ÄúWhat I’m not sure about is whether it’s a pure bluff or a double bluff. It looks like one or the other.‚Äù

The Inspector obviously had difficulty in interpreting this rather cryptic utterance. At last he saw his way through it.

‚ÄúI think I see what you mean, sir. Suppose it’s not Silverdale that did the murder. Then somebody‚Äîknowing that this kind of tubing’s common in Silverdale’s laboratory‚Äîmay have left it on purpose for us to find, so that we’d be bluffed into jumping to the conclusion‚Äîas I admit I did‚Äîthat Silverdale did the trick. That would be a simple bluff. Or again, supposing it’s Silverdale who’s the murderer, then he may have left the tubing on purpose, because he’d say to himself that we’d never believe that he’d be such a fool as to chuck a thing like that down beside the body‚Äîand hence we’d pass him over in our suspicions. Is that it, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt sounds devilish involved, as you put it, Inspector; but I have a sort of dim perception that you’ve grasped my meaning,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered. ‚ÄúMy own impression is simply that we musn’t let this tourniquet lead us too far, for fear we go completely astray. If we get on the right track, I’ve no doubt it’ll fit neatly enough to the rest of the evidence; but it’s not the sort of thing I’d care about staking a lot on by itself. Now suppose we come out of these flowery by-paths and get back to the main thoroughfare of the facts.‚Äù

The Inspector refused to be damped by his superior. Indeed, he had the air of a player holding good cards, and not caring who knew it.

‚ÄúIt was hard frost last night, sir, as you‚Äôll remember; so there were no foot-prints on the road, or anything of that sort. But the grass by the side of the ditch is fairly long; and when I examined it, it was clear enough that there hadn’t been any struggle on it. They may have struggled on the road, of course; but the grass was quite undisturbed.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen the body hadn’t been dragged off the road into the ditch? It must have been lifted and pitched in?‚Äù

‚ÄúSo I think, sir. The grass border between road and ditch is quite narrow‚Äîjust room to stand on it comfortably. One could hoist a body over it without too much trouble.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd from the look of the body you think it had been thrown in?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. It was huddled up anyhow in the ditch, just as it might have fallen if it had been dropped in with a thud.‚Äù

‚ÄúSingle-handed business, then, you believe?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, I think if two people had been handling him‚Äîone taking his shoulders and another taking his feet‚Äîhe’d have fallen more tidily. He certainly looked as if he’s been bundled in anyhow. I’d put it down as a single-handed job from the look of it.‚Äù

‚ÄúI suppose you examined the pockets, and so forth?‚Äù Sir Clinton asked.

‚ÄúOf course, sir. But there was nothing in them of any use to us.‚Äù

The Inspector’s voice betrayed that he had something still in reserve. Now he brought it forward.

‚ÄúI examined his hands, sir; and in the right one, I found something important. The hand was clenched, and when I got it open at last, this fell out.‚Äù

He produced a button with a shred of cloth attached to it, which he laid on the desk before Sir Clinton. The Chief Constable picked it up, examined it closely, and then, pulling out a pocket magnifying glass, made a still more minute inspection.

‚ÄúVery interesting, Inspector. What do you make of it?‚Äù

‚ÄúObviously it was torn off the murderer’s clothes during the struggle, sir. And I’ve seen something like it before. You see that canary-coloured stain on the bit of cloth and also on the threads that hold the button to the fabric?‚Äù

‚ÄúDyed with picric acid, by the look of it, I should say. Is that what you mean?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, sir. And the pattern of the cloth’s another point.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou mean it looks like a button torn off the old jacket that Silverdale was wearing, that day we saw him at the Croft-Thornton Institute‚Äîhis laboratory coat?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat’s undoubtedly what it is, sir. I remember that stain perfectly. And as soon as I saw it, I remembered the pattern of the cloth.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd your view is?‚Äù

‚ÄúI think that when Silverdale set out to murder Whalley he was afraid that some blood from the face might get on to his coat. So he put on his old laboratory jacket. If it got spotted, he could destroy it and rouse no suspicions. It was only an old coat that he might think was worn out. Quite a different thing from destroying some of his ordinary clothes. That would have been suspicious. But an old coat‚Äîno one would wonder if he got rid of it and brought another one down to the laboratory to replace it.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt sounds deuced plausible, Inspector, I must admit. But‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

‚ÄúBut what, sir?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell,‚Äù Sir Clinton answered thoughtfully, ‚Äúit leaves us again with the choice between the single and the double bluff, you see, even if one goes no further with one’s inquiries.‚Äù

The Inspector pondered over the point for a few seconds, but at the end of his cogitation he seemed unimpressed. Apparently, however, he thought it wise to change the subject.

‚ÄúIn any case, sir, I think Whalley’s part in the bungalow affair is pretty plain now. I told you he was the sort of fellow who was out for easy money, no matter how dirty it might be. By the way, he was the man who inquired about the number of that motor which he said knocked him spinning‚Äîan obvious try-on to get damages, although he wasn’t hurt at all. You can see he’d do anything to make money and save himself from honest work. If you remember that, it’s easy enough to see the part he played at the bungalow. He was the person you christened Peeping Tom.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnything further about him that you can think of, Inspector? I don’t say you’re wrong, of course.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, sir, if Silverdale expected to take his wife in flagrante delicto, he’d need an independent witness, wouldn’t he? Possibly Whalley was the man he picked out for the work.‚Äù

‚ÄúDo you think he was the sort of witness that was wanted? I’m not so sure of his suitability myself.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt wasn’t exactly a nice job, sir,‚Äù the Inspector pointed out. ‚ÄúSilverdale would hardly care to take one of his close friends to inspect an affair of that sort. And of course a woman‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

He broke off suddenly, as though struck by a fresh idea. Sir Clinton ignored the last phrase of the Inspector.

‚ÄúAssume that Whalley was the witness, then, what next?‚Äù

‚ÄúAssume that Silverdale posted Whalley at the second window and went round to the first one‚Äîat the front. Then, to make the thing complete, he breaks in through the window and jumps into the room. Young Hassendean has his pistol and mistakes the state of affairs‚Äîthinks that Silverdale means to thrash him or worse. He pulls out his pistol and there’s a struggle for the possession of it. The pistol goes off accidentally, and the bullet hits Mrs. Silverdale in the head by pure chance. Then the struggle goes on, and in the course of it, young Hassendean gets shot twice over in the lung.‚Äù

The Chief Constable looked at his subordinate with quite unaffected respect.

‚ÄúIt looks as if you’d come very near the truth there,‚Äù he admitted. ‚ÄúGo on.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe rest’s fairly obvious, if you grant what’s gone before. Whalley’s seen the whole affair from his post at the window. He sneaks off into the dark and gets out of Silverdale’s reach. If he hadn’t, then Silverdale would probably have shot him at sight to destroy the chance of evidence against him. But when Whalley has time to think things over, he sees he’s got a gold-mine in the business. If he can blackmail Silverdale, he’s got a steady income for life. But I expect he weakened and tried to play for safety. He blackmailed Silverdale; then he came to us, so that he could say he’d been to the police, meaning to give information. Then he went back to Silverdale, and in some way he let out that he’d given us a call. That would be enough for Silverdale. Whalley would have to go the way the maid went. And so he did.‚Äù

Sir Clinton had listened intently to the Inspector’s reconstruction of the episode.

‚ÄúThat’s very neat indeed, Inspector,‚Äù he adjudged at the close. ‚ÄúIt’s quite sound, so far as it goes, and so far as one can see. But, of course, it leaves one or two points untouched. Where does the murder of the maid come into the business?‚Äù

Flamborough reflected for a moment or two before answering.

‚ÄúI’m not prepared to fill that gap just at this moment, sir. But I’ll suggest something. Renard told us that Mrs. Silverdale was going to draw up a note of the terms of her new will. It’s on the cards that Silverdale knew about that‚Äîshe may have mentioned it to him. He’d want to get that note and destroy it at any cost, before there was any search of his house or any hunting through Mrs. Silverdale’s possessions.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe might have thought it worth while, I admit. But I’d hardly think it important enough to lead to an unnecessary murder. Besides, it wasn’t necessary for Silverdale to murder the maid at all. It was his own house. He could search where he chose in it and nobody could object. The maid wouldn’t see anything strange in that.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was pretty clear that the maid knew her murderer, anyhow,‚Äù the Inspector pointed out. ‚ÄúEverything points to that. I admit I’m only making a guess, sir. I can’t bring any evidence against Silverdale on that count yet. For all one can tell, she may have seen something‚Äîblood on his coat from the shots, or something of that sort. Then he’d have to silence her.‚Äù

Sir Clinton made no comment on the Inspector’s suggestion. Instead, he turned to a fresh aspect of the case.

‚ÄúAnd where does Mr. Justice come into your theory of the affair? He wasn’t your friend Whalley. That’s evident.‚Äù

The Inspector rubbed his nose thoughtfully, as though trying to gain inspiration from the friction.

‚ÄúIt’s a fact, sir, that I can’t fit Mr. Justice into my theory at present. He wasn’t Whalley, and that’s a fact. But hold on a moment! Suppose that Whalley wasn’t Silverdale’s witness at all. Come to think of it, Whalley was hardly the sort that one would pick out for the job, if one had been in Silverdale’s shoes.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m quite convinced of that, at any rate, Inspector. You needn’t waste breath in persuading me.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, but there’s another possibility that’s been overlooked, sir,‚Äù Flamborough interrupted eagerly. ‚ÄúI’ve been assuming all along that Silverdale was the only person at the opened window. But suppose he’d brought someone along with him. Both of them might have been looking through the front window, whilst Whalley was at the side window, quite unknown to them at the time.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow you’re getting positively brilliant, Inspector,‚Äù Sir Clinton commended. ‚ÄúI think you’ve got at least half the truth there, beyond a doubt.‚Äù

‚ÄúWho could Silverdale’s witness have been?‚Äù the Inspector pursued, as if impatient of the interruption. ‚ÄúWhat about the Deepcar girl?‚Äù

‚ÄúThink again,‚Äù Sir Clinton advised him drily. ‚ÄúDo you really suppose that Silverdale‚Äîwho seems in love with the girl‚Äîwould have picked her out for business of that sort? It’s incredible, Inspector.‚Äù

The first flush of enthusiasm at his discovery passed from Flamborough’s thoughts at the tone of the Chief Constable’s voice.

‚ÄúI suppose you’re right, sir,‚Äù he had to admit. ‚ÄúBut there’s another girl who’d have enjoyed the job‚Äîand that’s the Hailsham girl. She’d have given a good deal just for the pleasure of seeing those two humiliated. She’d have gloated over the chance of giving that particular evidence in court and squaring accounts with young Hassendean and Mrs. Silverdale. It would have been all jam to her, sir. You can’t deny that.‚Äù

Sir Clinton conceded the point without ado.

‚ÄúI won’t deny it,‚Äù he said curtly. ‚ÄúBut you needn’t let your mind run exclusively on the female population of Westerhaven in a matter of this sort. A man would be a much more convenient witness for Silverdale to take with him. Why leave Silverdale’s male friends out of account?‚Äù

‚ÄúIf you’re thinking of Markfield, sir, we’ll not get much out of him, I’m afraid,‚Äù Flamborough pronounced. ‚ÄúSo far, except when he couldn’t help it, he’s done his level best to refuse any information about Silverdale and his doings‚Äîif he hasn’t actually served out misleading statements to us. I don’t much care for Dr. Markfield’s way of going about things.‚Äù

Sir Clinton crossed the room and took down his hat from its peg.

‚ÄúWell, let’s sample his methods once more, Inspector. We’ll go round now to the Croft-Thornton and look into the question of the jacket. You can bear the burden of the interview, if you like; but I should prefer to hear what goes on. And you might press Silverdale a little more sharply about his doings on the night of the bungalow affair. We may as well give him a chance of second thoughts, though really I don’t expect anything from him at this stage.‚Äù

Chapter XIV

The Jacket

Sir Clinton and the Inspector found Markfield at work in his laboratory when they reached the Croft-Thornton Institute. Flamborough wasted no time in preliminaries, but plunged at once into the business which had brought him there.

‚ÄúWhat do you make of that, Dr. Markfield?‚Äù he demanded, producing the shred of cloth with the button attached and showing them to the chemist.

Markfield examined the object carefully, but his face showed only a certain bewilderment when he looked up at the Inspector again.

‚ÄúIt seems to be a button and a bit of cloth with a picric acid stain on it,‚Äù he pointed out with a tinge of irony. ‚ÄúDo you want me to make an expert examination of it? If so, you’d better tell me some more about it, so that I’ll know what you want with it.‚Äù

Flamborough stared at him for a moment or two, as though trying to read something in his expression, but Markfield seemed in no way put out.

‚ÄúI’m not a mind-reader, Inspector,‚Äù he pointed out. ‚ÄúYou’ll need to explain clearly what you expect me to do; and I’ll have to be told whether I can cut bits out of your specimen for chemical analysis.‚Äù

Flamborough saw that his attempt to draw Markfield was not going to be so easy as he had hoped.

‚ÄúHave a good look at the thing first of all,‚Äù he suggested. ‚ÄúCan you remember anything like it?‚Äù

Markfield stolidly examined the object once more.

‚ÄúIt’s a button and a piece of cloth,‚Äù he said at last. ‚ÄúOf course I’ve seen buttons before, and bits of cloth are not uncommon. I should think that this stain is a picric acid one, but that’s a matter for further examination before I could say anything definite. Is that what you wanted?‚Äù

Flamborough kept his temper with difficulty.

‚ÄúWhat I want to know, Dr. Markfield, is whether you have recently seen anything that you could associate with that thing‚Äîany garment from which it might have been torn, or anything of that sort.‚Äù

Markfield’s eyes narrowed and he glanced with obvious unfriendliness at the Inspector.

‚ÄúIt’s a coat-button, by the look of it. I’m no specialist in buttons, I admit. It might have come off any lounge suit, so far as I can see.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’d advise you not to fence with us too long, Dr. Markfield,‚Äù Flamborough suggested. ‚ÄúLook at the cloth. Does that remind you of anything that’s familiar to you?‚Äù

Markfield’s face betrayed his obvious annoyance.

‚ÄúI suppose you’ve identified it already for yourselves. Why come to me? Presumably you mean that it’s a bit torn off Dr. Silverdale’s laboratory coat. Well, I can’t swear to that. It may be, for all I know. Why not compare it with the coat, and if the coat’s torn, you’ve got your evidence, whatever it may be. I don’t see why you drag me into the thing at all.‚Äù

Flamborough’s voice grew hard as he answered:

‚ÄúThere’s one thing I want you to bear in mind, Dr. Markfield. A man may very easily become an accessory after the fact in a murder case; and the penalty runs as high as penal servitude for life. I’m not at all satisfied with the way in which you seem to have determined to evade some of the questions I’ve had to put to you; and I’d like to remind you that you may be running risks. It would be far better if you’d deal frankly with us instead of shuffling.‚Äù

The covert threat seemed to have its effect on Markfield. He looked sulky, but he appeared to make up his mind to alter his tactics.

‚ÄúWell, ask your questions, then,‚Äù he snapped. ‚ÄúBut put them on matters of fact. I’m not going to say what I think about this and what I suppose about that. I’ll tell you anything that I know definitely, if you ask about it.‚Äù

Flamborough wasted no time before taking up the challenge.

‚ÄúVery good, Dr. Markfield. We’ll stick to facts, if you like. Now once upon a time you saw Dr. Silverdale acting in some private theatricals, I believe. I learned that from Dr. Ringwood. That’s correct, isn’t it?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes. We were members of a small amateur show at one time.‚Äù

‚ÄúIn any of his parts, did Dr. Silverdale play the banjo?‚Äù

Markfield reflected for a moment.

‚ÄúI think he did.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe’s an expert banjo-player?‚Äù

‚ÄúHe plays the banjo,‚Äù Markfield corrected. ‚ÄúI’m not going to give you my opinion about his playing. That’s not a question of fact; it’s a mere matter of taste.‚Äù

Flamborough let this pass without comment.

‚ÄúHe plays the banjo, anyhow. That’s what I want to get at.‚Äù

He stepped across the laboratory to where a little glass apparatus was attached to a tap at a sink and examined the rubber tubing attached.

‚ÄúWhat’s this thing here?‚Äù he demanded.

‚ÄúA water-pump,‚Äù Markfield answered, as though not quite following the Inspector’s train of thought.

‚ÄúAnd this rubber tubing, what sort of stuff is it?‚Äù

‚ÄúPressure-tubing. What about it?‚Äù

‚ÄúDoes Dr. Silverdale use anything of that sort?‚Äù

‚ÄúEverybody in the place uses it. Whenever one wants quick filtering one uses a water-pump with pressure-tubing connections.‚Äù

‚ÄúMiss Deepcar and Miss Hailsham use it, then?‚Äù

‚ÄúI should think there are a dozen or two of these pumps in this department alone. They’re ordinary fittings in every chemical laboratory. If I may ask, Inspector, what are you getting at?‚Äù

Flamborough switched off to a fresh line without making any direct reply.

‚ÄúIs Miss Deepcar here to-day?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don’t think so. I believe she’s out of town‚Äîbeen away for a couple of days. I’ll send a message to find out definitely if you want to know.‚Äù

Flamborough shook his head.

‚ÄúDon’t trouble. I can find out for myself.‚Äù

‚ÄúI heard that she would be back the day after to-morrow,‚Äù Markfield volunteered. ‚ÄúBut you’d better find out for yourself of course.‚Äù

Again the Inspector turned to a fresh line.

‚ÄúDo you know anything about a man Whalley‚ÄîPeter Whalley?‚Äù he demanded.

‚ÄúWhalley?‚Äù Markfield repeated as though trying to recall the name. ‚ÄúWhalley? Oh, yes. He came to me with some story about having been hit by my car on a foggy night. I didn’t believe him. I knew I’d hurt no one with the car, though once I came near it that night. Mr. Whalley got no change out of me.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe didn’t go any further in the matter, then?‚Äù

‚ÄúI heard no more about it. The thing was so obviously a try-on that I didn’t even advise my insurance company about it.‚Äù

Flamborough reflected for a few moments, obviously trying to think of fresh questions which he could put; but apparently he had come to the end of his stock.

‚ÄúWe’ll go along to Dr. Silverdale’s room,‚Äù he said, leading the way to the door. ‚ÄúYou had better come with us, Dr. Markfield. You’ll do as a witness, perhaps.‚Äù

‚ÄúI’m not very keen,‚Äù Markfield retorted grumblingly.

However, he followed Sir Clinton and the Inspector along the corridors to Silverdale’s laboratory. The room was empty, but the door was unlocked and the Inspector opened it and stepped inside. A glance round the place revealed Silverdale’s laboratory jacket hanging on a peg; and Flamborough went over and took it down.

‚ÄúNow we’ll see,‚Äù he said, laying it on the table and spreading it out for examination. ‚ÄúAh, I thought there was no mistake.‚Äù

He pointed to the right-hand side, where it was obvious that one of the buttons had been wrenched away, taking a piece of the cloth with it.

‚ÄúNow we’ll see if it fits,‚Äù Flamborough continued, producing the fragment of fabric found in Whalley’s hand and adjusting it to the tear in the coat. ‚ÄúThat’s clear enough. You see now the stains on the two bits correspond exactly.‚Äù

Markfield leaned over and satisfied himself that the Inspector’s statement was accurate.

‚ÄúWhat is this bit of cloth?‚Äù he asked.

Flamborough, however, had found something further, and Markfield got no answer to his question.

‚ÄúLook there,‚Äù the Inspector ejaculated, indicating a a small brownish stain on the breast of the jacket. ‚ÄúThat’s blood, clear enough.‚Äù

Markfield seemed about to repeat his demand for information when steps sounded in the corridor outside. Flamborough picked up the coat, moved swiftly across the room, and hung the garment on its original peg. As he turned away unconcernedly from the spot, the door opened and Silverdale entered the laboratory. He seemed taken aback by the presence of the police and looked from one to another in the group without speaking. Then he came forward.

‚ÄúDo you want me?‚Äù he asked, in a colourless voice.

Markfield seemed rather ashamed at being caught there in the company of the two officials. He was about to say something when Flamborough robbed him of the opportunity.

‚ÄúI’ve come to put one or two questions, Dr. Silverdale,‚Äù the Inspector began. ‚ÄúFirst of all, have you had any dealings lately with a man named Peter Whalley?‚Äù

Silverdale was obviously taken aback.

‚ÄúWhalley?‚Äù he repeated. ‚ÄúI know nothing about anyone of that name. Who is he?‚Äù

Flamborough seemed to discount this statement, but he did not persist along that direct line.

‚ÄúCan you tell us what you were doing last night?‚Äù he demanded.

Silverdale reflected for a time before answering.

‚ÄúI left here about six o‚Äôclock‚Äîbetween six and six-thirty. Then I walked down to the Central Hotel and had dinner. I suppose I left the hotel again about a quarter to eight. I walked home, as it was a clear night; and I did some work until about half-past eleven. After that I went to bed and read for a while before going to sleep.‚Äù

Flamborough jotted something in his notebook before going further.

‚ÄúI suppose you could produce some witnesses in support of that?‚Äù he asked.

Silverdale appeared to consult his memory.

‚ÄúI met Miss Hailsham as I was leaving here,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúThat would give you the approximate time, if she remembers it. The waiter at the Central could probably satisfy you that I was there‚Äîit’s the tall one with the wart on his cheek who looks after the tables at the north window. After that, you’ll have to take my word for it.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat about your maids at Heatherfield?‚Äù

‚ÄúI haven’t anyone on the premises. No maid would take the place owing to the murder. I merely sleep there and take my meals at an hotel. A charwoman comes in during the day and cleans the place.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh,‚Äù said the Inspector, thoughtfully. ‚ÄúThen you can’t prove that you were actually at home after, say, half-past eight? By the way, you hadn’t a visitor by any chance?‚Äù

Silverdale shook his head.

‚ÄúNo, I was quite alone.‚Äù

Flamborough made another note; and then continued his interrogation.

‚ÄúI want you to cast your mind back to the night when Mrs. Silverdale came by her death. I asked you once before what you were doing that night, but you put me off. I think you’d find it more advisable to be frank, now that I’m putting the question again.‚Äù

Silverdale’s face showed some conflict of emotions, and he evidently considered the matter for almost a minute before answering.

‚ÄúI’ve nothing further to add,‚Äù he said at last.

‚ÄúI’ll put it plainly, so that there can be no mistake,‚Äù Flamborough emphasised. ‚ÄúCan you give us any account of your movements on the night that your maid was murdered at Heatherfield?‚Äù

Silverdale tightened his lips and shook his head.

‚ÄúI’ve no information to give you,‚Äù he said at length.

‚ÄúI may as well tell you, Dr. Silverdale,‚Äù said Flamborough warningly, ‚Äúthat we have a certain amount of information drawn from other sources. We may know more than you think. Wouldn’t it be best to be frank with us?‚Äù

Silverdale shook his head definitely without making any vocal reply. Flamborough concealed his disappointment, though his face grew darker. He put his hand into his waistcoat pocket and drew out something.

‚ÄúDo you recognise that, Dr. Silverdale?‚Äù

Silverdale examined it.

‚ÄúYes, that’s a cigarette-holder of mine. I recognise it by the fly in it.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen did you discover that you had lost it?‚Äù
